Nikah — Marriage

Ruling on Father Claiming Child from His Son's Slave-Girl
Commentary on the ruling of a father's claim of paternity from his son's slave-girl.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a father claims a child born from his son's slave-girl, explain the underlying proof based on guardianship, and evaluate the objections from Zufar and al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
A father's claim to his son's slave-girl's child establishes lineage and requires payment of value, not dower, due to his guardianship.

This lesson explores a nuanced area of Hanafi family law: what happens when a father has intercourse with his son's slave-girl, she bears a child, and he claims the child. The ruling balances the father's need to preserve his lineage against the son's property rights, leading to a unique financial obligation. We will unpack the core ruling, the reasoning behind it, and a key inter-madhhab disagreement.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: the child's lineage is established, the slave-girl becomes umm walad, and the father must pay her value and the child's value, but no dower (mahr) or 'uqr.
The father's payment is compensation for the slave-girl's value, not a sexual fee.
2
Identify the opposing view and its basis
Zufar and al-Shafi'i hold that a dower is obligatory because the father's ownership is only hypothetically established, so the intercourse is not on his full ownership.
This disagreement highlights the role of ownership in determining compensation.
3
Follow the Hanafi proof (dalil)
The Hanafi proof is based on the father's wilayah (guardianship) over his son's property for need, specifically to preserve his lineage. This allows the father to own the slave-girl just before conception, making his intercourse on his own property, thus no dower is due.
The key principle: al-amm yufidu al-wujub is not used here; instead, it's a specific qiyas based on need.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to the objection
Al-Nahr raised an objection involving a reclaimed slave-girl, but Sheikh Shahin answered: the case differs because reclamation due to emancipation does not affect the father's ownership at the time of the contract.
This response refines the application of the ownership principle.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Hanafi law, the father pays only the value of the slave-girl and child; under the opposing view, he would also owe a dower, increasing his financial burden.
This shows how a seemingly small doctrinal difference can have significant financial implications.

قوله : ( ومن وطى أمة ابنه فولدت فادعاه ثبت نسبه وصارت أم ولده وعليه قيمتها لا عقرها وقيمة ولدها لأن له ولاية تملك مال ابنه للحاجة إلى البقاء فله تملك جارية ابنه للحاجة إلى صيانة الماء. وحاصل وجوه مسألة جارية الابن إذا ولدت من الأب فادعاه ست وتسعون لأنه إما أن يصدقه الابن أو يكذبه أو يدعيه معه أو يسكت، وكل من الأربعة إما أن تكون قنة أو مدبرة أوأم ولد أو مكاتبة وكل من الستة عشر إما أن تكون كلها له أو بينه وبين أجنبي أو بينه وبين أبيه وكل من الثمانية والأربعين إما أن يكون الأب أهلاً للولاية أو لا غير أن الحاجة إلى إبقاء نسله دونها إلى إبقاء نفسه فلهذا يمتلك الجارية بالقيمة والطعام بغير القيمة، ثم هذا الملك يثبت قبيل الاستيلاد شرطاً له إذا المصحح حقيقة الملك أو حقه وكل ذلك غير ثابت للأب فيها حتى يجوز له التزوج بها فلا بد من تقديمه، فتبين أن الوطء يلاقي ملكه فلا يلزمه العقر وقيمة الولد . وقال زفر والشافعي يلزمه المهر لأنهما يثبتان الملك حكماً
 واعترض في النهر على ما أجاب به المؤلف فقال : وفيه بحث إذ يلزم على ما ادعاه أنه لو اشترى جارية فزوجها ودخل بها الزوج ثم استحق نصفها أن لا يقسم المهر بينهما لأنه اختلف المستحق وهو خلاف الواقع. قال محشي مسكين وأجاب الشيخ شاهين بأن مسألة الاستحقاق ورد العقد على ملكهما بخلاف هذه المسألة فإن استحقاق الجارية عارض بسبب العتق فلا تزاحم سيدها في ملكه وقت العقد فلا يقسم المهر بينهما
 وأفاد بإضافة الأمة إلى ابنه أنها مملوكة للابن من وقت العلوق إلى وقت الدعوى، فلو حبلت في غير ملكه أو فيه وأخرجها الابن . ثم استردها لم تصح الدعوة لأن الملك إنما يثبت بطريق الاستناد إلى وقت العلوق فيستدعي قيام الملك للتملك هذا إن كذبه الابن فإن صدقه صحت الدعوة ولا يملك الجارية كما إذا ادعاه أجنبي ويعتق على المولى كما في المحيط. وأفاد أيضاً أنها كلها للابن، فإن كانت مشتركة بينه وبين أجنبي كان الحكم كذلك إلا أنه يضمن لشريكه نصف عقرها ولم أره، ولو كانت مشتركة بين الأب والابن أو غيره تجب حصة الشريك الابن وغيره من العقر وقيمة باقيها إذا حبلت لعدم تقديم الملك في كلها لانتفاء موجبه وهو صيانة النسل إذ ما فيها من الملك يكفي لصحة الاستيلاد، وإذا صح ثبت الملك في باقيها حكماً له لا شرطاً. كذا في فتح القدير وهي مسألة عجيبة فإنه إذا لم يكن للواطيء فيها شيء لا مهر عليه، وإذا كانت مشتركة لزمه وأطلق الأمة وهي مقيدة بالقنة بقرينة قوله «وعليه قميتها» لأن القابل للانتقال من ملك المولى القنة فقط، فخرج عن هذا الحكم المدبرة وأم الولد والمكاتبة، فلو ادعى ولد مدبرة ابنه أو ولد أم ولده المنفي من جهة الابن أو ولد مكاتبته الذي ولدته في الكتابة أو قبلها لا تصح دعواه إلا بتصديق الابن كذا في المحيط . وقيد بابنه لأنه لو وطئ جارية امرأته أو والده أو جده فولدت وادعاه لا يثبت النسب ويدرأ عنه الحد للشبهة، فإن قال أحلها المولى لي لا يثبت النسب إلا أن يصدقه المولى في الإحلال وفي أن الولد منه، فإن صدقه في الأمرين جميعاً ثبت النسب وإلا فلا، وإن كذبه المولى ثم ملك الجارية يوماً من الدهر ثبت النسب. كذا في الخانية. وفي القنية وطيء جارية أبيه فولدت لا يجوز بيع هذا الولد، ادعى الواطيء الشبهة أو لا، لأنه ولد ولده فيعتق عليه حين دخل في ملكه، وإن لم يثبت النسب كمن زنى بجارية غيره فولدت منه ثم ملك الولد يعتق عليه وإن لم يثبت نسبه منه اهـ . وأطلق في الابن فشمل الكبير والصغير. كذا في المحيط. وقيد بالولادة لأنه لو وطيء أمة ابنه ولم تحبل فإنه يحرم عليه وإن كان لا يحد ولا يملكها ويلزمه عقرها بخلاف ما إذا
His statement: (And whoever has intercourse with his son's slave-girl and she gives birth, and he claims the child, his lineage is established and she becomes an umm walad, and upon him is her value, not her dower, and the value of her child. This is because he has guardianship to own his son's property due to need for survival, so he may own his son's slave-girl due to need to protect his lineage. And the totality of the aspects of the case of the son's slave-girl when she gives birth from the father and he claims it is ninety-six. This is because either the son confirms him, or denies him, or claims him with him, or remains silent. Each of these four is either that she is a regular slave, a mudabbara, an umm walad, or a mukataba. And each of these sixteen is either that she is entirely his, or between him and a stranger, or between him and his father. And each of these forty-eight is either that the father is qualified for guardianship or not. However, the need to preserve his lineage is more than the need to preserve himself. For this reason he owns the slave-girl by value, and food without value. Then this ownership is established just before the conception as a condition for him, since the actualizer is the reality of ownership or its right. And all of that is not established for the father concerning her such that it would be permissible for him to marry her, so it must be preceded. Thus it becomes clear that the intercourse meets his ownership, so the dower and value of the child are not obligatory upon him. And Zufar and al-Shafi'i said: The dower is obligatory upon him, because they establish ownership hypothetically. And he objected in al-Nahr to what the author answered and said: There is discussion in it, for it would follow upon his claim that if he bought a slave-girl, married her off, and the husband had intercourse, then half of her was reclaimed, that the dower would not be divided between them because the one entitled differed, and that is contrary to reality. The annotator Miskin said: And Sheikh Shahin answered that the case of reclamation is that the contract rests on the ownership of both, unlike this case, for the reclamation of the slave-girl occurs due to emancipation, so her master is not opposed in his ownership at the time of contract, so the dower is not divided between them. And he indicated by adding the slave-girl to his son that she is owned by the son from the time of conception until the time of claim. So if she conceived outside his ownership or while in it and the son took her out, then took her back, the claim is not valid because ownership is established by way of reference to the time of conception, so it requires the existence of ownership to own. This is if the son denies him. If he confirms him, the claim is valid and he does not own the slave-girl, as if a stranger claimed her, and she is emancipated upon the master as in al-Muhit. And he also indicated that she is entirely the son's. If she is shared between him and a stranger, the ruling is likewise, except that he guarantees half of her dower to his partner, and I have not seen this. And if she was shared between the father and the son or other, the share of the partner, the son and others, of the dower and the value of the remainder are obligatory if she conceived, due to the lack of precedence of ownership in all of her due to the absence of its cause, which is preservation of lineage, since what is in her of ownership suffices for the validity of becoming an umm walad. And if it is valid, ownership is established in the remainder hypothetically for him, not as a condition. Thus in Fath al-Qadir, and it is a wondrous case: for if the molester has no share in her, there is no dower upon him, and if she is shared, it is obligatory upon him. And he used the word 'slave-girl' unrestrictedly, but it is restricted to a regular slave by the indication of his statement 'upon him is her value', because only a regular slave can be transferred from the master's ownership. So excluded from this ruling are the mudabbara, umm walad, and mukataba. So if he claims the child of the son's mudabbara, or the child of his umm walad denied by the son, or the child of his mukataba born during the kitaba or before it, his claim is not valid except with the son's confirmation, as in al-Muhit. And he restricted it to 'his son' because if he had intercourse with the slave-girl of his wife, or his father, or his grandfather, and she gives birth and he claims it, the lineage is not established and the hadd penalty is repelled from him due to the doubt. If he says 'the master allowed her to me', the lineage is not established unless the master confirms him in the permission and that the child is from him. If he confirms him in both matters, the lineage is established, otherwise no. And if the master denies him and then he owns the slave-girl one day of the time, the lineage is established. Thus in al-Khaniyya. And in al-Quniyya: If one has intercourse with his father's slave-girl and she gives birth, it is not permissible to sell this child, whether the molester claims doubt or not, because he is the child of his child, so he is emancipated upon him when he enters his ownership, even if the lineage is not established, like one who commits zina with another's slave-girl and she gives birth from him, then he owns the child, it is emancipated upon him even if his lineage from him is not established. End quote. And he used the word 'son' unrestrictedly, so it includes the old and young. Thus in al-Muhit. And he restricted it to childbirth because if he had intercourse with his son's slave-girl and she did not conceive, then she becomes prohibited upon him, although he is not hadded and does not own her, and her dower is obligatory upon him, unlike the case when

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary on the ruling of Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding a father who has intercourse with his son's slave-girl, she gives birth, and he claims the child. The passage begins by stating the ruling: the child's lineage is established, the slave-girl becomes an umm walad (mother of a child by her master), and the father must pay her value and the child's value, but not the dower (mahr) or the customary compensation 'uqr. The reason given is that the father has guardianship to own his son's property for survival needs, and specifically he may own his son's slave-girl to preserve his lineage (safeguarding his procreative essence). The text then dives into the numerous possible scenarios (96 aspects) based on whether the son confirms or denies, the slave-girl's status (regular, mudabbara, umm walad, mukataba), her ownership (full or shared), and the father's eligibility for guardianship. The key legal point is that the father's ownership for the purpose of istilad (begetting a child) is established just before conception as a condition, so the intercourse occurs on his ownership, thus no 'uqr or child's value is due. Zufar and al-Shafi'i disagree, arguing that the dower is obligatory because ownership is established only hypothetically. The author addresses an objection from al-Nahr regarding a case of reclaimed slave-girl and resolves it via Sheikh Shahin. The text then clarifies conditions for the claim: the slave-girl must be the son's property from conception to claim; if the son denies, the claim fails unless he confirms; if he confirms, the claim is valid but the father does not own her. The ruling is restricted to the father's claim to his son's slave-girl, not other relatives, and only for regular slaves (qinn), not mudabbara, umm walad, or mukataba except with the son's confirmation. The passage also notes that if the intercourse does not result in pregnancy, the slave-girl becomes prohibited to the father, he is not hadded, he does not own her, and he must pay 'uqr.

Key Terms
أم ولد (umm walad): A slave-girl who bears a child from her master; she is freed upon her master's death and cannot be sold.
عقر ('uqr): Compensation for sexual intercourse with a slave-girl, typically the 'uqr is due when intercourse occurs without ownership or valid contract.
ولاية (wilayah): Guardianship or authority, here the father's legal authority over his son's property for the son's benefit and the father's need.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what must a father pay if he claims a child from his son's slave-girl?
A) The full dower (mahr) and 'uqr
B) The value of the slave-girl and the child
C) Only the value of the child
D) Nothing, because the slave-girl is already owned

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a father claiming a child from his son's slave-girl must pay the value of the slave-girl and the child, but no dower, due to his guardianship and the priority of lineage preservation.

Nikah — Marriage
advanced

Father's Claim to Son's Slave-Woman's Child
Ruling with conditions and proofs on father's paternity claim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a father's claim of paternity of his son's slave-woman's child and apply the ruling to scenarios of joint ownership and denial.

Core Idea
A father's claim requires his son's continuous ownership from conception to claim; the son's denial defeats the claim unless later affirmed.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses a specific family law scenario: when a father claims that a child born to his son's female slave is actually his own child. This ruling balances lineage (nasab) rights with ownership rights. The central condition is that the slave-woman must have been in the son's ownership from the moment of conception (علوق) until the claim. The passage then explores multiple variations: denial vs affirmation, joint ownership, and the effect on the child's freedom and compensation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the father claiming paternity of a child born to his son's slave-woman. The ruling is that the claim is conditionally valid (sahih) when all conditions are met.
The passage does not state the hukm explicitly but implies validity under conditions.
2
Determine the key condition
The slave-woman must be in the son's ownership from the time of conception (علوق) to the time of the claim. This is because ownership is established by attribution to the time of conception (الملك يثبت بطريق الاستناد إلى وقت العلوق).
If she became pregnant before the son owned her, or the son sold her then reclaimed, the claim fails.
3
Analyze the son's response
If the son denies the father's claim, the claim is invalid. If the son affirms, the claim is valid, the child is freed from the son's ownership (يعتق على المولى), and the father does not own the slave-woman.
This is analogous to a stranger's claim, which also requires the owner's affirmation.
4
Examine special cases
Joint ownership: if the slave-woman is co-owned, the father must compensate the co-owner for half the dower (عقر) and the value of the rest. For slave-women who are mudabbara, umm walad, or mukataba, the father's claim is not valid without the son's affirmation.
The general term 'slave-woman' is restricted to full slaves (qinn) because only they can transfer ownership.
5
Summarize the father's capacity
The father must be free, Muslim, sane, and not a mukatab or slave. This is wilayat al-da'wa (legal capacity to claim paternity).
If the father is a slave, mukatab, disbeliever, or insane, his claim is invalid due to lack of capacity.

And it is restricted to his son because if he had intercourse with his wife's slave-woman, or his father's, or his grandfather's, and she gave birth and he claimed it, the lineage is not established and the hadd penalty is repelled from him due to doubt. If he says 'the owner made her permissible for me', the lineage is not established unless the owner affirms him in the permissibility and that the child is from him. If he affirms him in both matters, the lineage is established; otherwise, no. And if the owner denies him then later owns the slave-woman at any time, the lineage is established, as in al-Khaniyya. And in al-Qunyah: if one had intercourse with his father's slave-woman and she gave birth, it is not permissible to sell this child, whether the fornicator claims shubha or not, because he is the child of his child, so he becomes free when he enters his ownership, even if the lineage is not established, like one who commits zina with another's slave-woman and she gives birth from him, then he owns the child, it is freed from him even if his lineage from him is not established, end. He used 'son' absolutely, including old and young, as in al-Muhit. And he restricted to birth because if he had intercourse with his son's slave-woman and she did not become pregnant, then it is forbidden for him, although he is not flogged and he does not own her, and her dower is required from him, unlike if she became pregnant from him, then it becomes clear that the intercourse was halal because his ownership preceded, and the accuser of fornication is not flogged in both issues. As for if she did not give birth from him, it is apparent because he committed forbidden intercourse without his ownership. And as for if she became pregnant from him, it is because the doubt of difference (of opinion) that ownership is established before penetration or after it repels his ihsan, as in Fath al-Qadir and others. And we have stated that if the father repeats intercourse and she does not become pregnant, one dower is required from him, unlike if the son had intercourse with his father's slave-woman repeatedly and he claimed shubha, then for each intercourse a dower is due, and the difference we have mentioned. And he indicated by his statement 'so he claimed it' that he is among those who have the capacity to claim (wilayat al-da'wa). So if the father was a slave, a mukatab, a disbeliever, or insane, his claim is not valid due to lack of capacity.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq, a commentary on Kanz al-Daqaiq, discusses the ruling of a father claiming paternity of a child born to his son's slave-woman. The author, Ibn Nujaym, explains the conditions for such a claim to be valid. The core principle is that the slave-woman must have been in the ownership of the son from the time of conception (علوق) until the time the father makes the claim (دعوى). If the son denies the father's claim, the claim fails unless the son later affirms it. The passage then addresses various scenarios: if the slave-woman was jointly owned with a stranger, the father must compensate the co-owner for half the dower (عقر). If the slave-woman is a mudabbara, umm walad, or mukataba, the claim is not valid without the son's affirmation. The father's capacity to claim (ولاية الدعوة) requires him to be free, sane, and Muslim. The text also touches on the father's liability for dower and the child's value, and distinguishes between cases where the father repeated intercourse but no pregnancy occurred (one dower) versus the son doing so with the father's slave-woman (multiple dowers). The passage uses proofs from al-Muhit, Fath al-Qadir, al-Khaniyya, and al-Qunyah, and resolves ambiguities by restricting general terms to specific contexts.

Key Terms
أمة (ama): A female slave owned by someone.
العلوق (al-uluq): The time of conception or impregnation.
العقر (al-aqr): The dower or compensation paid for intercourse with a slave-woman not owned by the man.
الاستيلاد (al-istilad): The act of begetting a child from one's own slave-woman, which makes her an umm walad.
ولاية الدعوة (wilayat al-da'wa): The legal capacity to claim paternity of a child.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a father claims paternity of a child born to his son's slave-woman, and the son denies, what is required for the claim to become valid?
A) The father must prove the child looks like him.
B) The son must later affirm the claim.
C) The father must pay the son compensation.
D) The slave-woman must be a full slave (qinn).

Key Takeaway
The father's claim to his son's slave-woman's child is valid only if the son's ownership was continuous from conception to claim and the son does not deny, or later affirms.

Father's Claim of Paternity for Child of Son's Slave Girl
Ruling with proof: conditions for father's paternity claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the father's claim of paternity for a child of his son's slave girl and identify the conditions and legal consequences of such a claim.

Core Idea
The father's claim establishes paternity if the slave girl was in his ownership from conception to claim.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, the commentator expands on the ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding the father's authority to claim paternity of a child born to his son's slave girl. This issue involves complex interactions of ownership, paternity, and the effects of legal doubt (shubha). We will analyze the conditions, the permissibility of intercourse, and the consequences for inheritance and criminal penalties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what legal act is being discussed: the father's claim of paternity for a child born to his son's slave girl. The ruling is that the claim is valid (sahih) under certain conditions.
The text uses 'yashuhhu' (it is valid) from the base text.
2
List the conditions for validity
Extract from the text the conditions required for the father's claim to be valid: the father must be free, Muslim, and sane; the slave girl must be in his ownership from the time of conception until the time of the claim.
The exception of the insane person who recovers is by istihsan.
3
Understand the legal effects
If the claim is valid, the intercourse is considered halal due to prior ownership, the child's lineage is established, and the accuser of fornication is not flogged (hadd is removed due to shubha).
Shubha of disagreement over ownership timing removes the hadd.
4
Identify exceptions and special cases
The text discusses cases where the claim is not valid (e.g., father is slave, kafir, insane at claim time; slave girl not continuously owned). Also if the claim is made before birth, it is not valid until after birth.
The text also covers shared ownership scenarios.
5
Apply the analysis to a scenario
Given a scenario, check each condition and determine if the father's claim is valid. Consider the effect of istihsan in edge cases.
E.g., father insane at intercourse but recovered before birth.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling from the Hanafi school regarding the father's claim of paternity for a child born to a slave girl owned by his son. The text clarifies that if the father has intercourse with his son's slave girl and she becomes pregnant, the intercourse is considered permissible due to his prior ownership (since only he can own her after the son? Actually, the text explains that the father's ownership is established in some sense, but the main point is the claim of lineage. The ruling discusses the father's authority to claim the child, the conditions for the claim, and the consequences. The passage is from the commentary of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, expanding on the base text. The author presents various scenarios: if the slave girl becomes pregnant, it is determined that the intercourse was lawful because the father's ownership preceded the intercourse. In both cases (whether she gives birth or not), the one who accuses the father of fornication is not flogged, due to the doubt (shubha) about the ownership. The text also distinguishes between the case where she does not give birth (the intercourse was clearly unlawful) and where she does give birth (the doubt removes the hadd). The authority weight is fatawa mutakhkhirin, as this is a later commentary. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that the father's claim is valid under certain conditions, primarily that the slave girl was in his ownership from the time of conception to the time of the claim. The son's denial does not prevent the claim's validity unless he denies the ownership. The key terms include 'wilayat al-da'wa' (authority to make the claim) and 'shubhat al-khilaf' (doubt due to legal disagreement).

Key Terms
ولاية الدعوة (wilāyat al-da‘wah): The legal authority to make a claim of paternity, which is possessed by the father as long as he is free, Muslim, and sane.
شبهة الخلاف (shubhat al-khilāf): A legal doubt arising from disagreement among scholars, here regarding whether ownership is established before or after intercourse.

The Grandfather's Paternity Claim Conditions
Explaining conditions for grandfather's paternity claim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a valid grandfather's paternity claim in Hanafi law and identify which relatives are excluded.

Core Idea
The grandfather's paternity claim is valid only when the father lacks legal capacity, not merely physical absence.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from the Hanafi school on paternity claims. The grandfather can stand in the father's place to claim a child, but only under strict conditions regarding the father's legal capacity and the timing of the child's birth. Understanding these conditions is essential for correctly applying lineage rules in cases where the father is absent.

Guided Path
1
Understand the meaning of 'absence of the father'
The text clarifies that 'absence' means absence of legal capacity (wilaya) due to death, apostasy, slavery, or insanity. Physical absence alone is insufficient.
2
Identify the required timing of the father's absence
The father must lack legal capacity both at the time of conception and at the time of the claim. This ensures the grandfather's guardianship covers the entire period of lineage.
3
Apply the six-month rule
If the child is born less than six months after the grandfather's wilaya begins, the claim is invalid. This is based on the minimum period of pregnancy.
This mirrors the same rule applied to the father's claim.
4
Determine which grandfather is meant
The ruling applies only to the paternal grandfather (abu al-ab), not the maternal grandfather or other mahram relatives, because they lack wilaya over the child's lineage.
Other relatives are not accepted in paternity claims at all.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ودعوة الجد كدعوة الأب حال عدمه أي عدم الأب لقيامه مقامه، والمراد بعدمه عدم ولايته بالموت أو الكفر أو الرق أو الجنون لا عدم وجوده فقط، وليس مراده بحال العدم أن يكون الأب معدوماً وقت الدعوة فقط لأنه يشترط أن يكون معدوماً وقت العلوق أيضاً، فيحنئذ يشترط أن يثبت ولايته من وقت العلوق إلى وقت الدعوة حتى لو أتت بالولد لأقل من ستة أشهر من وقت انتقال الولاية إليه لم تصح دعوته لما ذكرنا في الأب، ولما شرط المصنف الأب لولاية دعوة الجد علم أن ولاية الحد منتقلة الأب إليه فأفاد أنه من أبو الأب، وأما الجد أبو الأم وغيره من ذوي الرحم المحرم فلا يصدق في جميع الأحوال لفقد ولايتهم. كذا في المحيط
His saying: 'And the claim of the grandfather is like the claim of the father in his absence' means the absence of the father because he stands in his place. What is meant by his absence is the absence of his legal capacity due to death, apostasy, slavery, or insanity, not merely his physical non-existence. He does not mean by 'in his absence' that the father is non-existent only at the time of the claim, because it is also required that he be non-existent at the time of conception. Therefore, it is required that his legal capacity be established from the time of conception until the time of the claim. So if she gives birth to the child less than six months from the time the legal capacity was transferred to him, his claim is not valid, as we mentioned regarding the father. And since the author stipulated the father for the grandfather's authority to claim, it is known that the authority of the child is transferred from the father to him, so it benefits that he is the paternal grandfather. As for the maternal grandfather and others from the prohibited relatives (mahram), they are not accepted in all cases due to their lack of authority. Thus in al-Muhit.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling on the grandfather's paternity claim (da'wa al-jadd). The grandfather is treated like the father in claiming paternity, but only when the father is legally absent—meaning his guardianship (wilaya) is non-existent due to death, apostasy, slavery, or insanity. The father's physical absence alone is insufficient; he must lack capacity both at the time of conception and at the time of the claim. If the child is born less than six months after the grandfather gains guardianship, his claim is invalid. The text clarifies that the grandfather referred to here is the paternal grandfather (abu al-ab), not the maternal grandfather or other relatives, who lack legal authority for such claims. This is a ruling from the Hanafi madhhab, as recorded in al-Muhit.

Key Terms
دعوة الجد (da'wa al-jadd): A paternity claim made by the grandfather, which is treated like the father's claim under specific conditions.
ولاية (wilaya): Legal authority or guardianship, here referring to the father's capacity to claim paternity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The father is unknown.

Key Takeaway
A grandfather's paternity claim is valid only when the father lacks legal capacity at both conception and claim, the child is born at least six months after the grandfather's wilaya begins, and the grandfather is paternal.

Marriage of Slave-Girl to Her Owner's Father
Ruling on marriage between a slave-girl and her owner's father

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the marriage of a slave-girl to her owner's father, explain why the child is free, and contrast the Hanafi position with that of al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
A marriage between a slave-girl and her owner's father is valid, but her child is free and she does not become umm walad.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a specific contractual scenario: a son marries his own slave-girl to his father. The Hanafi school maintains its validity, in opposition to al-Shafi'i, based on the principle that the father has no ownership or right in the slave-girl. The consequences regarding umm walad status, dower, emancipation of the child, and the intra-madhhab dispute on timing of emancipation are all explored.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central issue and Hanafi position
What is the core legal question? What does the Hanafi school rule?
The issue is whether the marriage is valid and what the consequences are for the child and mother.
2
Examine the Hanafi proof (dalil)
What is the reasoning? Why does the father's lack of ownership validate the marriage?
The key point: 'خلوها عن ملك الأب' (her being free from the father's ownership).
3
Understand the opposing view (al-Shafi'i)
Who opposes and on what basis? What is their likely proof?
Al-Shafi'i is mentioned as the opponent; the text suggests he considers the marriage invalid due to potential ownership conflict.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
How does the Hanafi school rebut the opposition? What is the decisive argument?
The son's complete ownership negates any partial ownership by the father; thus no conflict.
5
Determine the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
What changes in real application between the two schools? How does the ruling affect inheritance, emancipation, and dower?
According to Hanafis, the child is free and inherits; according to al-Shafi'i, the marriage is invalid and the child would have a different status.

قوله : (ولو زوجها أباه فولدت لم تصر أم ولد له ويجب المهر لا القيمة وولدها (حر) لأنه يصح التزوج عندنا خلافاً للشافعي لخلوها عن ملك الأب، ألا ترى أن الابن ملكها من كل وجه فمن المحال أن يملكها الأب من وجه وكذلك يملك الابن من التصرفات ما لا يبقى معها ملك الأب لو كان فدل ذلك على انتفاء ملكه إلا أنه يسقط الخد للشبهة، فإذا أجاز النكاح صار ماؤه مصوناً به فلم يثبت ملك اليمين فلا تصير أم ولد له ولا قيمة عليه فيها ولا في ولدها لأنه لم يملكها وعليه المهر لالتزامه بالنكاح، والولد حر ملك أخاه فعتق عليه بالقرابة كذا في الهداية وظاهره أن الولد علق رقيقاً واختلف فيه؛ فقيل يعتق قبل الإنفصال وقيل يعتق بعد الإنفصال وثمرته تظهر في الإرث حتى لو مات المولى وهو الابن يرثه الولد على الأول دون الثاني والوجه هو الأول لأن الولد حدث على ملك الأخ من حين العلوق فلما ملكه عتق عليه بالقرابة بالحديث. كذا في غاية البيان. والظاهر عندي هو الثاني لأنه لا ملك له من كل وجه قبل الوضع لقولهم الملك على التصرفات في الشيء ،ابتداء ولا شك أنه لا قدرة للسيد على التصرف في الجنين قبل وضعه ببيع أو هبة وإن صح الإيصاء به واعتاقه فلم يتناوله الحديث لأنه في المملوك من كل وجه، ولذا قالوا: لو قال كل مملوك أملكه فهو حر لا يتناول الحمل لأنه ليس بمملوك من كل وجه . فلو قال المصنف ولو تزوجها أبوه بدل قوله ولو زوجها أباه لكان أولى لشموله ما إذا كانت الجارية لولده الصغير فتزوجها الأب فإنه صحيح ولا تصير أم ولد له. قال قاضيخان في فتاواه : إذا تزوج الرجل جارية ولده الصغير فولدت منه لا تصير أم ولد له ويعتق الولد بالقرابة، وإذا أراد الرجل أن يطأ جاريته لا تصير أم ولد منه لو ولدت فإنه يبيعها من ولده الصغير ثم يتزوجها .اهـ. أطلق في التزوج فشمل الصحيح والفاسد كما به في التبيين لأن الفاسد منه يثبت فيه النسب فاستغنى عن تقدم الملك له. وفي النهاية : حتى [؟] هو القدرة صرح [؟] تلد) قال في النهر : ينبغي أنها لو ولدته لأقل من ستة أشهر من وقت دعوته أن تصح قوله (والظاهر عندي هو الثاني نقله في النهر والرمز وأقراه عليه .
His saying: 'And if he marries her to his father and she gives birth, she does not become an umm walad for him, and the dower is obligatory, not the value, and her child is free.' Because the marriage is valid according to us, contrary to al-Shafi'i, due to her being free from the father's ownership. Do you not see that the son owns her completely, so it is impossible for the father to own her in any respect? Likewise, the son possesses powers of disposition that would not remain with any ownership of the father, so that indicates the negation of his ownership, except that the blood (mahr) is waived due to the doubt. So when the marriage is made, his seminal fluid becomes protected by it, so possession by right of ownership (yamin) is not established, so she does not become an umm walad for him, nor is he liable for her value or her child's value because he did not own her; and the dower is upon him due to his undertaking in the marriage. The child is free; he owned his brother, so he is emancipated by kinship. Thus in al-Hidaya, and apparently the child was conceived as a slave, and there is disagreement: some say he is emancipated before separation, some say after separation. Its fruit appears in inheritance: if the master (the son) dies, the child inherits from him according to the first view, not the second. The correct view is the first because the child came into being under the brother's ownership from the time of conception, so when he owned him, he emancipated him by kinship according to the hadith. Thus in Ghayat al-Bayan. The apparent to me is the second because he does not have complete ownership before birth, as they say: ownership is over dispositions in the thing initially, and there is no doubt that the master cannot dispose of the fetus before birth by sale or gift, although bequest and manumission are valid concerning it. So the hadith does not apply to it because it applies to a slave owned completely. Therefore they said: if one says 'every slave I own is free,' it does not include the pregnancy because it is not owned completely. So if the author had said 'if his father marries her' instead of 'if he marries her to his father,' it would be better to include the case where the slave-girl belongs to his minor son and the father marries her, for it is valid and she does not become an umm walad for him. Qadi Khan said in his Fatawa: If a man marries the slave-girl of his minor son and she gives birth from him, she does not become an umm walad for him, and the child is emancipated by kinship. If a man wants to have intercourse with his own slave-girl and not make her an umm walad if she gives birth, he sells her to his minor son then marries her. He used the general term 'marriage' including valid and invalid, as in al-Tabyin, because invalid marriage also establishes lineage, so prior ownership is no longer needed. And in al-Nihaya: ... [unclear] ... he said in al-Nahr: it is appropriate that if she gives birth to him less than six months from the time of his claim, that his saying (the apparent to me is the second) is valid, as quoted in al-Nahr and al-Ramz and they agreed with him.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling when a man (the son) marries his own slave-girl to his father. According to the Hanafi school, this marriage is valid, contrary to al-Shafi'i, because the father has no ownership over the slave-girl; she is completely owned by the son. Since the father does not own her, she does not become an umm walad (mother of a child) for the father upon giving birth, nor is he liable for her value or the child's value. However, the dower (mahr) is obligatory because he undertook it in the marriage. The child is free because he is related to the son as a brother, and thus the son's ownership of the child leads to emancipation by kinship (qaraba). There is a disagreement on whether the child is emancipated before or after birth; the author prefers before birth but some argue for after. The passage also includes a fatwa from Qadi Khan and a note from al-Nahr regarding the timing of the birth relative to the claim. The overall ruling is that the marriage is valid but with these specific consequences.

Key Terms
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who has given birth to a child of her owner; she becomes free upon the owner's death.
عتق بالقرابة (itq bi al-qaraba): Emancipation due to kinship; if a person owns a close relative, the relative is automatically freed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The child is a slave of the father.
B) The child is free and inherits from the son (owner).
C) The child is a slave of the son.
D) The child is free but does not inherit.

Key Takeaway
The marriage of a slave-girl to her owner's father is valid in Hanafi fiqh; the child is free by kinship and the mother does not become umm walad.

Nikah — Marriage

Iqtida' and Marriage Invalidity: Free Woman Frees Her Husband
Ruling on marriage invalidity via implied sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of iqtida' to explain why the marriage becomes invalid when a free woman purchases and frees her slave husband, and distinguish the positions of Zufar and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
Manumission by a wife on her husband implies a sale; ownership of spouse invalidates marriage.

When a free woman asks her husband's master to free him for a price, and the master complies, what happens to the marriage? The Hanafi school rules that the marriage becomes invalid (fasid) and the dower drops. This conclusion hinges on the legal device of iqtida' (implication), where the request is interpreted as a request to first purchase the slave, then free him. Zufar and Abu Yusuf raise different objections, giving us a rich case study in legal interpretation and the limits of contractual implication.

Guided Path
1
Identify the fact pattern and the central ruling
A free woman tells her husband's master: 'Free him on my behalf for a thousand dirhams.' The master does so. State the Hanafi ruling: the marriage becomes invalid (fasid) and the dower is dropped.
The husband is a slave, so the wife is dealing with his master.
2
Trace the reasoning using iqtida'
Understand that the wife's statement 'free him on my behalf' is impossible to execute literally because she does not own him. Therefore, for her speech to be true/valid, we must imply a prior step: she first acquires ownership of him (through a sale) and then frees him. This implied sale is the iqtida'.
The Hanafi principle: al-iqtida' min dalalat al-lafz 'ala maskut yatawaqqaf 'alayhi sidquhu aw sihhatahu.
3
Connect ownership to marriage invalidity
Once the wife becomes the owner of her husband, there is a conflict: a person cannot own his or her spouse (the two ownerships are incompatible). Hence the marriage becomes invalid (fasid).
Compare with the case of a man who owns his wife as a slave: the marriage is automatically dissolved.
4
Examine the dispute with Zufar
Zufar argues that since the wife cannot own the slave, the request is invalid, so the manumission is on the master's own behalf. Thus the marriage remains valid. Note the Hanafi response: the request is reinterpreted via iqtida' as a two-step process.
This is a genuine ikhtilaf (legitimate difference).
5
Analyze the intra-Hanafi difference on the case without payment
If the wife said only 'free him on my behalf' without a price, Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that the marriage remains valid and the wala' goes to the master. Abu Yusuf analogizes to the case with payment, arguing that a transfer of ownership (gift) is implied. Understand the reason: hiba requires possession, which cannot be implied.
This is a dispute within the Hanafi school itself (intra-madhhab ikhtilaf).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (حرة قالت لسيد زوجها أعتقه عني بألف ففعل فسد النكاح) وقال زفر : لا يفسد وأصله أنه يقع العتق عن الآمر عندنا حتى يكون الولاء له ولو نوى به الكفارة يخرج عن العهدة وعنده يقع عن المأمور لأنه طلب أن يعتق المأمور عبده عنه وهذا محال لأنه لا عتق فيما لا يملك ابن آدم فلم يصح الطلب فيقع العتق عن المأمور. ولنا أنه أمكن تصحيحه بتقديم الملك بطريق الاقتضاء إذ الملك شرط لصحة العتق عنه فيصير قوله «أعتق» طلب التمليك منه بالألف ثم أمره بإعتاق عبد الآمر عنه . وقوله أعتقت تمليك منه ثم إعتاق عنه، وإذا ثبت الملك للآمر فسد النكاح للتنافي بين الملكين. فالحاصل أن هذا باب الاقتضاء من دلالة اللفظ على مسكوت يتوقف صدقه عليه أو صحته فالمقتضي - بالفتح - ما استدعاه صدق الكلام كرفع الخطأ والنسيان أو حكم لزمه شرعاً كمسألة الكتاب فالملك فيه شرط وهو تبع للمقتضى وهو العتق إذ الشروط اتباع فلذا ثبت البيع المقتضى - بالفتح - بشروط المقتضى وهو العتق لا بشروط نفسه إظهاراً للتبعية فسقط القبول الذي هو ركن البيع، ولا يثبت فيه خيار الرؤية والعيب ولا يشترط كونه مقدور التسليم حتى صح الأمر بإعتاق الآبق. ولو بألف ورطل من خمر فأعتقه وقع عن الآمر وسقط اعتبار القبض في الفاسد لأنه ملحق بالصحيح في احتمال سقوط القبض هنا، ويعتبر في الأمر أهلية الإعتاق حتى لو كان صبياً مأذوناً لم يثبت البيع بهذا الكلام لكونه ليس بأهل للإعتاق. وأشار بفساد النكاح إلى سقوط المهر لاستحالة وجوبه على عبدها، وإلى أنه لو قال رجل تحته أمة لمولاها أعتقها عني بألف ففعل عتقت الأمة وفسد النكاح للتنافي أيضاً لكن لا يسقط المهر. وقيد بكون المأمور فعل ما أمر به لأنه لو زاد عليه بأن قال بعتك بألف ثم أعتقت لم يصر مجيباً لكلامه بل كان مبتدأ وقع العتق عن نفسه كما في غاية البيان، يعني فلا يفسد النكاح في مسألة الكتاب قوله : (ولو لم تقل بألف لا يفسد النكاح والولاء له أي للمأمور وهذا عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد، وقال أبو يوسف هذا والأول سواء لأنه يقدم التمليك بغير عوض تصحيحاً لتصرفه، ويسقط اعتبار القبض كما إذا كان عليه كفارة ظهار فأمر غيره أن يطعم عنه. ولهما أن الهبة من شروطها القبض بالنص ولا يمكن إسقاطه ولا إثباته اقتضاء لأنه فعل حسي بخلاف البيع لأنه تصرف شرعي وفي تلك المسألة الفقير ينوب عن الآمر في القبض، أما العبد فلا يقع في قال أعتقه عني
His saying: (A free woman said to the master of her husband: 'Free him on my behalf for a thousand,' and he did it, so the marriage becomes invalid.) Zufar said: It does not become invalid. The basis is that the manumission occurs on behalf of the one who gave the order (the wife) according to us, so the wala' belongs to her, and if he intended it for expiation, it fulfills the obligation. According to him (Zufar), it occurs on behalf of the one who is commanded (the master) because he requested that the master free his slave on his behalf, but this is impossible because there is no freeing of what a person does not own, so the request is invalid, so the manumission occurs on behalf of the master. Our argument is that it is possible to correct it by first transferring ownership via the mode of implication (iqtida'), because ownership is a condition for the validity of freeing on his behalf. Thus his saying 'free' becomes a request to transfer ownership from him for a thousand, and then an order to free the slave of the one who gave the order (the wife). His saying 'I freed' is a transfer of ownership from him and then a freeing on his behalf. When ownership is established for the one who gave the order, the marriage becomes invalid due to conflict between two ownerships. In summary, this is the chapter of iqtida', from the indication of the wording to what is unspoken that its truth or validity depends on. So al-muqtaqi (what necessitates?) is what the truth of the speech requires, like removing error and forgetfulness, or a ruling that follows by law like the issue of the book. Ownership here is a condition and it is subsidiary to the muqtaqa (the necessitated), which is manumission, because conditions follow. Therefore the sale necessitated (al-bay' al-muqtaqa) is established under the conditions of the muqtaqa (manumission), not under its own conditions, to show subservience. So the acceptance, which is a pillar of sale, is dropped. Options of inspection and defect are not established, and it is not required that it be deliverable, so it is valid to order the freeing of an absconding slave. Even if it is with a thousand and a pound of wine, and he frees him, it occurs on the orderer's behalf, and the requirement of possession is dropped in case of invalidity because it is treated like valid in the possibility of dropping possession here. Eligibility for freeing is considered in the order; if the orderer is a permitted minor, the sale is not established by this speech because he is not eligible for freeing. He indicated that the invalidity of marriage implies dropping of dower because it is impossible for it to be obligatory on her slave, and that if a man has a slave-girl under him and says to her master: 'Free her on my behalf for a thousand,' and he does it, the slave-girl becomes free and the marriage becomes invalid due to conflict as well, but the dower is not dropped. It is conditioned that the one commanded does what he was ordered; if he adds to it, such as saying 'I sold to you for a thousand and then freed,' he does not become a respondent to his speech but rather an initiator, and the manumission occurs on his own behalf, as in Ghayat al-Bayan. So the marriage does not become invalid in the issue of the book. His saying: 'And if she did not say 'for a thousand,' the marriage does not become invalid and the wala' is for him (the master).' This is according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Abu Yusuf said this and the first are the same, because he effects transfer of ownership without compensation to validate his action, and possession is not required, as in the case where he owes the expiation of zihar and orders another to feed on his behalf. Their argument is that for a gift (hiba), possession is required by text, and it cannot be dropped nor established by iqtida' because it is a physical action, unlike sale which is a legal act. In that issue, the poor person acts as deputy for the orderer in possession, but the slave does not fall [under this]? He said 'free him on my behalf.'
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage deals with the case where a free woman says to the master of her husband (who is a slave): 'Free him on my behalf for a thousand dirhams,' and the master does so. According to the Hanafi school, the marriage becomes invalid (fasid) because the wife acquires ownership of her husband, which is incompatible with marriage. Zufar disagreed, arguing that the manumission cannot be on behalf of the wife since she does not own the slave, so it is on behalf of the master. The Hanafis respond using the principle of iqtida' (implication): the wife's request 'free him on my behalf' implies a request to first transfer ownership to her (sale) and then free him. Thus, the husband becomes owned by the wife, causing a conflict with marriage (a person cannot own his spouse). The dower is dropped. If she did not mention a payment (just said 'free him on my behalf'), then according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, the marriage remains valid and the wala' (clientage) goes to the master. Abu Yusuf disagreed, analogizing it to the case with payment. The passage also discusses conditions for the validity of such transactions, including eligibility of the orderer and the precise execution of the command.

Key Terms
اقتضاء (iqtida'): A legal implication where the wording necessitates something not explicitly stated for the utterance to be valid or truthful.
ولاء (wala'): Clientage; the bond of loyalty between a freed slave and the emancipator, which carries inheritance rights.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what happens to the marriage when a free woman tells her husband's master to free him for a price and the master does so?
A) The marriage remains valid and the husband is free.
B) The marriage becomes invalid because the wife now owns her husband.
C) The marriage remains valid but the husband is still a slave.
D) The marriage becomes invalid but the dower is still due.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle of iqtida' transforms a seemingly impossible request (free someone you don't own) into a two-step legal transaction, yielding the ruling that the marriage is invalidated due to ownership conflict.

Invalidation of Marriage When Wife Pays for Husband's Freedom
Detailed ruling with intra-madhhab dispute on a wife freeing her slave-husband

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the marriage becomes fasid when a wife pays to free her slave-husband and apply the principle of iqtida' to similar cases.

Core Idea
A wife cannot own her husband – iqtida' implies a sale, invalidating the marriage.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq presents a case from Kanz al-Daqaiq: a free woman tells her slave-husband's master, 'Free him for me for a thousand.' The master does so, and the marriage becomes invalid. Zufar disagrees, arguing the emancipation is on the master's behalf. The Hanafi school defends the ruling using the principle of iqtida' (legal implication). This lesson unpacks the reasoning and the legal fiction that preserves the validity of the woman's request while leading to the intended outcome.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case and ruling
Read the opening statement: a free woman asks the master of her slave-husband to free him for a price. The master does so. The ruling is that the marriage becomes invalid (fasid).
2
Understand Zufar's objection
Zufar argues that the request is impossible (one cannot free another's slave), so the emancipation is on the master's behalf. Thus the marriage remains valid.
3
Learn the Hanafi response via iqtida'
The Hanafi school uses the principle of iqtida' (implication) to correct the request. The speech implies a prior sale: the master sells the slave to the woman for a thousand, then frees him on her behalf. Ownership transfers to her, invalidating the marriage due to conflict of ownership.
This is a legal fiction that maintains the validity of the woman's request.
4
Analyze implications for the marriage and dower
When the wife owns her husband, the marriage becomes fasid. The dower falls because the wife cannot owe mahr to her own slave. In the analogous case of a husband freeing his slave-wife, the dower does not fall because the husband is not owned.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (حرة قالت لسيد زوجها أعتقه عني بألف ففعل فسد النكاح) وقال زفر : لا يفسد وأصله أنه يقع العتق عن الآمر عندنا حتى يكون الولاء له ولو نوى به الكفارة يخرج عن العهدة وعنده يقع عن المأمور لأنه طلب أن يعتق المأمور عبده عنه وهذا محال لأنه لا عتق فيما لا يملك ابن آدم فلم يصح الطلب فيقع العتق عن المأمور. ولنا أنه أمكن تصحيحه بتقديم الملك بطريق الاقتضاء إذ الملك شرط لصحة العتق عنه فيصير قوله «أعتق» طلب التمليك منه بالألف ثم أمره بإعتاق عبد الآمر عنه . وقوله أعتقت تمليك منه ثم إعتاق عنه، وإذا ثبت الملك للآمر فسد النكاح للتنافي بين الملكين. فالحاصل أن هذا باب الاقتضاء من دلالة اللفظ على مسكوت يتوقف صدقه عليه أو صحته فالمقتضي - بالفتح - ما استدعاه صدق الكلام كرفع الخطأ والنسيان أو حكم لزمه شرعاً كمسألة الكتاب فالملك فيه شرط وهو تبع للمقتضى وهو العتق إذ الشروط اتباع فلذا ثبت البيع المقتضى - بالفتح - بشروط المقتضى وهو العتق لا بشروط نفسه إظهاراً للتبعية فسقط القبول الذي هو ركن البيع، ولا يثبت فيه خيار الرؤية والعيب ولا يشترط كونه مقدور التسليم حتى صح الأمر بإعتاق الآبق. ولو بألف ورطل من خمر فأعتقه وقع عن الآمر وسقط اعتبار القبض في الفاسد لأنه ملحق بالصحيح في احتمال سقوط القبض هنا، ويعتبر في الأمر أهلية الإعتاق حتى لو كان صبياً مأذوناً لم يثبت البيع بهذا الكلام لكونه ليس بأهل للإعتاق. وأشار بفساد النكاح إلى سقوط المهر لاستحالة وجوبه على عبدها، وإلى أنه لو قال رجل تحته أمة لمولاها أعتقها عني بألف ففعل عتقت الأمة وفسد النكاح للتنافي أيضاً لكن لا يسقط المهر. وقيد بكون المأمور فعل ما أمر به لأنه لو زاد عليه بأن قال بعتك بألف ثم أعتقت لم يصر مجيباً لكلامه بل كان مبتدأ ووقع العتق عن نفسه كما في غاية البيان، يعني فلا يفسد النكاح في مسألة الكتاب
His saying: (A free woman said to the master of her husband: 'Free him for me for a thousand' and he did, so the marriage becomes invalid.) Zufar said: It does not become invalid. Its basis is that the emancipation occurs on behalf of the commander according to us, so that the clientage (wala) belongs to him, and if he intended it for expiation, he is discharged. According to him (Zufar), it occurs on behalf of the commanded, because he requested that the commanded free his slave for him, which is impossible because there is no emancipation of what one does not own, so the request is invalid, so the emancipation occurs on behalf of the commanded. Our proof: It is possible to correct it by preceding ownership through the principle of implication (iqtida'), since ownership is a condition for the validity of emancipation on his behalf. So his saying 'Free' becomes a request for transfer of ownership from him for a thousand, then a command to free the slave of the commander on his behalf. His saying 'I freed' is a transfer of ownership from him, then emancipation on his behalf. When ownership is established for the commander, the marriage becomes invalid due to the conflict between the two ownerships. In summary, this is the chapter of implication: the implication of a word indicates something unspoken that its truth or validity depends on. The 'muqtada' (what is implied) is what the truthfulness of the speech requires, like removing error and forgetfulness, or a legal ruling that follows from it like the case of the book. Ownership here is a condition and is subordinate to the muqtada, which is emancipation, because conditions follow. Therefore the implied sale is established by the conditions of the muqtada (emancipation) not by its own conditions, to show subordination. So acceptance (qabul), which is a pillar of sale, is dropped. Option of inspection (khiyar al-ru'ya) and option of defect are not established in it, and it is not required that it be capable of delivery, so the command to free an absconding slave is valid. If it is for a thousand and a ratl of wine, and he freed him, it occurs on behalf of the commander, and the requirement of possession (qabd) is dropped in the invalid (fasid) because it is attached to the valid in the possibility of dropping possession here. Capacity for emancipation is considered in the commander; so if he is a minor permitted to trade, the sale is not established by this speech because he is not qualified for emancipation. He indicated by the invalidation of marriage the fall of the dower (mahr) due to the impossibility of its obligation on her slave. And that if a man who has a slave-wife under him says to her master: 'Free her for me for a thousand' and he does so, the slave-wife is freed and the marriage becomes invalid due to conflict as well, but the dower does not fall. It is conditioned that the commanded does what he was commanded; because if he added to it by saying 'I sold you for a thousand and then freed,' he does not become a respondent to his speech, but rather initiated, and the emancipation occurs on his own behalf as in Ghayat al-Bayan, meaning that the marriage does not become invalid in the case of the book.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a ruling from the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq. It presents a case: a free woman tells the master of her slave-husband, 'Free him for me for a thousand dirhams.' When the master does so, the marriage becomes invalid (fasid). Zufar (a Hanafi scholar) disagrees, arguing that the emancipation occurs on behalf of the master himself, not the woman, because the request to free another's slave is impossible. The Hanafi school counters using the principle of 'iqtida' (implication). The woman's speech implies a request to first transfer ownership of the slave to her (through a sale for a thousand), and then to free him on her behalf. Since ownership is a condition for valid emancipation on someone's behalf, the speech is interpreted as a sale and then emancipation. When ownership of the slave passes to the woman, she now owns her husband, which is incompatible with marriage (a free woman cannot be married to her own slave). Thus the marriage is invalidated. The commentary elaborates on the legal fiction of implication: the muqtada (implied action) is the sale, but its conditions (like acceptance, option of inspection) are dropped because they are secondary to the primary act of emancipation. This is a unique feature of Hanafi legal reasoning, allowing flexibility. The passage also notes that the dower (mahr) falls in this case because the wife cannot owe mahr to her own slave, but in the analogous case where a husband frees his slave-wife, the mahr does not fall because the husband is not her slave. Additionally, if the commanded person does exactly what was commanded, the ruling applies; if he modifies it, the emancipation is on his own behalf.

Key Terms
الاقتضاء (al-iqtida'): A legal principle where the speech implies a necessary precondition for its validity, such as implying a sale before emancipation.
المقتضي (al-muqtadi): What is implied by the speech, i.e., the sale that is necessary for the validity of the emancipation request.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the marriage become invalid (fasid) when a wife pays to free her slave-husband?
A) Because the husband becomes free, ending the marriage.
B) Because the wife becomes the owner of her husband, which is incompatible with marriage.
C) Because the master loses ownership, so the marriage contract is void.
D) Because the dower becomes impossible to pay.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle of iqtida' allows correcting an impossible request by implying a preceding necessary action, here a sale, leading to the marriage's invalidity due to conflict of ownership.

Wife Frees Husband Without Price: Marriage Remains Valid
Intra-Hanafi ruling and talil on marriage validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold the marriage valid when a wife frees her husband without mentioning a price, and evaluate Abu Yusuf's contrary argument.

Core Idea
Without a price, freeing is a gift (hiba) requiring possession; no possession means no ownership transfer, so marriage remains valid.

When a wife tells her husband 'You are free' without setting a price, is the marriage invalidated? The Hanafi school has an internal disagreement. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say no – it remains a valid marriage. Abu Yusuf argues it should be treated like the case where a price is mentioned. Let's trace the reasoning behind each position, focusing on the nature of a gift (hiba) and the role of physical possession (qabd).

Guided Path
1
State the Ruling
Identify the main ruling: If the wife says 'Free him' without specifying a price, the marriage is not invalidated. Note that this is the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
The wala (clientage) goes to the master who performs the emancipation, i.e., the commander.
2
Understand Abu Yusuf's Dissent
Abu Yusuf equates this case with the previous one where a price was mentioned. He argues that the master intends to grant ownership without compensation, so the requirement of possession is dropped by analogy with kaffarah (expiation) where one commands another to feed.
In his view, the marriage becomes invalid like in the price case.
3
Analyze the Majority's Proof
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad distinguish: A gift (hiba) requires physical possession (qabd) by textual evidence. Possession cannot be implied by speech alone; it is a physical act. Sale does not require qabd because it is a legal transaction. Without a price, the wife does not take possession, so no ownership transfers, hence no conflict of ownership and the marriage remains valid.
The distinction between physical acts (qabd) and legal transactions is key.
4
Distinguish the Kaffarah Analogy
In kaffarah, the poor person can take possession on behalf of the commander. Here, the slave (husband) does not receive anything in his hand to act as deputy. The analogy fails because the slave cannot deputize for the wife in possession.
Thus, the condition of qabd cannot be dropped by implication.
5
Note the Practical Outcome (Thamarah)
The practical difference: Under Abu Yusuf, the marriage would be invalid and the wala likely goes to the wife? (not specified). Under the majority, the marriage remains valid and wala stays with the master.
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.

Source Text

His saying: (And if she did not say 'for a thousand', the marriage does not become invalid and the clientage (wala) is for him, i.e., for the commanded one. This is according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Abu Yusuf said: This and the first case are equal, because he grants ownership without compensation to validate his action, and the requirement of possession (qabd) is dropped, as in the case where a man has the expiation of zihar and commands another to feed on his behalf. Their (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad's) argument: For a gift (hiba), possession is a condition by text (nass), and it cannot be dropped nor established by implication because it is a physical action, unlike sale because it is a legal transaction. In that case (kaffarah), the poor person acts as deputy for the commander in possession, but the slave does not receive anything in his hand to act as deputy. So the conclusion: a physical act of taking cannot be implied by speech and exist by its existence, unlike speech which can be implied within another speech and its intent considered. That is clear. Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute. The matn ruling states: If the wife says 'Free him' without mentioning a price, the marriage is not invalidated, and the wala (clientage) goes to the master who performed the freeing. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold this. Abu Yusuf disagrees, equating it with the previous case where a price was mentioned. He argues that the master is granting ownership of the slave to the wife without compensation (a gift/hiba), and the requirement of physical possession (qabd) is dropped by necessity, analogous to the case of kaffarah where a person commands another to feed on his behalf. The two imams (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) respond: A gift (hiba) requires possession as a condition based on primary texts (nass), and possession cannot be implied or dropped because it is a physical act, unlike sale which is a legal transaction where conditions can be implied. In the kaffarah case, the deputy (the poor person) can take possession on behalf of the commander, but in this case, the slave (the husband) does not receive anything in his hand to deputize. Thus, without a price, the wife does not acquire ownership, so no conflict of ownership arises, and the marriage remains valid. The wala goes to the master because he performed the emancipation. The commentary also notes that possession can be dropped in a defective contract (fasid) like the earlier case with wine, because the defective is treated like a valid contract in some respects.

Key Terms
الهبة (al-hiba): A gift; a transfer of ownership without compensation.
القبض (al-qabd): Physical possession or taking delivery; a condition for the completion of a hiba.
الولاء (al-wala): Clientage; the legal relationship between an emancipator and a freed slave, granting inheritance rights and responsibility.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because the master retains ownership.
D) Because the husband’s consent is needed.

Key Takeaway
The majority Hanafi position: without a price, freeing a slave is a gift requiring possession, so no ownership transfer and marriage remains valid.

Validity of Non-Muslim Marriages After Conversion: Witnesses and 'Iddah
Principles of non-Muslim marriage validity upon conversion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the validity of a disbeliever's marriage without witnesses or during 'iddah after conversion to Islam, and explain the disagreements among Abu Hanifa, the Sahibayn, and Zufar.

Core Idea
A disbeliever's marriage valid by their own religion is confirmed after conversion unless it violates a universally prohibited element.

This passage introduces three principles governing marriages of disbelievers. The core rule is that any marriage valid in Islam is also valid among disbelievers if it conforms to general Shari'ah. However, for marriages invalid in Islam due to missing conditions (like witnesses or 'iddah), the Hanafi school holds that disbelievers are not bound by all Islamic specifics. The dispute between Abu Hanifa and his disciples turns on whether the prohibition is universal and agreed upon (ijma') or merely a disputed condition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three principles
The author lists three principles about marriages of disbelievers. The first covers marriages that are valid in Islam; the second covers marriages invalid due to missing conditions; the third covers marriages invalid due to mahram.
Focus on the second principle as it is the main subject of the lesson.
2
Understand the specific ruling from the matn
The matn states: 'A disbeliever married without witnesses or during the 'iddah of a disbeliever, and this is permissible in their religion, then they both become Muslim: they are confirmed upon it.' This is the ruling according to Abu Hanifa.
Note the condition that the 'iddah is of a disbeliever, not of a Muslim.
3
Follow the dispute: Sahibayn vs. Abu Hanifa
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad agree with Abu Hanifa on the case of no witnesses, but disagree on the 'iddah case. Their reasoning: prohibition of marrying a woman in 'iddah is by ijma', so disbelievers are bound by it; prohibition of marriage without witnesses is disputed, so disbelievers are not bound by it.
This distinction refutes Zufar who equated both cases.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa argues that disbelievers are not addressed (mukallaf) by the Shari'ah in these specifics. Therefore, even the 'iddah prohibition is not binding on them as a Shari'ah right; their marriage according to their own rules is valid and confirmed upon Islam.
This is a fundamental usul principle: the disbelief of the person affects the applicability of certain rulings.
5
Note the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
If a couple converts, under Abu Hanifa they remain married in both cases. Under Sahibayn, they must separate if the marriage occurred during 'iddah, but may stay married if only lacking witnesses.
This affects real-life cases of converts.

Commentary
This passage introduces the chapter on marriage of disbelievers in a Hanafi fiqh manual. The author first explains why the term 'disbeliever' (kafir) is preferred over 'polytheist' (mushrik) — to include People of the Book, though some consider them mushrik due to their beliefs. Three general principles are laid out: (1) Any marriage valid in Islam is also valid among disbelievers if it conforms to the general Shari'ah; contrary to Malik who denies this for certain cases. Evidence includes the Quranic verse about Abu Lahab's wife and the hadith that the Prophet was born from marriage. (2) Marriages invalid in Islam due to missing conditions (e.g., no witnesses, during 'iddah) are valid for disbelievers if they consider them valid, according to Abu Hanifa. After conversion to Islam, such marriages are confirmed. (3) Marriages invalid due to mahram relationship are disputed among Hanafis: some say valid, some say invalid. The specific ruling from the matn is then quoted: a disbeliever marries without witnesses or during 'iddah, and both convert; Abu Hanifa says they are confirmed. The two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) agree on the case of no witnesses but differ on 'iddah, because the prohibition of marrying a woman in 'iddah is agreed upon by all (ijma'), so disbelievers are considered bound by it, while the requirement of witnesses is a disputed matter in Islamic law, so disbelievers are not held to it. This refutes Zufar who held both cases similar. The passage ends with a note that the matn conditions the 'iddah to be of a disbeliever, but does not explicitly state that the man must be a disbeliever too.

Key Terms
المشرك (al-mushrik): Polytheist; one who associates partners with Allah. In this context, distinguished from 'kafir' to include or exclude People of the Book.
العدة (al-iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of her husband before remarrying.
شهود (shuhud): Witnesses; required for a valid Muslim marriage contract according to the majority.

Marriage of Non-Muslims: Validity After Islam and the Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Iddah
Explaining the Hanafi position on non-Muslim marriages and the intra-school dispute on iddah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the validity of marriages contracted by non-Muslims without witnesses or during iddah, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding iddah, identifying the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
A non-Muslim marriage valid in their religion remains valid after Islam; but the iddah dispute splits the Hanafi school.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq explains the three principles governing marriages of non-Muslims, with a focus on two scenarios: marriage without witnesses and marriage during iddah. While both are invalid for Muslims, the Hanafi school holds that non-Muslims are confirmed upon such marriages if they were valid in their religion—except the Sahibayn differ on the iddah case, creating a key intra-school dispute with practical consequences for lineage and remarriage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three foundational principles
The passage lays out three principles: (1) any marriage valid among Muslims is valid among non-Muslims; (2) any marriage invalid due to missing a condition (e.g., witnesses, iddah) is valid for non-Muslims if they consider it permissible; (3) marriages invalid due to sanctity of the spouse (mahram) are disputed. Focus on the second principle.
The first principle is supported by the Quranic verse about Abu Lahab's wife and the hadith 'I was born from marriage, not from fornication'.
2
Analyze the second principle: agreement and disagreement
Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn agree that marriage without witnesses is valid after conversion. They disagree on marriage during iddah: Abu Hanifa says it is valid, the Sahibayn say it is invalid. The Sahibayn argue that the prohibition of marrying a woman in iddah is a matter of consensus (ijma), so non-Muslims are bound by it.
Zufr also held a view of complete equivalence, but the author refutes it.
3
Examine Abu Hanifa's proof
Abu Hanifa argues that the prohibition cannot be established as a right of the Shari'ah because non-Muslims are not addressed (mukhattaboon) with its obligations. Iddah is a right of the husband, and since the husband does not believe in it, it cannot be enforced. The marriage is based on their own religious validity.
The author notes that even if non-Muslims are addressed with transactional laws, marriage has an element of worship, making it different.
4
Examine the Sahibayn's proof and the Hanafi response
The Sahibayn rely on the consensus of Muslims that marrying a woman in iddah is prohibited; non-Muslims are bound by such universal prohibitions. The Hanafi response is that the specific ruling of iddah is a right of the husband, and since the husband does not believe, it cannot be imposed. Also, marriage is not purely transactional.
The author also discusses a nuance about dhimmi vs. harbi but concludes no difference.
5
Determine the practical consequences (thamarah al-khilaf) and the mufta bihi
The practical difference appears in the validity of return (rujoo') after divorce and the attribution of a child born less than six months after separation. Abu Hanifa's view denies both; the Sahibayn's view establishes them. The passage states that the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is Abu Hanifa's view, as chosen in Fath al-Qadir.
The mufta bihi is that no iddah is required for non-Muslim women, so the marriage is confirmed after conversion.

باب نكاح الكافر
رب
لما فرغ من نكاح المسلمين بمرتبتيه الأحرار والأرقاء، شرع في بيان نكاح الكفار . والتعبير بنكاح الكافر أولى من التعبير بنكاح أهل الشرك كما في الهداية لأنه لا يشمل الكتابي إلا على قول من يدخله في المشرك باعتبار قول طائفة منهم عزير ابن الله والمسيح ابن الله العزة والكبرياء المنزه عن الولد وههنا ثلاثة أصول الأول أن كل نكاح صحيح بين المسلمين فهو صحيح إذا تحقق بين أهل الكفر لتظافر الاعتقادين على صحته ولعموم الرسالة، فحيث وقع من الكفار على وفق الشرع العام وجب الحكم بصحته خلافاً لمالك، ويرده قوله تعالى وامرأته حمالة الحطب ﴾ [المسد : ٤] وقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام ولدت من نكاح لا من سفاح كما في المعراج الثاني أن كل نكاح حرم بين المسلمين لفقد شرطه كالنكاح بغير شهود أو في العدة من الكافر يجوز في حقهم إذا اعتقدوه وعند أبي حنيفة ويقران عليه بعد الإسلام الثالث أن كل نكاح حرم لحرمة المحل كنكاح المحارم واختلف فيه على قوله؛ قال مشايخنا : يقع جائزاً. وقال مشايخ العراق : يقع فاسداً وسيأتي قوله : (تزوج كافر بلا شهود أو في عدة كافر وذا في دينهم جائز ثم أسلما اقرا عليه يعني عند أبي حنيفة، ووافقاه في الأول وخالفاه في الثاني لأن حرمة نكاح المعتدة مجمع عليها فكانوا ملتزمين لها، وحرمة النكاح بغير شهود مختلف فيها ولم يلتزموا أحكامنا بجميع الاختلافات، وبه اندفع قول زفر من التسوية بينهما. ولأبي حنيفة أن الحرمة لا يمكن إثباتها حقاً للشرع لأنهم لا يخاطبون
