كتاب النكاح باب نكاح الكافر
٣٦١
بحقوقه، ولا وجه إلى إيجاب العدة حقاً للزوج لأنه لا يعتقده، وإذا صح النكاح فحالة الإسلام والمرافعة حالة البقاء والشهادة ليست شرطاً فيها وكذا العدة لا تنافيها كالمنكوحة إذا وطئت بشبهة . أطلق الكافر فشمل الذمي والحربي وبحث المحقق في فتح القدير في قولهم إن الحرمة لا يمكن إثباتها حقاً للشرع لأنهم لا يخاطبون بحقوقه بأن أهل الأصول اتفقوا على أنهم مخاطبون بالمعاملات والنكاح منها، وكونه من حقوق الشرع لا ينافي كونه معاملة فيلزم اتفاق الثلاث على أنهم مخاطبون بأحكام النكاح غير أن حكم الخطاب إنما يثبت في حق المكلف ببلوغه إليه والشهرة تنزل منزلته وهي متحققة في حق أهل الذمة دون أهل الحرب، فمقتضى النظر التفصيل بين أن يكون ذمياً فلا يقر عليه وبين أن يكون حربياً فيقر عليه ا هـ. وجوابه أن النكاح لم يتمحض معاملة بل فيه معنى العبادة ولهذا كان الاشتغال به أولى من التخلي للنوافل، فما ذكره الأصوليون إنما هو في المعاملة المحضة فلا منافاة بين الموضعين، فلا فرق بين الذمي والحربي في هذا الحكم، وقيد بكونه في عدة كافر لأنها لو كانت في عدة مسلم فإنه لا يجوز يقران عليه اتفاقاً وظاهر كلام الهداية أنه لا عدة من الكافر عند الإمام أصلاً وفيه اختلاف المشايخ ؛ فذهب طائفة إليه، وأخرى إلى وجوبها عنده لكنها ضعيفة لا تمنع من صحة النكاح لضعفها كالاستبراء وفائدة الاختلاف تظهر في ثبوت الرجعة للزوج بمجرد طلاقها وفي ثبوت نسب الولد إذا أتت به لأقل من ستة أشهر؛ فعلى الأول لا يثبتان وعلى الثاني يثبتان واختار في فتح القدير الأول ومنع عدم ثبوت النسب لا فرق بينه وبين المسلم. ففي الخانية من فصل المحرمات والذمي إذا أبان امرأته الذمية فتزوجها مسلم أو ذمي من ساعته ذكر بعض المشايخ أنه يجوز له نكاحها ولا يباح له وطؤها حتى يستبرئها بحيضه في قول أبي حنيفة، وفي قول صاحبيه نكاحها باطل حتى تعتد بثلاث حيض، وروى أصحاب الأمالي عن أبي حنيفة أنه لا عدة عليها .اهـ. وقال في النهر وأقول : ينبغي أن لا يختلف في وجوبها بالنسبة إلى المسلم لأنه يعتقد وجوبها، ألا ترى أن القول بعدم وجوبها في حق الكافر مقيد بكونهم لا يدينونها وبكونه جائزاً عندهم لأنه لو لم يكن جائزاً بأن اعتقدوا وجوبها يفرق إجماعاً اهـ. قلت : لكن قد علمت أن العدة تجب حقاً للزوج وإذا كان الزوج كافراً لا يعتقدها لا يمكن إثباتها حقاً له، ولذا نقل بعض المحشين عن ابن كمال باشا عند قوله وذا في دينهم جائز» أن الشرط جوازه في دين الزوج خاصة .اهـ. أي الزوج الذي طلقها على أنه بعد ثبوت نقل ذلك عن الإمام لا وجه لإنكاره تأمل قوله وظاهر كلام (الهداية أي قوله ولا وجه إلى إيجاب العدة حقاً للزوج لأنه لا يعتقده قوله (كالاستبراء) فإنه يجوز تزويج الأمة في حال قيام وجوبه على السيد. كذا في الفتح قوله (واختار في فتح القدير الأول) عبارة الفتح وقيل الأليق الأول أي عدم وجوب ) العدة لما عرف من وجوب تركهم وما يدينون وفيه نظر لأن تركهم تحرزاً عن الغدر لعقد الذمة لا يستلزم صحة ما
A disbeliever marries without witnesses or during the iddah of a disbeliever, and if this is permissible in their religion, then later they both embrace Islam, they are confirmed upon it. ... The chapter on marriage of the disbeliever. After finishing the marriage of Muslims with its two categories of free persons and slaves, he began to explain the marriage of disbelievers. Expressing it as 'marriage of the disbeliever' is better than 'marriage of the people of shirk' as in al-Hidayah, because it does not include the People of the Book except according to the opinion of one who includes them in the polytheists due to the statement of a group of them that 'Uzair is the son of Allah and the Messiah is the son of Allah... There are three principles here: First, every marriage that is valid among Muslims is valid when it occurs among the disbelievers, due to the mutual confirmation of both beliefs on its validity and the generality of the message... Second, every marriage that is prohibited among Muslims due to the lack of a condition, such as marriage without witnesses or during iddah, is permissible for the disbelievers if they consider it so, according to Abu Hanifa, and they are confirmed upon it after Islam. Third, every marriage prohibited due to the sanctity of the place, such as marriage to mahrams, and there is disagreement on it... His statement: 'A disbeliever marries without witnesses or during the iddah of a disbeliever, and if this is permissible in their religion, then they embrace Islam, they are confirmed upon it' means according to Abu Hanifa, and the two companions agreed with him on the first but disagreed on the second because the prohibition of marrying a woman in iddah is a matter of consensus, so they were bound by it, while the prohibition of marriage without witnesses is disputed, and they did not adopt all our rulings with all their differences... For Abu Hanifa, the prohibition cannot be established as a right of the Shari'ah because they are not addressed with its obligations... The discussion continues about iddah: whether a disbeliever's iddah exists according to the Imam, and the debate about whether it is a right of the husband or of the Shari'ah, and the distinction between dhimmi and harbi.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute on marriage contracted by non-Muslims and its validity after conversion. The author, al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah, presents the three foundational principles: (1) Any marriage valid among Muslims is valid among non-Muslims, contrary to Malik. (2) Marriages invalid among Muslims due to missing a condition (like witnesses or iddah) are valid for non-Muslims if they consider them permissible, and they are confirmed upon conversion. This is Abu Hanifa's position, with the two companions agreeing on the witnesses part but disagreeing on iddah because its prohibition is based on consensus (ijma), which non-Muslims are bound by. (3) Marriages invalid due to the sanctity of the spouse (like mahram) are disputed—some Hanafi scholars say it is valid, others say it is invalid. The passage then delves into the iddah issue in detail. Abu Hanifa holds that a non-Muslim woman has no iddah because iddah is a right of the husband, and a non-Muslim husband does not believe in it, so it cannot be enforced. The two companions hold that iddah is a right of the Shari'ah, and since non-Muslims are addressed with legal rules, iddah is obligatory. The author discusses the nuance regarding dhimmi (protected non-Muslim) vs. harbi (enemy), but concludes that there is no difference in this ruling because marriage has an element of worship. The practical difference appears in the validity of returning to the wife after divorce and the lineage of a child born less than six months after separation. Abu Hanifa's view denies both, while the companions' view establishes them. The author mentions that the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in Fath al-Qadir is the first (no iddah). The passage also notes a narration from the Ashab al-Amali that Abu Hanifa said there is no iddah for a non-Muslim woman at all. Finally, the author addresses a possible objection: if the husband is Muslim, then iddah is required because he believes in it. The overall thrust is that the Hanafi school, through Abu Hanifa, gives significant weight to the non-Muslim's own religious practice and their non-address with Islamic obligations, while the two companions give more weight to the intrinsic prohibition of certain acts like marrying a woman in iddah.

Key Terms
عدة (iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of her husband before remarrying. In this context, the passage discusses whether it applies to non-Muslim women.
ذمي (dhimmi): A non-Muslim living under the protection of an Islamic state, bound by certain contractual obligations.
حربي (harbi): A non-Muslim who is not under Islamic protection and is considered at war with the Islamic state.
استبراء (istibra'): A waiting period imposed on a female slave after being purchased or separated from her master to ensure she is not pregnant. In the text, it is compared to iddah as a weak obligation that does not prevent marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a non-Muslim marries a woman during her iddah (waiting period from a previous marriage) and this is permissible in their religion, then both embrace Islam. What is the status of their marriage?
A) The marriage is invalid and must be renewed after Islam.
B) The marriage is valid and they are confirmed upon it.
C) The marriage is valid only if they perform a new contract in front of Muslim witnesses.
D) The marriage is suspended until the iddah ends, after which it becomes valid.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, non-Muslims are not bound by Islamic obligations they do not believe in, but prohibitions based on consensus may still apply, leading to an intra-school dispute on iddah; the mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view that no iddah is required for non-Muslim women.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
8 min
Ruling on a Disbeliever Marrying a Woman in 'Iddah from a Disbeliever
States the ruling of a disbeliever marrying a woman in 'iddah, with intra-Hanafi disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi preferred view on the validity of a disbeliever's marriage to a woman in 'iddah from a disbeliever, identify the intra-Hanafi dispute with the Sahibayn, and explain the practical consequences (thamarah al-khilaf) regarding raj'a and lineage.

Core Idea
A disbeliever's marriage to a woman in 'iddah from a disbeliever is valid without 'iddah, because 'iddah is his right and he does not believe in it.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a rare but legally significant scenario: a disbeliever marrying a woman who is still in the waiting period ('iddah) from a previous disbeliever husband. Unlike the Muslim context where 'iddah is strictly required, the Hanafi school, led by Imam Abu Hanifa, takes a unique position that such a marriage is valid without observing 'iddah. However, the two senior students of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), disagreed on this point. This lesson unpacks the preferred view, the reasoning, and the practical consequences of the dispute.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Recall the preferred view: the marriage of a disbeliever to a woman in 'iddah from a disbeliever is valid immediately, and no 'iddah is required.
This is the view attributed to Abu Hanifa and preferred by later authorities like al-Marghinani.
2
Identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Note that the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) held that the woman must observe three menstrual cycles of 'iddah, and without it the marriage is invalid.
Some later mashayikh also held the obligation of 'iddah but considered it weak and not invalidating the marriage.
3
Analyze the Hanafi dalil
Understand the proof based on istihsan: 'iddah is a right of the husband; since the disbeliever does not believe in it, it cannot be imposed. Additionally, marriage includes an element of worship, so the principle that disbelievers are not addressed by Shari'ah rights does not fully apply.
The dalil is aqli (reason-based), not naqli.
4
Examine the response to the Sahibayn
The Sahibayn's view is considered weak (da'if) and does not invalidate the marriage; at most, istibra' is required out of caution.
This is similar to the case of marrying a slave-girl without istibra'.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify two practical consequences: (1) raj'a (husband's right to take back his wife) is established only in the Sahibayn's view; (2) if the wife gives birth within six months, lineage is attached to the new husband only in the Sahibayn's view.
In Abu Hanifa's view, the child is attributed to the ex-husband.

With his rights, and there is no way to impose the waiting period as a right of the husband because he does not believe in it. If the marriage is valid, then the state of Islam and litigation are conditions for its continuation and testimony are not conditions in it, and likewise the waiting period does not contradict it, like the married woman who is penetrated by mistake. He (the author) made the disbeliever general, including the dhimmi and the harbi. The investigator in Fath al-Qadir researched their statement that the prohibition cannot be established as a right of the Shari'ah because they are not addressed by its rights; the usul scholars agreed that they are addressed by transactions and marriage is among them. Being from the rights of the Shari'ah does not contradict it being a transaction, so the three (Imams) agree that they are addressed by the rulings of marriage, however, the effect of the address is established for the one obligated when it reaches him, and notoriety takes its place, and it is realized for the dhimmis but not for the harbis. So the logical requirement is to differentiate between a dhimmi, so it is not upheld against him, and a harbi, so it is upheld against him. The answer is that marriage is not purely a transaction but has the meaning of worship, and for this reason engaging in it is better than devoting oneself to supererogatory acts. What the usul scholars mentioned is only about pure transactions, so there is no contradiction between the two contexts, so there is no difference between the dhimmi and the harbi in this ruling. He restricted it to being during the waiting period of a disbeliever, because if it were during the waiting period of a Muslim, then it is not permissible for them to be left on it by consensus. The apparent statement of al-Hidayah is that there is no waiting period from the disbeliever according to the Imam (Abu Hanifa) at all, and there is a difference among the mashayikh: a group went to that, and others to its obligation with him, but it is weak and does not prevent the validity of the marriage due to its weakness, like istibra'. The benefit of the difference appears in the establishment of the right of return (raj'a) for the husband merely by divorcing her and in the establishment of the lineage of the child if she brings it at less than six months. According to the first, they are not established, and according to the second, they are established. And (the author) chose in Fath al-Qadir the first view and prevented the non-establishment of lineage, saying there is no difference between him and a Muslim. In al-Khaniyyah, from the chapter on prohibitions: if a dhimmi divorced his dhimmi wife and a Muslim or a dhimmi marries her immediately, some mashayikh mentioned that it is permissible for him to marry her, but it is not permissible for him to have intercourse with her until he makes istibra' by her menstruation according to Abu Hanifa's statement, and according to the two companions, her marriage is invalid until she observes the waiting period of three menstruations. And the authors of al-Amali narrated from Abu Hanifa that there is no waiting period for her. In al-Nahr: I say: There should be no difference in its obligation with respect to a Muslim because he believes in its obligation. Do you not see that the statement on its non-obligation for a disbeliever is restricted to the fact that they do not practice it and to its being permissible among them, because if it were not permissible, and they believed in its obligation, they would separate by consensus? I say: But you have known that the waiting period is obligatory as a right of the husband, and if the husband is a disbeliever who does not believe in it, it cannot be established as his right. Therefore, some commentators from Ibn Kamal Pasha, when he said 'and if it is permissible in their religion', that the condition is its permissibility in the religion of the husband specifically. That is, the husband who divorced her. After the confirmation of that transmission from the Imam, there is no way to deny it. Ponder his statement: 'And the apparent statement of al-Hidayah, i.e., his statement: 'And there is no way to impose the waiting period as a right of the husband because he does not believe in it.' His statement: 'like istibra',' for it is permissible to marry a slave-girl while its obligation upon the master is ongoing. As in al-Fath. His statement: 'And (the author) chose in Fath al-Qadir the first statement,' the wording of al-Fath: 'And it is said: the more appropriate is the first, i.e., non-obligation of the waiting period, because of what is known about the obligation to leave them and what they practice.' And there is a consideration, because their leaving as a precaution against betrayal due to the dhimma contract does not entail the validity of what they have done. Do not say: The waiting period is not obligatory. And if the owner of the child is known by another way, it becomes necessary to annex him to him after the child comes from a valid marriage bed and she brings it at less than six months from the divorce, which indicates that, so he is annexed to him. But they did not transmit that from Abu Hanifa regarding its establishment or non-establishment; rather, they differed: his statement that the marriage is valid based on the non-obligation of the waiting period, so that difference branches off from that or not? So we say: we say by its non-obligation, and the lineage is established in the mentioned case. And he restricted it to being permissible in their religion because if it were not permissible in their religion, they would separate between them by consensus, because it occurred invalidly, so renewal is necessary. And in Fath al-Qadir: So in the case of hijra, the necessity of the waiting period follows if they believe in it, because the result of the change of abode is separation, not negation of the waiting period. And he made absolute the non-separation upon Islam, including the case where both embrace Islam and the waiting period has expired or not. But if they embrace Islam and it has expired, they are not separated by consensus, as in al-Mabsut. And he did not mention non-separation in the case where they come to us for judgment, because it is known from Islam by priority.
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This is a ruling with an intra-Hanafi dispute from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, commenting on Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The issue is whether a disbeliever's marriage to a woman who is in 'iddah (waiting period) from a previous disbeliever husband is valid, and whether the disbeliever is required to observe 'iddah. The passage begins by stating the principle that 'iddah is a right of the husband; since the disbeliever does not believe in it, it cannot be imposed as his right. The marriage is valid, and the fact that the woman is in 'iddah does not invalidate it, just as in the case of penetration by mistake (shubha). The author then distinguishes between dhimmi and harbi: some argued that the prohibition of marrying a woman in 'iddah can only be established as a right of the Shari'ah for those who are addressed by it, and since disbelievers are not addressed except in transactions (mu'amalat), and marriage is a transaction, they are addressed. Fath al-Qadir raised a subtle point: if they are not addressed by the Shari'ah rights, then for a dhimmi, the marriage is not upheld (since he is under Islamic rule), but for a harbi, it is upheld (since he is outside Islamic jurisdiction). The commentator responds that marriage is not purely a transaction but also an act of worship (because it is a means to lawful cohabitation and is preferred over supererogatory acts), so the principle that disbelievers are not addressed by Shari'ah rights does not fully apply; hence there is no difference between dhimmi and harbi in this ruling. The ruling is restricted to a woman in 'iddah from a disbeliever; if she were in 'iddah from a Muslim, the marriage would be invalid by consensus. The passage then discusses the view of Al-Hidayah that according to Imam Abu Hanifa, there is no 'iddah from a disbeliever at all. The mashayikh differed: some agree, others say 'iddah is required but it is weak and does not prevent the validity of the marriage (similar to istibra'). The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) concerns two matters: (1) the husband's right to take back his wife (raj'a) after a revocable divorce—if no 'iddah is required, raj'a does not occur; if 'iddah is required, it does. (2) The lineage of a child born within six months of the divorce—if no 'iddah, the child is not attributed to the new husband (since the child would be from the ex-husband due to the minimum pregnancy period of six months), but if 'iddah is required, the child is attributed to the new husband. The author of Fath al-Qadir preferred the first view (no 'iddah) and argued that the non-establishment of lineage is not specific to disbelievers; it applies equally to Muslims. Then, a specific case from Al-Khaniyyah is cited: a dhimmi divorces his dhimmi wife, and she is immediately married by a Muslim or another dhimmi. Some mashayikh rule that the marriage is permissible but the husband cannot have intercourse until istibra' (one menstruation) according to Abu Hanifa; according to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the marriage is invalid until she completes three menstruations. Another report from Ashab al-Amali states that Abu Hanifa said no 'iddah at all. The commentator from Al-Nahr then adds that there should be no difference regarding the Muslim husband because the Muslim believes in 'iddah; the ruling of non-obligation for the disbeliever is conditioned on the fact that the disbeliever does not practice it and it is permissible in their religion. If the disbeliever believed in its obligation, they would be separated by consensus. The author of Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq (Ibn Nujaym) responds that since 'iddah is a right of the husband, and the disbeliever does not believe in it, it cannot be imposed as his right. He cites Ibn Kamal Pasha who clarified that the condition is its permissibility in the religion of the husband specifically. The passage then engages with Fath al-Qadir's choice, arguing that the obligation to leave disbelievers and their practices (as a dhimma contract condition) does not entail the validity of their marriages. The discussion continues with the issue of lineage: if the child is born within six months of the new marriage, lineage is attributed to the new husband only if 'iddah is required; otherwise, it is not. The author criticizes the contradictory reports from Abu Hanifa. Finally, the passage discusses the condition that the marriage must be permissible in their religion: if it is not permissible, they must separate by consensus because the marriage is batil and needs renewal. It also touches on hijra: in cases of emigration, 'iddah may be required if they believe in it, because the separation due to change of abode is different from negation of 'iddah. If both spouses embrace Islam and the 'iddah has expired, they are not separated by consensus (as in Al-Mabsut). The passage concludes by noting that if they come to us for judgment, it is even more clear that they should not be separated.

Key Terms
العدة (al-‘iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of her husband before she can remarry. In this context, it specifically refers to the waiting period from a disbeliever husband.
الذمي (al-dhimmī): A non-Muslim subject living permanently under Muslim rule with a pact of protection (dhimma).
الحربي (al-ḥarbī): A non-Muslim who is not under Muslim rule and with whom Muslims are in a state of war (or not in a peace treaty).
الاستبراء (al-istibra’): A waiting period of one menstrual cycle (or a lunar month if no menstruation) to ensure the womb is empty of pregnancy, typically for slave-girls or women whose status is uncertain.
الرجعة (al-raj‘a): The right of a husband to take back his wife during the 'iddah period after a revocable divorce (ṭalāq raj‘ī).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view (mufta bihi) in this passage, what is the ruling on a dhimmi man marrying a dhimmi woman who has just been divorced by her dhimmi husband and is in 'iddah?
A) The marriage is valid immediately and no 'iddah is required.
B) The marriage is invalid until she completes three menstrual cycles.
C) The marriage is valid but he cannot have intercourse until she completes one menstruation.
D) The marriage is invalid regardless of 'iddah.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi preferred view holds that a disbeliever's marriage to a woman in 'iddah from a disbeliever is valid without 'iddah, because 'iddah is a right of the husband and he does not believe in it.

Disbeliever's Marriage to a Mahram: Validity and Consequences upon Islam
Intra-Hanafi dispute on mahram marriage validity with thamarah al-khilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi positions on the validity of a disbeliever's marriage to a mahram and apply the practical consequences in maintenance, ihsan, and inheritance.

Core Idea
A disbeliever's marriage to a mahram is valid in form but must be dissolved upon Islam; the difference matters for maintenance and ihsan.

When a disbeliever marries a prohibited relative (mahram) and then embraces Islam, what is the status of that marriage? The Hanafi school has two views: Abu Hanifa considers the marriage valid in its outward form, while the two companions (Sahibayn) deem it invalid from the start. This lesson unpacks the dispute, the reasoning, and the practical consequences that depend on which view is adopted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
What is the ruling on a marriage contract between a disbeliever and his mahram (e.g., mother, sister) when one or both embrace Islam?
The passage contrasts this with 'iddah, where the marriage can continue after waiting period.
2
State the two Hanafi positions
Abu Hanifa: The marriage is sahih (valid) in form but must be dissolved by the judge. Sahibayn: The marriage is batil (invalid) from the start, and separation is automatic.
Both agree on separation, but differ on the legal effect before separation.
3
Understand the basis for Abu Hanifa's view
The contract was valid in the disbelievers' religion, so it has some legal recognition. However, the prohibition is absolute in Islam, so it cannot continue.
Unlike 'iddah, mahram prohibits the relationship itself, not just temporary waiting.
4
Analyze the effect of one spouse embracing Islam alone
If only one spouse refers the case to the judge, Abu Hanifa says the judge does not separate them because the non-Muslim's belief does not change. If both refer, separation by consensus.
This shows the impact of referral versus actual Islam.
5
Extract the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequences)
The difference affects: (1) maintenance during the marriage, (2) ihsan status (whether the husband becomes muhsan and subject to stoning for zina), (3) inheritance between the spouses.
According to the correct view (sahih), maintenance is owed, ihsan is not removed, and inheritance should apply but al-Bada'i reports no inheritance.

His statement: (And if she were a mahram, he separates between them) means if the woman is a mahram (prohibited relative) for the disbeliever, then the judge separates between them if both or one of them embraces Islam, by consensus, because marriage to mahrams has the ruling of invalidity between them (in the view of the two companions). And (the ruling) becomes necessary upon Islam, so he separates. And in his view (Abu Hanifa), it has the ruling of validity in the sound (view), except that regarding the waiting period (we mentioned that) in mahram it contradicts the continuation of the marriage, so he separates, unlike the waiting period because it does not contradict it. Then, if one of them embraces Islam, he separates between them. And if one of them refers the matter (to the judge), he does not separate in his view, contrary to the two companions. The difference is that the entitlement of one does not become invalid by the referral of the other, since his belief does not change by it. But the belief of the stubborn does not oppose the Islam of a Muslim because Islam is high and nothing is higher than it. And if both refer, he separates by consensus because their referral is like their arbitration. Thus in al-Hidayah. So it shows that the correct (view) is that his marriage to his mahram is valid, and it is said it is corrupt. The benefit of the difference appears in the obligation of maintenance if she demands it, and in the removal of his ihsan (immunity from stoning) through consummation. According to the correct view, it (maintenance) is obligatory and (ihsan) is not removed, so that if he embraces Islam and someone accuses him of unlawful intercourse, he would be subject to the hadd (punishment) for defamation. And the requirement of the view of validity is that they inherit from each other. Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses the ruling on a disbeliever's marriage to a mahram (a prohibited relative, such as mother, sister, etc.) and how it is affected when the couple embraces Islam. It compares this with the earlier discussion on 'iddah. The key point is that marriage to a mahram is fundamentally problematic because the relationship itself is inherently prohibited in Islam. According to the two companions (Sahibayn), such a marriage is batil (invalid) from the outset, so when they embrace Islam, the judge separates them by consensus. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the contract is sahih (valid) in its outward form (because it was valid in their pre-Islamic religion), but due to the nature of the relationship, it cannot continue after Islam. So separation is also required. The difference between 'iddah and mahram: in 'iddah, the marriage can continue after the expiry of the waiting period, but in mahram, the relationship itself is permanently forbidden, so separation is immediate. If only one spouse embraces Islam, they separate (according to both views). However, if only one spouse refers the case to a judge (without the other), Abu Hanifa says the judge does not separate them because the one who did not embrace Islam still does not believe in the prohibition, and his belief does not change by litigation. But if both refer, separation is by consensus. The practical consequences of the difference (between the valid and invalid views) are: (1) Maintenance: if the wife demands maintenance during the marriage, according to the correct view (validity), she is entitled to it; according to the other view, she is not. (2) Ihsan (being muhsan for stoning): if the husband consummates the marriage and then embraces Islam, does he become muhsan? According to the correct view, he does not lose his ihsan; if someone accuses him of unlawful intercourse (zina) after Islam, the accuser is subject to hadd for qadhf (false accusation). According to the other view, he loses ihsan. (3) Inheritance: logically, if the marriage is valid, they should inherit from each other, but it is reported in al-Bada'i that they do not inherit, contradicting a claim of consensus in al-Qahistani. The passage ends incomplete.

Key Terms
المحرم (al-maḥram): A person with whom marriage is permanently forbidden due to blood relationship, marriage, or suckling. In this context, it refers to a woman who is a prohibited relative for the disbeliever, e.g., his mother or sister.
الإحصان (al-iḥṣān): A legal status of being married (to a valid marriage) and having consummated the marriage, which increases the punishment for zina (stoning) and requires proof by four witnesses.
النفقة (al-nafaqah): Maintenance (food, clothing, shelter) that a husband is obliged to provide to his wife.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The marriage is valid and they can remain married.
B) The marriage is invalid from the start (batil) and they must separate.
C) The marriage is valid (sahih) but the judge separates them because it is a mahram relationship.
D) The marriage is considered only as a formality with no legal effects.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's mufta bihi holds that a disbeliever's marriage to a mahram is valid in form but must be dissolved upon Islam, with practical consequences for maintenance, ihsan, and inheritance.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on 'Iddah for Disbelievers and Lineage
Intra-Hanafi dispute on 'iddah for disbelievers and lineage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the differing Hanafi views on whether 'iddah is obligatory for disbelievers and explain its impact on establishing lineage.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school is divided: is 'iddah required for non-Muslims, and does its absence affect lineage?

This passage explores a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute. At issue is whether the 'iddah (waiting period) is obligatory for disbelieving spouses when one converts to Islam, and how that affects the establishment of lineage (nasab). Some Hanafi scholars argue that Abu Hanifa's validation of such marriages implies non-obligation of 'iddah, while others contend that 'iddah is still required, especially if the disbelievers themselves believe in it. Understanding this dispute is crucial for correctly applying rulings on remarriage and paternity in cross-religious contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core dispute
Determine whether the disagreement is about the obligation of 'iddah for disbelievers when the marriage is valid in their religion, and what the two main positions are.
2
Examine the two Hanafi positions
State each position: (1) 'Iddah is not obligatory because non-Muslims do not believe in it. (2) 'Iddah is obligatory if the disbelievers themselves believe in it (Fath al-Qadir).
3
Analyze the textual basis
Look at how the author of Fath al-Qadir argues that 'iddah is necessary for the emigrant woman (muhajira) if they believe in it, and how the present source critiques the inference from Abu Hanifa.
The source says 'they did not transmit that from Abu Hanifa with its affirmation nor its negation.'
4
Consider the impact on lineage
Note that if the child is born within six months of divorce and from a valid bed, lineage is established even without 'iddah. This shows that lineage does not absolutely depend on 'iddah in all cases.
Lineage can be established through other means, like known paternity.
5
Apply the restriction on marriage validity
Understand that the marriage must be permissible in the disbelievers' religion; otherwise, they must separate. This restricts the application of the dispute to valid marriages only.
If the marriage is invalid in their own religion, it is void ab initio.

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute. It discusses whether 'iddah is obligatory for disbelievers when one spouse converts to Islam and the marriage is valid in their religion. Some Hanafi scholars hold that 'iddah is not obligatory because the non-Muslims do not believe in it, but this raises the question of whether lineage can be established without 'iddah. The passage clarifies that the disagreement stems from different interpretations of Abu Hanifa's statements, with some scholars deducing that his ruling of validity of such marriages was based on non-obligation of 'iddah. The text critiques this deduction, noting that it does not necessarily follow.

Key Terms
العدة (al-ʿiddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of husband before remarrying.
النسب (al-nasab): Lineage or paternity, established by valid marriage or acknowledgment.

Marriage of Mahram among Disbelievers: Dispute between Imam and Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on mahram marriage validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions (Imam vs Sahibayn) on the validity of a mahram marriage among disbelievers and explain the practical differences upon conversion to Islam.

Core Idea
Imam Abu Hanifa validates a disbeliever's mahram marriage; the Sahibayn invalidate it — the difference affects nafaqah, ihsan, and inheritance.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq (commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq) examines a critical intra-Hanafi dispute: when a disbeliever marries a mahram (a prohibited relative), what is the status of that marriage if one or both convert to Islam? Imam Abu Hanifa holds the marriage is valid in their religion and separation requires specific conditions; his two students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, consider it invalid ab initio. The practical consequences — nafaqah, hadd al-qadhf, and inheritance — depend on which view is adopted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core dispute
State the central issue: whether a disbeliever's marriage to a mahram is valid or invalid, and under what conditions the judge separates upon conversion to Islam.
2
State Imam Abu Hanifa's position and dalil
Imam: the contract is valid (sahih); upon Islam of one spouse, the judge does not separate unless both litigate or both convert. Dalil: the principle 'Islam is high and nothing is higher' and that the non-Muslim's right is not nullified by the other's conversion alone.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and dalil
Sahibayn: the marriage is invalid (batil); upon conversion of either spouse, the judge separates immediately. Dalil: such marriages have no validity in any divine law and must be terminated.
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf (practical differences)
According to Imam: nafaqah is owed, ihsan remains intact (so hadd al-qadhf applies), and inheritance would normally follow (though later works say no inheritance by consensus). According to Sahibayn: nafaqah not owed, ihsan is lost (no hadd), and no inheritance.
5
Examine the tarjih (preferred view)
Al-Hidayah sides with Imam Abu Hanifa on validity and the need for both to litigate. However, later works like al-Bada'i' and al-Muhit agree that inheritance does not occur, regardless of the validity view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ولو كانت محرمه فرق بينهما) أي لو كانت المرأة محرماً للكافر فإن القاضي يفرق بينهما إذا أسلما أو أحدهما اتفاقاً لأن نكاح المحارم له حكم البطلان فيما بينهم عندهما كما وجب التعرض بالإسلام فيفرق وعنده له حكم الصحة في الصحيح إلا أن في العدة ذكرنا في المحرمية تنافي بقاء النكاح فيفرق بخلاف العدة لأنها لا تنافيه ثم بإسلام أحدهما يفرق بينهما وبمرافعة أحدهما لا يفرق عنده خلافاً لهما والفرق أن استحقاق أحدهما لا يبطل بمرافعة صاحبه إذ لا يتغير به اعتقاده أما اعتقاد المصرّ لا يعارض إسلام المسلم لأن الإسلام يعلو ولا يعلى عليه، ولو ترافعا يفرق بالإجماع لأن مرافعتهما كتحكيمهما. كذا في الهداية. فأفاد أن الصحيح أن عقده على محرمه صحيح، وقيل فاسد، وفائدة الخلاف تظهر في وجوب النفقة إذا طلبت وفي سقوط إحصانه بالدخول فيه، فعلى الصحيح يجب ولا يسقط حتى لو أسلم وقذفه إنسان يحد، ومقتضى القول بالصحة أن يتوارثا والمنقول في البدائع أنهما لا يتوارثان اتفاقاً، وعلله في التبيين بأن الإرث يثبت بالنص على خلاف القياس فيما إذا كانت الزوجية مطلقة بنكاح صحيح فيقتصر عليه وعلله في المحيط بأن نكاح المحارم في شريعة آدم لم يثبت كونه سبباً لاستحقاق الميراث في دينه فلا يصير سبباً للميراث في ديانتهم لأنه لا عبرة لديانتهم إذ لم يعتمد شرعاً ما اهـ. وقد يقال : هل كان نكاح المحارم في تلك الشريعة سبباً لوجوب النفقة؟ فالحاصل أن في نكاح المحارم يفرق بينهما القاضي بإسلام أحدهما أو بمرافعتهما لا بمرافعة أحدهما عند الإمام، وإما إذا لم تحصل المرافعة أصلاً فلا تفريق اتفاقاً الأمر بتركهم وما يدينون وفي التبيين وعلى هذا الخلاف المطلقة ثلاثاً والجمع بين المحارم أو الخمس ا هـ .
His statement: 'And if she was a mahram, he separates between them' i.e., if the woman was a mahram to the disbeliever, then the judge separates between them if both become Muslim or one of them by agreement, because the marriage of mahrams has the ruling of invalidity between them according to them (the two students) as it becomes obligatory to address it upon Islam, so he separates. And according to him (Imam) it has the ruling of validity in the correct view, except regarding the 'iddah we mentioned in the mahram discussion that it is incompatible with the continuation of marriage, so he separates, unlike 'iddah because it does not contradict it. Then by the Islam of one of them he separates between them, and by the litigation of one of them he does not separate according to him, contrary to them. The difference is that the right of one of them is not nullified by the litigation of his companion since his belief does not change thereby. As for the belief of the one who persists, it does not oppose the Muslim's Islam because Islam is high and nothing is higher than it. And if both litigate, he separates by consensus because their litigation is like their arbitration. Thus in al-Hidayah. So it indicates that the correct view is that his contract upon a mahram is valid, and it was said invalid. The benefit of the dispute appears in the obligation of nafaqah if demanded and in the fall of his ihsan by consummation therein. According to the correct view, it is obligatory and does not fall, so even if he becomes Muslim and someone slanders him, he is flogged. And the implication of the view of validity is that they inherit from each other, but it is transmitted in al-Bada'i' that they do not inherit from each other by agreement, and he explained in al-Tabyin that inheritance is established by text contrary to analogy when the marital relationship is absolute by a valid marriage, so it is confined to that. And he explained in al-Muhit that the marriage of mahrams in the sharia of Adam was not established as a cause for deserving inheritance in his religion, so it does not become a cause for inheritance in their religion because their religion is not considered since it is not based on revelation. It may be said: Was the marriage of mahrams in that sharia a cause for the obligation of nafaqah? The conclusion is that in the marriage of mahrams, the judge separates between them by the Islam of one of them or by their litigation, not by the litigation of one of them according to Imam. And if no litigation occurs at all, then there is no separation by agreement, as per the order to leave them and their beliefs, and in al-Tabyin, and on this dispute are the thrice-divorced woman and the combination of mahrams or five, end.
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and an intra-Hanafi dispute. It addresses the situation where a disbeliever marries a woman who is a mahram (prohibited relative) in Islam, but permissible in his religion. Upon one or both converting to Islam, the judge must separate them according to all Hanafi scholars because such a marriage is invalid in Islam. However, Imam Abu Hanifa differs from his two students (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) on whether the judge can separate based on the request of only one spouse: Imam says no, while the Sahibayn say yes. The reason given is that Islam is superior and does not require agreement of the non-Muslim partner. The passage also discusses the consequences: if the marriage is considered valid (Imam's view), then nafaqah is owed, the husband's ihsan (status of being muhsan) remains intact (so he is liable for hadd slander), and inheritance would normally follow, but later Hanafi works like al-Bada'i' state that they do not inherit by agreement because such marriages are not recognized as a cause for inheritance in divine law. The text presents the conflicting views and the practical differences.

Key Terms
محرم (mahram): A person with whom marriage is permanently forbidden due to blood relationship or close kinship.
إحصان (ihsan): The state of being muhsan (chaste, free, married) which affects hadd penalties for slander and adultery.
حد القذف (hadd al-qadhf): The prescribed punishment (80 lashes) for slandering a chaste person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Immediately upon conversion of either spouse
B) Only if both spouses convert to Islam
C) Only if the non-Muslim spouse also litigates or both convert
D) Only if the judge deems it necessary

Key Takeaway
Imam Abu Hanifa validates a disbeliever's mahram marriage; separation upon conversion requires either both to convert or both to litigate, affecting nafaqah, ihsan, and inheritance.

Cases Where Judge Separates Disbelieving Couples Without Litigation
Lists specific cases for judicial separation without litigation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the cases where a judge separates a disbelieving couple without litigation and explain the intra-Hanafi difference regarding renewal of the marriage contract after conversion.

Core Idea
A judge must separate disbelieving couples in certain invalid marriages even without a lawsuit.

This lesson examines specific cases from Al-Muhit where a judge must separate a disbelieving couple even if they do not bring the case to court. These cases involve marriages that are fundamentally invalid under Islamic law, such as triple divorce without intervening marriage or marriage during a Muslim's 'iddah. Understanding these cases clarifies the boundaries of judicial intervention in non-Muslim marriages.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three specific cases
Locate the three cases listed in the passage: (1) khul' without new contract, (2) triple divorce without intervening marriage, (3) marriage during a Muslim's 'iddah.
These are cases where the marriage is inherently invalid.
2
Apply the principle of triple divorce
Note that triple divorce severs marriage in all religions, so the judge can separate without violating the husband's rights.
Consensus (ijma') supports separation in this case.
3
Recognize the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Identify the disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding a couple who renews the contract after converting to Islam.
Imam says no separation; Sahibayn say separation required.
4
Understand practical implications
If the judge follows Imam's view, the couple inherits, nafaqah is due, and the husband remains muhsan.
These consequences do not apply if the judge separates them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذكر في المحيط : لو كانت امرأة الذمي مطلقة ثلاثاً فطلبت التفريق يفرق بينهما بالإجماع لأن هذا التفريق لا يتضمن إبطال حق على الزوج لأن الطلقات الثلاث قاطعة لملك النكاح في الأديان كلها. ثم ذكر بعدها أنه يفرق بينهما من غير مرافعة في مواضع بأن يخلعها ثم يقيم معها من غير عقد أو يطلقها ثلاثاً ثم يتزوجها قبل التزوج بآخر لأنه زنا، أو يتزوج كتابية في عدة مسلم صيانة لماء المسلم ا هـ . فحاصله أنه إذا طلقها ثلاثاً إن أمسكها من غير أن يجدد النكاح عليها فرق بينهما، وإن لم يترافعا إلى القاضي، وإن جدد عقد النكاح يسلما بل ترافعا إلينا لم يفرق بينهما معتقدين ذلك ويجري الإرث بينهما ويقضى بالنفقة ولا يسقط إحصانه حتى بحد قاذفه ، وهذا عنده خلافاً لهما في كل من الأربعة كما في المحيط اهـ.
And he mentioned in al-Muhit: If the wife of a dhimmi was thrice-divorced and she requests separation, he separates between them by consensus because this separation does not entail invalidating a right of the husband, for the three divorces terminate the ownership of marriage in all religions. Then he mentioned after it that he separates between them without litigation in situations: that he divorces her by khul' then lives with her without a contract, or divorces her thrice then marries her before marrying another, because it is zina; or he marries a kitabiyya while she is in the 'iddah of a Muslim, to protect the water of a Muslim, end. So the conclusion is that if he divorces her thrice and he retains her without renewing the marriage contract, he separates between them, even if they do not litigate before the judge. And if he renews the marriage contract, they become Muslim but they litigate before us, he does not separate between them while they believe that (their marriage is valid), and inheritance runs between them, and nafaqah is decreed, and his ihsan does not fall, so the slanderer is flogged. And this is according to him (Imam), contrary to them (Sahibayn) in each of the four, as in al-Muhit, end.
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement listing specific cases where the judge must separate a disbelieving couple without them bringing a lawsuit. The cases are: (1) the husband divorces his wife by khul' and then continues to live with her without a new contract; (2) he divorces her thrice and then remarries her without an intervening marriage to another; (3) he marries a Kitabiyya (Jewish or Christian woman) who is still in the 'iddah of a Muslim man. In all these, the separation is to protect the integrity of Islamic rulings and prevent zina. The passage also notes that according to Imam Abu Hanifa, if the couple renews the marriage contract after converting to Islam and then comes to the judge, the judge does not separate them because the marriage is now valid in Islam. This contrasts with the Sahibayn who would separate. The practical difference: if they are not separated, they inherit, nafaqah is due, and the husband remains muhsan.

Key Terms
مطلقة ثلاثاً (mutallaqah thalāthan): A woman who has been divorced three times, making her permanently forbidden to her husband until she marries another man and is divorced.
خلع (khulʿ): Divorce initiated by the wife in exchange for compensation (usually with the husband's consent).
عدة المسلم (ʿiddat al-muslim): The waiting period of a Muslim woman after divorce or death; marrying a woman in her 'iddah is invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which of the following cases does the judge separate a disbelieving couple without them requesting it, according to Al-Muhit?
A) The wife requests a divorce
B) The husband divorces his wife once and then takes her back
C) The husband divorces his wife thrice and then remarries her without an intervening marriage to another
D) The husband converts to Islam and the wife remains a disbeliever

Key Takeaway
The judge separates a disbelieving couple without litigation in three specific cases of invalid marriage, but if they renew the contract after conversion, Imam Abu Hanifa does not require separation, while the Sahibayn do.

Invalid Marriages of Dhimmis: No Inheritance or Legal Effects
Explains why invalid dhimmi marriages yield no inheritance or maintenance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that marriages invalid in Islam (e.g., marriage to mahrams) among dhimmis create no inheritance or maintenance rights in Islamic law.

Core Idea
A marriage invalid in Islam yields no legal effects even if the couple's prior religion permitted it.

The Hanafi school holds that when a marriage is fundamentally invalid in Islam—such as marriage to a close blood relative (mahram)—it cannot produce legal consequences like inheritance or maintenance, even if the couple were dhimmis who considered it valid under their own religion. This lesson explores the basis for this rule and its implications for converts and their families.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
Recognize that the passage deals with marriages among dhimmis that are invalid in Islam (e.g., nikah al-maharim). The ruling is that such marriages do not create inheritance or maintenance obligations in Islamic law.
This is the foundational point from which all further details flow.
2
Understand the basis in earlier laws
The author explains that marriage to mahrams was not established as a cause for inheritance even in the law of Prophet Adam (peace be upon him). Therefore, it cannot produce such effects in Islam, as previous religions are not legally recognized sources of rulings.
This argument uses the principle that a cause (sabab) for inheritance must be valid in Islamic law itself.
3
Distinguish between valid and invalid marriages
Contrast this with a valid marriage among dhimmis (e.g., a proper marriage to a non-mahram), which is recognized and produces inheritance. Only marriages that are fundamentally invalid in Islam yield no effects.
The key distinction is whether the marriage itself is considered valid (sahih) or void (batil) in Islamic law.
4
Apply the rule to inheritance and maintenance
From the passage, inheritance is not established for invalid marriages, and likewise maintenance is not obligatory. The text notes that even if the couple lived together under their former religion, no financial claims arise in Islamic courts.
This has practical implications for cases where one spouse converts and the marriage is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يتوارثان اتفاقاً، وعلله في التبيين بأن الإرث يثبت بالنص على خلاف القياس فيما إذا كانت الزوجية مطلقة بنكاح صحيح فيقتصر عليه وعلله في المحيط بأن نكاح المحارم في شريعة آدم لم يثبت كونه سبباً لاستحقاق الميراث في دينه فلا يصير سبباً للميراث في ديانتهم لأنه لا عبرة لديانتهم إذ لم يعتمد شرعاً ما اهـ. وقد يقال : هل كان نكاح المحارم في تلك الشريعة سبباً لوجوب النفقة؟ فالحاصل أن في نكاح المحارم يفرق بينهما القاضي بإسلام أحدهما أو بمرافعتهما لا بمرافعة أحدهما عند الإمام، وإما إذا لم تحصل المرافعة أصلاً فلا تفريق اتفاقاً الأمر بتركهم وما يدينون وفي التبيين وعلى هذا الخلاف المطلقة ثلاثاً والجمع بين فحاصله أنه إذا طلقها ثلاثاً إن أمسكها من غير أن يجدد النكاح عليها فرق بينهما، وإن لم يترافعا إلى القاضي، وإن جدد عقد النكاح يسلما بل ترافعا إلينا لم يفرق بينهما معتقدين ذلك ويجري الإرث بينهما ويقضى بالنفقة ولا يسقط إحصانه حتى بحد قاذفه ، وهذا عنده خلافاً لهما في كل من الأربعة كما في المحيط اهـ. وفي سكب الأنهر للطرابلسي: ولا يتوارثون بنكاح لا يقران عليه كنكاح المحارم وهذا هو الصحيح. ثم إن ما ذكرناه عن القهستاني يخالف ما نقله المؤلف عن الهداية من أنهما لو ترافعا يفرق بالإجماع قوله (ثم ذكر بعدها أنه يفرق قال الزيلعي : وذكر في الغاية معزياً إلى المحيط أن المطلقة ثلاثاً لو طلبت التفريق يفرق بينهما بالإجماع لأنه لا يتضمن إبطال حق الزوج، وكذا في الخلع وعدة المسلم لو كانت كتابية، وكذا لو تزوجها قبل زوج آخر في المطلقة ثلاثاً .اهـ. وما ذكره المؤلف عن المحيط قال في النهر : هو الذي رأيته في تزوج الذمي الذمية وهي أولاً في من زوجها ولو كانت محرمة فرق بينهما.
They inherit from each other by agreement. And he explained in al-Tabyeen that inheritance is established by text contrary to analogy when the marital relationship is absolute by a valid marriage, so it is restricted to that. And he explained in al-Muhit that marriage to mahrams in the law of Adam was not established as a cause for entitlement to inheritance in his religion, so it does not become a cause for inheritance in their religions because their religion is not considered since it is not legally recognized. It may be said: Was marriage to mahrams in that law a cause for the obligation of maintenance? So the conclusion is that in marriage to mahrams, the judge separates them by the conversion of one spouse or by both litigating, not by the litigation of one according to Imam Abu Hanifa. But if no litigation occurs at all, then there is no separation by agreement, leaving them and what they practice. And in al-Tabyeen, this difference applies to the woman divorced three times and the combination of mahrams or five. And he mentioned in al-Muhit: If the dhimmi's wife is divorced three times and she requests separation, they are separated by consensus because this separation does not entail annulling a right of the husband because three divorces sever the marital bond in all religions. Then he mentioned after that that they are separated without litigation in cases: if he divorces her then stays with her without a contract, or divorces her three times then marries her before she marries another because it is zina, or he marries a kitabi woman during the idda of a Muslim to protect the lineage of the Muslim. So the summary: if he divorces her three times and keeps her without renewing the marriage contract, they are separated even if they do not come to the judge. And if he renews the contract and they both convert but litigate before us, they are not separated, maintaining that, and inheritance is established between them, maintenance is decreed, and his ihsan is not nullified such that the one who slanders him is hudud. And this is according to Abu Hanifa, contrary to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) in each of the four as in al-Muhit. And in Saki al-Anhar al-Tarabilsi: They do not inherit by a marriage they are not confirmed upon like marriage to mahrams, and this is correct. Then what we mentioned from al-Qahistani contradicts what the author related from al-Hidayah that if they litigate, they are separated by consensus. He said (then he mentioned after that that he separates) al-Zaylai said: And he mentioned in al-Ghayah attributing to al-Muhit that the woman divorced three times if she requests separation, they are separated by consensus because it does not entail annulling the husband's right. Similarly in khul' and the idda of a Muslim if she was kitabi. Similarly if he married her before another husband in the case of triple divorce. And what the author mentioned from al-Muhit said in al-Nahr: That is what I saw in al-Muhit al-Radawi and he quoted his statement then said: This contradicts what is in al-Ghayah regarding waiting for the request in khul' and similar. And based on the apparent meaning of al-Ghayah it was interpreted in al-Fath that if the dhimmi made khul' then kept her and she raised it to the judge, then he separates. I say: But what he quoted here from al-Muhit is problematic where he mentioned the woman divorced three times that they are separated if she requests, then he mentioned that they are separated if he marries her before another husband without restricting to her request. Its implication is that they are separated even if she does not request, and that they are separated if he did not marry her before another husband a fortiori because if he marries her after triple divorce there is a semblance of a marriage contract, unlike if he divorces her and stays with her without a contract. Hence al-Sabijabi distinguished between the two cases: he affirmed separation if he keeps her without renewing the contract, and denied it if he renewed it. This, and I saw in al-Kafi by al-Hakim al-Shahid the text: If a dhimmi divorces his wife three times then stays with her, and she litigates to the authority, they are separated. Similarly if she made khul' from him. And if a dhimmi marries a dhimmi woman who was first... and if she was a mahram, they are separated.
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage is a detailed Hanafi commentary on rulings concerning marriages of non-Muslims (dhimmis) that are considered invalid in Islam, specifically marriage to mahram (close relatives) and triple divorce without renewal. The author discusses inheritance, separation by the judge, and maintenance. The key point is that a marriage invalid in Islam does not create inheritance rights in Islamic law, even if it was valid in the dhimmi's previous religion. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two students (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding whether the judge should separate the couple if they come to court after one spouse converts. Abu Hanifa holds that separation requires the conversion of one spouse or both litigating, while the Sahibayn hold that mere conversion triggers separation. The text also covers cases where separation is automatic without litigation, such as triple divorce followed by cohabitation without a new contract. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is not explicitly stated, but the author notes that the correct view is that they do not inherit from invalid marriages.

Key Terms
نكاح المحارم (nikah al-maharim): Marriage to close blood relatives, which is invalid in Islam but may have been lawful in previous religions.
مرافعة (murafa'ah): Litigation; the act of bringing a case before a judge for resolution.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a dhimmi couple in a mahram marriage both convert to Islam, which of the following is true?
A) The marriage becomes valid because both are now Muslims.
B) The marriage remains invalid but they inherit from each other.
C) The marriage remains invalid and they do not inherit from each other.
D) The judge must separate them immediately.

Key Takeaway
A marriage that is invalid in Islam (like mahram) creates no inheritance, maintenance, or other legal rights, even if it was valid in the couple's previous religion.

Nikah — Marriage
advanced
15 min
Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Separating Dhimmis After Conversion in Invalid Marriages
Intra-Hanafi dispute on separation conditions for dhimmis in invalid marriages upon conversion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding when a judge must separate a dhimmi couple in an invalid marriage upon one spouse's conversion.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa requires litigation or conversion; Sahibayn require automatic separation upon conversion.

When one spouse in an invalid dhimmi marriage converts to Islam, the question arises: must the judge intervene to separate them? Abu Hanifa holds that separation only occurs if the couple comes to court or if one converts (but not automatically). The Sahibayn argue that conversion itself triggers mandatory separation. This lesson examines their proofs and the practical outcome, including exceptions where both agree on automatic separation (e.g., triple divorce).

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi dispute
Identify the two positions: Imam Abu Hanifa holds that separation requires either the conversion of one spouse or both parties going to court (litigation). The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that conversion alone suffices for mandatory separation.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, not a cross-madhhab dispute.
2
Examine the source of the dispute
The difference stems from how they view the legal effect of the previous religion. Abu Hanifa sees the marriage as legally irrelevant for Islamic courts unless the couple actively seeks adjudication. The Sahibayn prioritize preventing continued sin after conversion.
The core issue is whether the judge has a proactive duty to separate or only a reactive one.
3
Explore the exceptions where both agree on automatic separation
The text lists cases where separation is automatic without litigation: triple divorce followed by cohabitation without a new contract, khul' followed by cohabitation, marriage during a Muslim's idda, etc. In these, the marriage is so clearly invalid that action is required ex officio.
These exceptions highlight that the dispute is limited to cases where the invalidity is not self-evident to the court.
4
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
Under Abu Hanifa, if a dhimmi converts and his wife is his mahram, they remain together unless someone (the wife or the court) initiates action. Under the Sahibayn, the judge must proactively separate them upon learning of the conversion.
This affects whether the couple can continue living together and whether any children born after conversion are legitimate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

بالنفقة ولا يسقط إحصانه حتى بحد قاذفه ، وهذا عنده خلافاً لهما في كل من الأربعة كما في المحيط اهـ. وفي سكب الأنهر للطرابلسي: ولا يتوارثون بنكاح لا يقران عليه كنكاح المحارم وهذا هو الصحيح. ثم إن ما ذكرناه عن القهستاني يخالف ما نقله المؤلف عن الهداية من أنهما لو ترافعا يفرق بالإجماع قوله (ثم ذكر بعدها أنه يفرق قال الزيلعي : وذكر في الغاية معزياً إلى المحيط أن المطلقة ثلاثاً لو طلبت التفريق يفرق المحيط الرضوي وساق عبارته ثم قال : وهذا كما ترى يخالف ما في الغاية من التوقف على الطلب في الخلع ونحوه، وعلى ظاهر ما في الغاية فسر في الفتح الخلع بأن اختلعت الذمي ثم أمسكها فرفعته إلى الحاكم فإنه يفرق تزوج الذمي الذمية وهي أولاً في من زوجها ولو كانت محرمة فرق بينهما.
They inherit from each other by agreement. And he explained in al-Tabyeen that inheritance is established by text contrary to analogy when the marital relationship is absolute by a valid marriage, so it is restricted to that. And he explained in al-Muhit that marriage to mahrams in the law of Adam was not established as a cause for entitlement to inheritance in his religion, so it does not become a cause for inheritance in their religions because their religion is not considered since it is not legally recognized. It may be said: Was marriage to mahrams in that law a cause for the obligation of maintenance? So the conclusion is that in marriage to mahrams, the judge separates them by the conversion of one spouse or by both litigating, not by the litigation of one according to Imam Abu Hanifa. But if no litigation occurs at all, then there is no separation by agreement, leaving them and what they practice. And in al-Tabyeen, this difference applies to the woman divorced three times and the combination of mahrams or five. And he mentioned in al-Muhit: If the dhimmi's wife is divorced three times and she requests separation, they are separated by consensus because this separation does not entail annulling a right of the husband because three divorces sever the marital bond in all religions. Then he mentioned after that that they are separated without litigation in cases: if he divorces her then stays with her without a contract, or divorces her three times then marries her before she marries another because it is zina, or he marries a kitabi woman during the idda of a Muslim to protect the lineage of the Muslim. So the summary: if he divorces her three times and keeps her without renewing the marriage contract, they are separated even if they do not come to the judge. And if he renews the contract and they both convert but litigate before us, they are not separated, maintaining that, and inheritance is established between them, maintenance is decreed, and his ihsan is not nullified such that the one who slanders him is hudud. And this is according to Abu Hanifa, contrary to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) in each of the four as in al-Muhit. And in Saki al-Anhar al-Tarabilsi: They do not inherit by a marriage they are not confirmed upon like marriage to mahrams, and this is correct. Then what we mentioned from al-Qahistani contradicts what the author related from al-Hidayah that if they litigate, they are separated by consensus. He said (then he mentioned after that that he separates) al-Zaylai said: And he mentioned in al-Ghayah attributing to al-Muhit that the woman divorced three times if she requests separation, they are separated by consensus because it does not entail annulling the husband's right. Similarly in khul' and the idda of a Muslim if she was kitabi. Similarly if he married her before another husband in the case of triple divorce. And what the author mentioned from al-Muhit said in al-Nahr: That is what I saw in al-Muhit al-Radawi and he quoted his statement then said: This contradicts what is in al-Ghayah regarding waiting for the request in khul' and similar. And based on the apparent meaning of al-Ghayah it was interpreted in al-Fath that if the dhimmi made khul' then kept her and she raised it to the judge, then he separates. I say: But what he quoted here from al-Muhit is problematic where he mentioned the woman divorced three times that they are separated if she requests, then he mentioned that they are separated if he marries her before another husband without restricting to her request. Its implication is that they are separated even if she does not request, and that they are separated if he did not marry her before another husband a fortiori because if he marries her after triple divorce there is a semblance of a marriage contract, unlike if he divorces her and stays with her without a contract. Hence al-Sabijabi distinguished between the two cases: he affirmed separation if he keeps her without renewing the contract, and denied it if he renewed it. This, and I saw in al-Kafi by al-Hakim al-Shahid the text: If a dhimmi divorces his wife three times then stays with her, and she litigates to the authority, they are separated. Similarly if she made khul' from him. And if a dhimmi marries a dhimmi woman who was first... and if she was a mahram, they are separated.
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage is a detailed Hanafi commentary on rulings concerning marriages of non-Muslims (dhimmis) that are considered invalid in Islam, specifically marriage to mahram (close relatives) and triple divorce without renewal. The author discusses inheritance, separation by the judge, and maintenance. The key point is that a marriage invalid in Islam does not create inheritance rights in Islamic law, even if it was valid in the dhimmi's previous religion. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two students (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding whether the judge should separate the couple if they come to court after one spouse converts. Abu Hanifa holds that separation requires the conversion of one spouse or both litigating, while the Sahibayn hold that mere conversion triggers separation. The text also covers cases where separation is automatic without litigation, such as triple divorce followed by cohabitation without a new contract. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is not explicitly stated, but the author notes that the correct view is that they do not inherit from invalid marriages.

Key Terms
نكاح المحارم (nikah al-maharim): Marriage to close blood relatives, which is invalid in Islam but may have been lawful in previous religions.
مرافعة (murafa'ah): Litigation; the act of bringing a case before a judge for resolution.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, when does a judge separate a dhimmi couple in an invalid mahram marriage after one spouse converts?
A) Immediately upon learning of the conversion.
B) Only if both spouses come to court to litigate.
C) Only if one spouse converts or if both litigate.
D) Only if the non-converting spouse complains.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa conditions separation in invalid dhimmi marriages on litigation or conversion; the Sahibayn require automatic separation upon conversion.

Nikah — Marriage
beginner

Prohibition of Marriage for Apostates
Explains prohibition of marriage for apostates with reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on apostate marriage and explain the rationale (talil) based on the apostate's deserving execution.

Core Idea
An apostate cannot marry because marriage distracts from the reflection needed for repentance before execution.

This lesson examines why a male or female apostate is forbidden from marrying according to the Hanafi school. The ruling is based on the apostate's legal status as deserving execution, which precludes the distractions of marriage. The passage also distinguishes the apostate from a person subject to qisas, where marriage is permissible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subjects
The text addresses both male and female apostates. What are the rules applied to each?
2
Understand the rationale for the male apostate
He is deserving of execution, respite is for reflection, marriage distracts. The male apostate is forbidden because his primary obligation is to repent, not marry.
3
Contrast with the person subject to qisas
Why can a qisas offender marry? Because forgiveness is recommended, potentially saving him. This contrast clarifies the apostate's unique prohibition.
4
Understand the rationale for the female apostate
She is imprisoned for reflection, serving a husband would distract. Also, the interests of marriage are not realized. The prohibition is symmetrical.
5
Determine the scope of the prohibition
The text uses 'one' to indicate generality: the apostate cannot marry any woman (Muslim, kitabi, or apostate). The ruling is absolute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ولا ينكح مرتد أو مرتدة أحدا أما المرتد فلأنه مستحق القتل، والإمهال ضرورة التأمل، والنكاح يشغله عنه فلا يشرع في حقه، ولا يرد مستحق القتل للقصاص حيث يجوز له التزوج مع أنه يقتل لأن العفو مندوب إليه فيه فيسلم منه بخلاف المرتد لأنه لا غالباً، وأما المرتدة فلأنها محبوسة للتأمل وخدمة الزوج تشغلها عنه، ولأنه لا ينتظم بينهما المصالح والنكاح ما شرع لعينه بل لمصالحه. وعبر بأحد في سياق النفي ليفيد العموم فلا يتزوج المرتد مسلمة ولا كتابية ولا مرتدة ولا يتزوج المرتدة مسلم ولا كافر ولا مرتد قوله : (والولد يتبع خير الأبوين (دينا لأنه أنظر له فإن كان الزوج مسلماً فالولد على دينه، وكذا إن أسلم أحدهما وله ولد صغير صار ولده مسلماً بإسلامه، سواء كان الأب أو الأم. في عدة من زوج مسلم قد طلقها أو مات عنها فإني أفرق بينهما .اهـ. قلت: وهذا مثل ما عزاه في الغاية إلى المحيط من التوقف على الطلب في الطلاق ثلاثاً بدون تجديد العقد وفي الخلع، لكن مفاده أن في التزوج في عدة المسلم لا يحتاج إلى طلب ومرافعة أصلاً وهو ظاهر، ومثله ما لو تزوج الذمي مسلمة حرة أو أمة فقد صرح الحاكم بأنه يفرق بينهما ويوجع عقوبة إن دخل بها ويعزر من زوجه وتعزر المرأة، وإن أسلم بعد النكاح لم يترك على نكاحه قوله (وهو مخالف لما في المحيط) أي ما ذكره من الحاصل عن الأسبيجابي مخالف لكلام المحيط السابق لأنه جعل التفريق فيما إذا طلقها ثلاثاً ثم تزوجها قبل التزوج بآخر، وصريح كلام الأسبيجابي أنه لا تفريق في هذه الصورة وإنما هو فيما إذا أمسكها من غير تجديد النكاح. وقول المؤلف لأنه سوى الخ أي صاحب المحيط حكم بالتفريق فيما إذا لم تتزوج بغيره سواء عقد عليها أم لا قوله ( وفي فتح القدير وينبغي الخ) قال في النهر : لا يخفى أن مجرد وقوع العقد فاسداً لا أثر له في وجوب التفرقة وإلا لفرق في النكاح بلا شهود بل لا بد من قيام المنافي مع البقاء كالمحرمية وهو هنا قد زال فما في النهاية أوجه قوله (صار ولده مسلماً بإسلامه) قال الرملي : أطلقه فشمل المميز وغيره وقد قال في التتارخانية نقلاً عن الذخيرة بعض المشايخ . قالوا : إنما يصير مسلماً تبعاً لأحد أبوية إذا كان لا يعبر عن نفسه فأما إذا كان يعبر عن نفسه لا مسلماً يصير بإسلام أحد أبويه، وإليه أشار محمد وبعضهم قالوا يصير مسلماً بإسلام أحد أبويه وإن كان يعبر عن نفسه، واستدل هذا القائل بما ذكر محمد أن المستأمن في دارنا إذا أسلم وله ولد صغير في دار الحرب فخرج إلى دار الإسلام لزيارة أبيه بأمان وهو ممن يعبر عن نفسه ثم أراد أن
His statement: (And an apostate male or female does not marry anyone. As for the male apostate, because he is deserving of execution, and respite is necessary for reflection, and marriage would occupy him from it, so it is not legislated for him. And one deserving of execution for qisas is not prevented because it is permissible for him to marry even though he will be killed, because forgiveness is recommended in that case, so he may be saved from execution, unlike the apostate because he is not generally forgiven. As for the female apostate, because she is imprisoned for reflection and serving the husband would occupy her from it, and because the interests (of marriage) are not established between them, and marriage was not legislated for its own sake but for its interests. And he used 'one' in the context of negation to indicate generality, so the male apostate does not marry a Muslim woman, nor a kitabi woman, nor an apostate, and the female apostate is not married by a Muslim, nor a disbeliever, nor an apostate. His statement: (And the child follows the better of the two parents in religion because it is more beneficial for him. If the husband is Muslim, the child follows his religion. Similarly, if one of them converts and he has a young child, his child becomes Muslim by his conversion, whether it is the father or the mother. In the idda of a Muslim husband who has divorced her or died, I separate them. He said: This is similar to what he attributed in al-Ghayah to al-Muhit regarding waiting for the request in triple divorce without renewing the contract and in khul'. But its implication is that in marrying during the idda of a Muslim, there is no need for request or litigation at all, and this is apparent. Similarly, if a dhimmi marries a free Muslim woman or a slave, the judge explicitly ruled that they are separated, and he is punished if he entered, and the one who married her is reprimanded, and the woman is reprimanded. And if he converts after the marriage, he is not left on his marriage. His statement (And it is contrary to what is in al-Muhit) means that what he mentioned as the summary from al-Sabijabi contradicts the previous words of al-Muhit because he made separation in the case where he divorced her three times then married her before another husband, and the explicit words of al-Sabijabi are that there is no separation in this situation, rather it is when he kept her without renewing the marriage. And the author's statement 'because he equaled' etc. i.e. the author of al-Muhit ruled separation whether he contracted or not. His statement (And in Fath al-Qadir it is better etc.) said in al-Nahr: It is not hidden that mere occurrence of a void contract has no effect in obligating separation, otherwise they would separate in marriage without witnesses. Rather there must be a hindrance that remains, like kinship, and here it has ceased, so what is in al-Nihayah is more appropriate. His statement (His child becomes Muslim by his conversion) al-Ramli said: He made it absolute, including the discerning and other. And it has been said in al-Tatarkhaniyah quoting from al-Dhakhirah some mashayikh: They said: He only becomes Muslim following one of his parents if he is not able to express himself. But if he is able to express himself, he does not become Muslim by the conversion of one of his parents. Muhammad pointed to this. And some said: He becomes Muslim by the conversion of one of his parents even if he can express himself. And this proponent argued with what Muhammad mentioned that if a musta'min in our land converts and he has a young child in dar al-harb, and he comes to dar al-islam to visit his father under aman and he is one who can express himself, then he wanted to...

Commentary
This passage covers two rulings: first, the prohibition of marriage for apostates, and second, the rule that a minor child follows the better religion of the parents. The author explains that a male apostate is prohibited from marrying because he is subject to execution for apostasy, and marriage would distract him from reflection. The female apostate is also prevented because she is imprisoned for reflection and marriage would hinder that. The text then states the general rule that a child follows the better religion of the parents, meaning the parent with the most beneficial religion (i.e., Islam). The author then discusses a related point: if a dhimmi marries a Muslim woman, the judge must separate them. The passage includes a discussion of the child of discernment (tamyiz) and whether such a child becomes Muslim automatically when one parent converts. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute on this point: some scholars hold that a child who can express himself does not automatically become Muslim by conversion of a parent, while others say he does. The relied-upon position is not definitively stated, but the text notes that Imam Muhammad's example of the musta'min child supports the view that even a discerning child becomes Muslim.

Key Terms
مرتد (murtadd): A person who has renounced Islam after having been Muslim; subject to execution in Islamic law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is a male apostate prohibited from marrying?
A) Because he is considered a non-Muslim.
B) Because marriage distracts from reflection needed for repentance.
C) Because he cannot pay the dower.
D) Because he is imprisoned.

Key Takeaway
Apostate marriage is absolutely prohibited because it obstructs the apostate's primary obligation to reflect and repent.
