The Child Follows the Better Religion
Explains rule of child following better religion and intra-Hanafi dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that a minor child follows the better religion of the parents and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a discerning child (mumayyiz).

Core Idea
A minor child becomes Muslim automatically if one parent converts, but later Hanafi scholars differ on whether a discerning child is included.

This lesson explores the Hanafi rule that a child follows the religion of the parent with the better faith (i.e., Islam). The passage establishes the base rule and then discusses a controversy among later scholars: does a child who has reached the age of discernment (tamyiz) automatically become Muslim when one parent converts, or does he retain his previous religion until he chooses? The text presents both views without definitive tarjih.

Guided Path
1
State the base rule
The child follows the better religion (Islam) of the parents.
2
Apply to conversion of one parent
If one parent converts, the minor child automatically becomes Muslim.
3
Identify the dispute about discerning child
Some scholars (e.g., from al-Dhakhira) say a child who can express himself does not automatically convert; others say he does.
4
Examine the evidence from Imam Muhammad
The text mentions a musta'min child example that supports the latter view.
5
Determine the practical consequence
The child's religion affects inheritance, marriage, etc. The lack of clear tarjih leaves room for fatwa variation.

Source Text

قوله: (ولا ينكح مرتد أو مرتدة أحدا أما المرتد فلأنه مستحق القتل، والإمهال ضرورة التأمل، والنكاح يشغله عنه فلا يشرع في حقه، ولا يرد مستحق القتل للقصاص حيث يجوز له التزوج مع أنه يقتل لأن العفو مندوب وله ولد صغير صار ولده مسلماً بإسلامه، سواء كان الأب أو الأم. في عدة من زوج مسلم قد طلقها أو مات عنها فإني أفرق بينهما .اهـ. قلت: وهذا مثل ما عزاه في الغاية إلى المحيط من التوقف على الطلب في الطلاق ثلاثاً بدون تجديد العقد وفي الخلع، لكن مفاده أن في التزوج في عدة المسلم لا يحتاج إلى طلب ومرافعة أصلاً وهو ظاهر، ومثله ما لو تزوج الذمي مسلمة حرة أو أمة فقد صرح الحاكم بأنه يفرق بينهما ويوجع عقوبة إن دخل بها ويعزر من زوجه وتعزر المرأة، وإن أسلم بعد النكاح لم يترك على نكاحه قوله (وهو مخالف لما في المحيط) أي ما ذكره من الحاصل عن الأسبيجابي مخالف لكلام المحيط السابق لأنه جعل التفريق فيما إذا طلقها ثلاثاً ثم تزوجها قبل التزوج بآخر، وصريح كلام الأسبيجابي أنه لا تفريق في هذه الصورة وإنما هو فيما إذا أمسكها من غير تجديد النكاح. وقول المؤلف لأنه سوى الخ أي صاحب المحيط حكم بالتفريق فيما إذا لم تتزوج لأحد أبوية إذا كان لا يعبر عن نفسه فأما إذا كان يعبر عن نفسه لا مسلماً يصير بإسلام أحد أبويه، وإليه أشار محمد وبعضهم قالوا يصير مسلماً بإسلام أحد أبويه وإن كان يعبر عن نفسه، واستدل هذا القائل بما ذكر محمد أن المستأمن في دارنا إذا أسلم وله ولد صغير في دار الحرب فخرج إلى دار الإسلام لزيارة أبيه بأمان وهو ممن يعبر عن نفسه ثم أراد أن
His statement: (And an apostate male or female does not marry anyone. As for the male apostate, because he is deserving of execution, and respite is necessary for reflection, and marriage would occupy him from it, so it is not legislated for him. And one deserving of execution for qisas is not prevented because it is permissible for him to marry even though he will be killed, because forgiveness is recommended in that case, so he may be saved from execution, unlike the apostate because he is not generally forgiven. As for the female apostate, because she is imprisoned for reflection and serving the husband would occupy her from it, and because the interests (of marriage) are not established between them, and marriage was not legislated for its own sake but for its interests. And he used 'one' in the context of negation to indicate generality, so the male apostate does not marry a Muslim woman, nor a kitabi woman, nor an apostate, and the female apostate is not married by a Muslim, nor a disbeliever, nor an apostate. His statement: (And the child follows the better of the two parents in religion because it is more beneficial for him. If the husband is Muslim, the child follows his religion. Similarly, if one of them converts and he has a young child, his child becomes Muslim by his conversion, whether it is the father or the mother. In the idda of a Muslim husband who has divorced her or died, I separate them. He said: This is similar to what he attributed in al-Ghayah to al-Muhit regarding waiting for the request in triple divorce without renewing the contract and in khul'. But its implication is that in marrying during the idda of a Muslim, there is no need for request or litigation at all, and this is apparent. Similarly, if a dhimmi marries a free Muslim woman or a slave, the judge explicitly ruled that they are separated, and he is punished if he entered, and the one who married her is reprimanded, and the woman is reprimanded. And if he converts after the marriage, he is not left on his marriage. His statement (And it is contrary to what is in al-Muhit) means that what he mentioned as the summary from al-Sabijabi contradicts the previous words of al-Muhit because he made separation in the case where he divorced her three times then married her before another husband, and the explicit words of al-Sabijabi are that there is no separation in this situation, rather it is when he kept her without renewing the marriage. And the author's statement 'because he equaled' etc. i.e. the author of al-Muhit ruled separation whether he contracted or not. His statement (And in Fath al-Qadir it is better etc.) said in al-Nahr: It is not hidden that mere occurrence of a void contract has no effect in obligating separation, otherwise they would separate in marriage without witnesses. Rather there must be a hindrance that remains, like kinship, and here it has ceased, so what is in al-Nihayah is more appropriate. His statement (His child becomes Muslim by his conversion) al-Ramli said: He made it absolute, including the discerning and other. And it has been said in al-Tatarkhaniyah quoting from al-Dhakhirah some mashayikh: They said: He only becomes Muslim following one of his parents if he is not able to express himself. But if he is able to express himself, he does not become Muslim by the conversion of one of his parents. Muhammad pointed to this. And some said: He becomes Muslim by the conversion of one of his parents even if he can express himself. And this proponent argued with what Muhammad mentioned that if a musta'min in our land converts and he has a young child in dar al-harb, and he comes to dar al-islam to visit his father under aman and he is one who can express himself, then he wanted to...
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage covers two rulings: first, the prohibition of marriage for apostates, and second, the rule that a minor child follows the better religion of the parents. The author explains that a male apostate is prohibited from marrying because he is subject to execution for apostasy, and marriage would distract him from reflection. The female apostate is also prevented because she is imprisoned for reflection and marriage would hinder that. The text then states the general rule that a child follows the better religion of the parents, meaning the parent with the most beneficial religion (i.e., Islam). The author then discusses a related point: if a dhimmi marries a Muslim woman, the judge must separate them. The passage includes a discussion of the child of discernment (tamyiz) and whether such a child becomes Muslim automatically when one parent converts. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute on this point: some scholars hold that a child who can express himself does not automatically become Muslim by conversion of a parent, while others say he does. The relied-upon position is not definitively stated, but the text notes that Imam Muhammad's example of the musta'min child supports the view that even a discerning child becomes Muslim.

Key Terms
الولد يتبع خير الأبوين ديناً (al-walad yattabi'u khayr al-abawayn dinan): A minor child follows the religion of the parent that is better (i.e., Islam) when one is Muslim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He automatically becomes Muslim.
B) He remains on his previous religion.
C) There is a disagreement among Hanafi scholars.
D) He must be consulted and his choice is decisive.

Key Takeaway
The rule that a child follows the better religion is absolute for young children, but for discerning children, there is an undecided dispute within the Hanafi school.

Apostate Marriage Prohibition in Hanafi Fiqh
Matn and its sharh (expansion) with rational proof and a contrastive analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that an apostate cannot marry anyone and explain the rational proof (istihsan) based on distraction from repentance.

Core Idea
An apostate is totally prohibited from marriage to anyone, as it distracts from the essential reflection for repentance.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, the author explains a key ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq: an apostate (male or female) cannot contract a valid marriage with anyone. The ruling is not based on a direct text but on rational reasoning (ijtihad bi al-ra'y). The commentator provides a subtle contrast with a person sentenced to death for qisas, who may marry because forgiveness is possible. Understanding this ruling requires grasping the legal 'illa—that marriage distracts from the obligatory istitabah (invitation to repent).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
Recognize that the act is the marriage contract performed by an apostate (male or female). The ruling is that it is prohibited (haram) and the contract is invalid (batil).
2
Understand the proof
The proof is not a direct Qur'anic verse or hadith but istihsan (juristic preference) based on the purpose of postponing execution—to allow reflection (tadabbur). Marriage distracts from this critical task.
3
Locate the 'illa (effective cause)
The effective cause (illa) is that marriage distracts the apostate from the essential reflection required during the three-day period before execution. This prevents the maqsad (objective) of istitabah.
4
Contrast with qisas case
The qisas convict is allowed to marry because forgiveness (afw) is recommended in his case, so marriage may still produce benefit. The apostate is not often forgiven, so no benefit is expected.
5
Determine the scope
The word 'aḥad' (anyone) in the matn indicates universality: a male apostate cannot marry a Muslim woman, a Kitabi woman, or a female apostate. Similarly, a female apostate cannot be married by a Muslim, a disbeliever, or a male apostate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا ينكح مرتد أو مرتدة أحداً

قوله: (ولا ينكح مرتد أو مرتدة أحدا أما المرتد فلأنه مستحق القتل، والإمهال ضرورة التأمل، والنكاح يشغله عنه فلا يشرع في حقه، ولا يرد مستحق القتل للقصاص حيث يجوز له التزوج مع أنه يقتل لأن العفو مندوب إليه فيه فيسلم منه بخلاف المرتد لأنه لا غالباً، وأما المرتدة فلأنها محبوسة للتأمل وخدمة الزوج تشغلها عنه، ولأنه لا ينتظم بينهما المصالح والنكاح ما شرع لعينه بل لمصالحه. وعبر بأحد في سياق النفي ليفيد العموم فلا يتزوج المرتد مسلمة ولا كتابية ولا مرتدة ولا يتزوج المرتدة مسلم ولا كافر ولا مرتد
And an apostate male or female shall not marry anyone. He said: (And an apostate male or female shall not marry anyone) As for the male apostate, because he is deserving of execution, and postponement is necessary for reflection, and marriage distracts him from it, so it is not legislated for him. And the one deserving of execution for retaliation is not prevented from marrying, because forgiveness is recommended in it, so he may be saved, unlike the apostate because he is not often (saved). As for the female apostate, because she is imprisoned for reflection, and serving a husband distracts her from it, and because the interests cannot be established between them, and marriage is not prescribed for itself but for its interests. And he expressed 'anyone' in a negative context to indicate generality, so the apostate male does not marry a Muslim woman, nor a woman of the Book, nor an apostate woman, and the apostate woman is not married by a Muslim, nor a disbeliever, nor an apostate.
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement followed by its rational proof. The matn from Kanz al-Daqa'iq states that an apostate (male or female) is prohibited from contracting marriage with anyone. The sharh (expansion) provides the reasoning: The male apostate is under sentence of death for apostasy, and the postponement of execution is only to allow him time to repent and return to Islam. Marriage would distract him from this essential reflection. The commentator contrasts this with someone who is sentenced to death for qisas (retaliation); such a person is still allowed to marry because the victim's family may waive the right to execution (forgiveness is recommended), so marriage could still have benefit. In contrast, forgiveness for apostasy is rare (since the apostate rarely returns to Islam). As for the female apostate, she is imprisoned for the same purpose of reflection, and marriage would distract her from it. Moreover, the essential interests of marriage (like mutual love and procreation) cannot be soundly established due to the difference in religion and the instability of the situation. The commentator also clarifies the word 'anyone' (aḥad) in the matn: it indicates universality, so a male apostate cannot marry a Muslim woman, a Christian/Jewish woman, or a female apostate. Similarly, a female apostate cannot be married by a Muslim, a disbeliever (even a book-person), or a male apostate. This shows the complete prohibition.

Key Terms
مرتد (murtadd): A person who has renounced Islam and is legally liable for execution after being given the chance to repent.
مرتدة (murtaddah): Female apostate; same ruling as male but imprisoned for reflection, not killed immediately? Actually execution applies to both after istitabah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a male apostate prohibited from marrying while a person sentenced to qisas is allowed?
A) Because the apostate has no religion.
B) Because marriage distracts from the required reflection (tadabbur).
C) Because the apostate is considered dead.
D) Because the apostate's marriage would not be recognized by Muslims.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prohibits an apostate from marrying anyone based on the rationale that marriage distracts from the essential reflection for repentance; this ruling is universal and applies regardless of the apostate's potential repentance.

The Child Follows the Better Parent in Religion
Matn and sharh on the child's religion following the parent with the better faith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that a child follows the better parent in religion and apply it to scenarios involving parental conversion, including the dispute regarding mumayyiz children.

Core Idea
A child's religion follows the parent with the superior faith — Islam — automatically, even if only one parent converts.

This lesson examines a key Hanafi principle regarding the religious status of minor children when parents are of different faiths or when one or both parents convert. The rule 'the child follows the better of the two parents in religion' establishes that Islam prevails, and the child is considered Muslim if either parent is or becomes Muslim. We will explore the legal basis, the conditions, and an intra-Hanafi disagreement about children who have reached the age of discretion (mumayyiz).

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
State the rule: the child follows the better parent in religion. Understand that 'better' means superior, which is Islam. This applies regardless of which parent is Muslim.
2
Apply to Conversion Scenarios
Consider cases where one parent converts to Islam. The child becomes Muslim immediately, whether the converter is the father or mother, as long as the child is a minor.
The child's abode (dar al-Islam vs dar al-harb) matters, but the general rule holds.
3
Analyze the Mumayyiz Dispute
Some Hanafi scholars (e.g., al-Sarakhsi) argue that a child who can distinguish (mumayyiz) does not automatically follow the parent but should choose. The majority holds that the child follows regardless.
The mufta bihi is the majority view: the child follows the parent even if mumayyiz.
4
Note the Effect of Puberty
Once a child becomes Muslim by following a parent and reaches puberty, there is no need to renew the faith. The previous Islam was valid even without the child's intention.
This highlights the principle of tabi'iyya (subordination) in Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والولد يتبع خير الأبوين دينا

قوله: (والولد يتبع خير الأبوين دينا لأنه أنظر له فإن كان الزوج مسلماً فالولد على دينه، وكذا إن أسلم أحدهما وله ولد صغير صار ولده مسلماً بإسلامه، سواء كان الأب أو الأم. ... كذا لو أسلمت ... وتتصور تبعيته لأمه المسلمة وأبوه كافر بأن كانا كافرين فأسلمت فقبل عرض الإسلام عليه ولدت كما في المعراج وفي التبيين وهذا إذا لم تختلف الدار ... ثم اعلم أنه إذا صار مسلماً بالتبعية ثم بلغ فإنه لا يلزمه تجديد الإيمان لوقوعه فرضاً ... وتمامه في فتح القدير من باب المرتدين
And the child follows the better of the two parents in religion. He said: (And the child follows the better of the two parents in religion) because it is more favorable for him. If the husband is Muslim, then the child follows his religion. Likewise, if one of them becomes Muslim and he has a small child, his child becomes Muslim by his Islam, whether it is the father or the mother. ... Similarly if she becomes Muslim ... And his following his Muslim mother is conceivable while his father is a disbeliever, such as if both were disbelievers and she became Muslim, then before the presentation of Islam to him she gave birth, as in al-Mi'raj and al-Tabyin. This is if the abodes are not different. ... Then know that if he becomes Muslim by following (a parent) and then reaches puberty, he is not required to renew faith because it occurred as an obligation... And its completion is in Fath al-Qadir from the chapter of apostates.
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage states the principle that a child follows the parent with the better (i.e., superior, meaning Islamic) religion. If one parent is Muslim, the child is Muslim. The commentary discusses that if a disbelieving couple has a child and then the mother converts to Islam, the child becomes Muslim by following her, provided the child is not yet capable of independent choice (mumayyiz). There is detail about the child's abode (dar al-Islam vs dar al-harb) affecting this ruling. The commentator also notes that once a child becomes Muslim by following a parent, upon reaching adulthood he does not need to renew his faith, because the previous Islam was valid even if he did not intend it. Different Hanafi scholars have opinions on whether a mumayyiz child can choose his own religion; the dominant view is that he follows the parent, but some say if he can distinguish, he does not automatically follow. The text refers to Fath al-Qadir for further detail.

Key Terms
الولد يتبع خير الأبوين (al-walad yatba'u khayr al-abawayn): Rule that a minor child's religion follows the parent with the superior religion (i.e., Islam).
مميز (mumayyiz): A child who has reached an age of discretion (usually around 7-10) and can understand basic matters of religion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority Hanafi position, what happens to a minor child's religion when one parent converts to Islam?
A) The child remains in the original religion until puberty.
B) The child becomes Muslim immediately, even if the child can distinguish (mumayyiz).
C) The child becomes Muslim only if the father converts.
D) The child must choose upon reaching the age of discretion.

Key Takeaway
The child follows the better parent in religion, meaning Islam, automatically and without need to renew faith upon maturity.

Magians Are Worse Than People of the Book: A Definitional Ruling
Ruling on the status of Magians compared to People of the Book

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the status of Magians compared to the People of the Book, and apply the rule to determine the status of a child of mixed parentage.

Core Idea
A Magian is worse than a Jew or Christian because their meat and women are not permissible.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a comparative ruling between Magians (Zoroastrians) and the People of the Book (Jews and Christians). The Hanafi school distinguishes them based on the claim of a revealed scripture. The ruling has direct practical consequences for marriage and dietary laws.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two groups
The passage compares two categories: Majusi (Magian) and Kitabi (People of the Book). Identify the defining characteristic of each in this context.
Magians follow Zoroastrianism, which is not considered a revealed religion in Islamic law.
2
Understand the basis for distinction
The Kitabi claims a heavenly religion (din samawi). This claim permits eating their slaughtered meat and marrying their women. The Magian lacks this claim, so those permissions do not apply.
The distinction is not about monotheism vs. polytheism per se, but about recognition of a revealed scripture.
3
Apply the rule to a mixed parentage case
If a child is born to a Kitabi father and a Magian mother, the child is considered Kitabi. This is because there is consideration (nazar) for the child even in the Hereafter with reduced punishment.
The child follows the better lineage according to the principle of tasarruf (favoring the better status).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمجوسي شر من الكتابي لأن للكتابي ديناً سماوياً بحسب الدعوى ولهذا تؤكل ذبيحته وتجوز مناكحة الكتابية بخلاف المجوسي فكان شراً منه حتى إذا ولد ولد بين كتابي ومجوسي فهو كتابي لأن فيه نوع نظر له حتى في الآخرة بنقصان العقاب كما في فتح الأئمة
And the Magian is worse than the People of the Book because the People of the Book have a heavenly religion by claim, and therefore their slaughtered meat may be eaten and marrying a woman of the Book is permissible, unlike the Magian, so he is worse than him, so if a child is born between a father from the People of the Book and a Magian, he is considered from the People of the Book because there is a kind of consideration for him even in the Hereafter with a reduction of punishment, as in Fath al-A'imma.
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage provides a comparative ruling between the Magians (مجوس – Zoroastrians) and the People of the Book (Jews and Christians). The matn states the Magian is worse (sharr) than a Jew or Christian. The reasoning: People of the Book claim to follow a divinely revealed scripture (al-din al-samawi), and because of this claim, Islamic law permits eating the meat of their slaughtered animals (dhabiha) and marrying their women (mankiha). In contrast, Magians do not have a revealed scripture, so their slaughtered meat is not permissible, and Muslim men cannot marry Magian women. Therefore, they are considered worse. The text also mentions a practical implication: if a child is born from a Jewish/Christian father and a Magian mother, the child is considered a member of the People of the Book (i.e., Christian or Jew) because there is some consideration (nazar) for him even in the afterlife by reduction of punishment (since he follows the better religion line). This is referenced from Fath al-A'imma.

Key Terms
مجوسي (majusi): A Zoroastrian; a follower of a dualistic religion with fire worship, considered not to have a revealed scripture.
كتابي (kitabi): A member of the People of the Book (Jew or Christian), who believe in a revealed scripture.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a Magian (Zoroastrian) considered worse than a Jew or Christian in Islamic law?
A) Because Magians are polytheists and Jews/Christians are monotheists.
B) Because the People of the Book claim a heavenly scripture, so their slaughtered meat and women are permissible, unlike Magians.
C) Because Magians are fire-worshippers, which is a greater sin.
D) Because Magians do not believe in any prophets.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, Magians are not considered People of the Book, so their slaughtered meat is not halal and marriage to them is not permitted; a child of a Kitabi father and Magian mother is considered Kitabi.

10 min
Child's Religion When One Parent Converts to Islam
Ruling on child following better religion when one spouse converts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on a child's religion when a parent converts, and state whether renewal of faith is required at puberty.

Core Idea
A child who becomes Muslim by following a converting parent does not need to renew faith upon puberty.

When one spouse converts to Islam, Islam is presented to the other. But what about the child? The Hanafi school has detailed rules on whether the child follows the Muslim parent and under what conditions. The passage from al-Bahr al-Ra’iq also addresses a key question: if the child becomes Muslim through this parent-child relationship, does the child need to renew faith upon reaching puberty? The answer, based on Hanafi authority, is no.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the rule of 'ard al-Islam
Read the passage's initial ruling: when one spouse converts, the other is presented with Islam. This sets the context for the child's status.
2
Determine the child's religion through tayyibah
The passage explains that a child can follow the Muslim mother if she converted before birth, provided the child is in the same dar (territory). Analyze the three dar scenarios given.
The child follows the better religion (Islam) if conditions are met.
3
Trace the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on obligation
The passage mentions three Hanafi authorities (al-Maturidi, Fakhr al-Islam, Shams al-A'imma) who differ on whether the sane minor is originally obligated to perform faith. Understand each view.
Despite difference, all agree that if the child performed faith as a minor, it is valid and no renewal is needed.
4
State the mufta bihi (relied-upon position)
Conclude that the child does not need to renew faith upon puberty. This is the ruling adopted in Fath al-Qadir and the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا أسلم أحد الزوجين عرض الإسلام على الآخر فإن وتتصور تبعيته لأمه المسلمة وأبوه كافر بأن كانا كافرين فأسلمت فقبل عرض الإسلام عليه ولدت كما في المعراج وفي التبيين وهذا إذا لم تختلف الدار بأن كانا في دار الإسلام أو في دار الحرب أو كان الصغير في دار الإسلام وأسلم الوالد في دار الحرب لأنه من أهل دار الإسلام حكماً، فأما إذا كان الولد في دار الحرب والوالد في دار الإسلام فأسلم لا يتبعه ولده ولا يكون مسلماً بإسلامه لأنه لا يمكن أن يجعل الوالد من أهل دار الحرب بخلاف العكس ا هـ . وفي فتح القدير: أما لو تباينت دارهما بأن كان الوالد في دار الإسلام والولد في دار الحرب أو على العكس فإنه لا يصير مسلماً بإسلام الأب ا وهو سهو فاجتنبه. ثم اعلم أنه إذا صار مسلماً بالتبعية ثم بلغ فإنه لا يلزمه تجديد الإيمان لوقوعه فرضاً، أما على قول الماتريدي فظاهر لأنه قائل بوجوب أداء الإيمان على الصبي العاقل كما في التحرير، وأما على قول فخر الإسلام فظاهر أيضاً لأنه قائل بأصل الوجوب عليه وإن لم يجب أداؤه، فإذا أداه وقع فرضاً كتعجيل الزكاة قبل الحول وأما على قول شمس الأئمة فكذلك وإن قال بعدم أصل الوجوب عليه لأنه إنما قال به للترفية عليه فإذا وجد منه وجد الوجوب كالمسافر إذا صلى الجمعة، ولا خلاف لأحد في عدم وجوب نية الفرض عليه بعد بلوغه، وتمامه في فتح القدير من باب المرتدين .
And if one of the two spouses converts to Islam, Islam is presented to the other. And his following of his Muslim mother while his father is a disbeliever is conceivable, if both were disbelievers and she converted before giving birth, as in al-Mi'raj and al-Tabyin. This is if the dar is not different, i.e., they are in Dar al-Islam or Dar al-Harb, or the child is in Dar al-Islam and the father converted in Dar al-Harb, because he is considered from Dar al-Islam legally. But if the child is in Dar al-Harb and the father in Dar al-Islam and he converts, the child does not follow him and is not Muslim by his conversion, because the father cannot be considered from Dar al-Harb, unlike the opposite. In Fath al-Qadir: If their dar differs, such as father in Dar al-Islam and child in Dar al-Harb or vice versa, the child does not become Muslim by the father's conversion. That is an error, avoid it. Then know that if he becomes Muslim by following [a parent] and then reaches puberty, he is not required to renew his faith because it occurred as an obligation. According to al-Maturidi, this is apparent because he holds that the sane child is obligated to perform faith. According to Fakhr al-Islam, it is also apparent because he holds the original obligation upon him even if performance is not obligatory, so when he performs it it becomes obligatory like advancing zakat before the year. According to Shams al-A'imma, similarly, although he denies the original obligation because he holds it for leniency, so if it occurs, obligation occurs like a traveler praying Friday. There is no disagreement on the non-obligation of intending obligation after puberty. The rest is in Fath al-Qadir in the chapter of apostates.
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra’iq discusses the child's religion when one parent converts to Islam. It begins with the ruling that Islam is presented to the non-Muslim spouse. Then it discusses the scenario where the mother is Muslim and father is disbeliever, clarifying that the child can follow the mother if she converted before birth. A key condition is whether the child is in the same dar; the text explains the rulings for different dar combinations. Then it addresses the question of whether a child who became Muslim through following a parent must renew faith upon puberty. The author explains the views of three Hanafi authorities (al-Maturidi, Fakhr al-Islam, Shams al-A'imma) on the obligation of faith for the discerning child, concluding that no renewal is needed because the previous faith was valid.

Key Terms
تبعية (tābiʿiyya): The legal status of a child following a parent's religion, specifically becoming Muslim through a parent's conversion.
عرض الإسلام (ʿard al-Islām): The act of presenting Islam to a non-Muslim spouse for conversion, required when one spouse accepts Islam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, if a child becomes Muslim by following a parent (tayyibah), what is the ruling upon reaching puberty?
A) The child must renew faith by pronouncing the shahada.
B) The child must renew faith only if he was not discerning earlier.
C) The child does not need to renew faith because it occurred as an obligation.
D) The child must repeat all previous acts of worship.

Key Takeaway
A minor child who becomes Muslim through tayyibah does not need to renew faith upon puberty; the initial conversion is valid and sufficient.

Child's Religion Between Kitabi and Majusi
Explanation of why Majusi is worse than Kitabi and child's religion when born between them

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the child of a Kitabi and Majusi parent and explain why the Majusi is considered worse than the Kitabi.

Core Idea
The child follows the Kitabi parent because the Kitabi religion has a claim to divine origin and legal benefits.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies why a Majusi is considered worse than a Kitabi in Hanafi fiqh, and the consequential ruling that a child born to one Kitabi and one Majusi parent follows the Kitabi. The reasoning is grounded in the legal benefits (permissibility of marriage and slaughtered meat) that the Kitabi enjoys in this world and the lighter punishment in the Hereafter.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key distinction
Compare the legal status of a Kitabi (Jew/Christian) and a Majusi (Zoroastrian) according to Hanafi fiqh. What makes one 'worse'?
The difference lies in the claim to a heavenly book and its legal consequences.
2
Note the worldly benefits
List the practical legal concessions granted to a Kitabi: permissibility of eating their slaughtered meat and marrying Kitabi women. These are absent for Majusi.
These benefits serve as evidence of the Kitabi's superior status.
3
Apply to a mixed-parent child
When one parent is Kitabi and the other Majusi, the child takes the religion of the Kitabi. Understand that this is because the Kitabi is 'better' in both worldly and Hereafter terms.
The text explicitly states there is consideration for the child even in the Hereafter by reduction of punishment.
4
Understand the underlying reasoning (talil)
The ruling is based on a causal link: the Kitabi's claim to a heavenly book makes him better, so the child follows him. This is a form of qiyas where the illa (effective cause) is superiority in religion.
The argument type is talil (reasoning by cause), not direct textual proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والمجوسي شر من الكتابي لأن للكتابي ديناً سماوياً بحسب الدعوى ولهذا تؤكل ذبيحته وتجوز مناكحة الكتابية بخلاف المجوسي فكان شراً منه حتى إذا ولد ولد بين كتابي ومجوسي فهو كتابي لأن فيه نوع نظر له حتى في الآخرة بنقصان العقاب كما في فتح القدير.
His statement: 'The Majusi is worse than the Kitabi because the Kitabi has a heavenly religion according to claim, and therefore his slaughtered meat is eaten and marriage with a Kitabi woman is permissible, unlike the Majusi. So he is worse than him, such that if a child is born between a Kitabi and a Majusi, it is Kitabi, because there is a kind of consideration for him even in the Hereafter by reduction of punishment as in Fath al-Qadir.'
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage explains the reason for the ruling that a child born between a Kitabi and a Majusi follows the Kitabi. The author, commenting on the text of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, states that the Majusi is worse than the Kitabi because the Kitabi follows a religion that claims a divine book, which has legal benefits in this world: the meat slaughtered by a Kitabi is permissible (for Muslims) and marriage to a Kitabi woman is permissible, unlike the Majusi. Therefore, when a child is born from parents of these two religions, the child takes the religion of the Kitabi, as there is some goodness in it, even in the Hereafter the punishment is lighter.

Key Terms
مجوسي (majūsī): A follower of the Magian religion (Zoroastrianism), considered a non-Muslim group without a revealed book.
كتابي (kitābī): A follower of a religion with a revealed book (Jew or Christian), according to claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because Majusi are fire worshippers.
B) Because Kitabi have a heavenly book, making their slaughtered meat permissible and marriage to their women permissible.
C) Because Majusi are from a different race.
D) Because Kitabi are monotheists.

Key Takeaway
The child born to a Kitabi and a Majusi follows the Kitabi parent because the Kitabi religion has a claim to divine origin and legal benefits, making the child's punishment lighter in the Hereafter.

Permanence of Child's Status After Parent's Apostasy
Ruling on permanence of child's following status after parent's apostasy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on the permanence of a child's following status after a parent's apostasy and identify the specific exception involving Dar al-Harb.

Core Idea
The child's status as follower of the better parent is permanent; apostasy of the parent does not change it, except in Dar al-Harb.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies an important nuance in Hanafi family law. While a child is initially ruled to follow the religion of the better parent, this determination is final. Even if that parent later apostatizes, the child does not automatically follow into apostasy. However, a specific exception exists when the apostate parent takes the child into Dar al-Harb, which can affect marriage ties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial ruling
The child follows the better parent in religion. Determine which parent has the better religion (e.g., Muslim vs. non-Muslim).
2
Understand the principle of permanence
Once the child is ruled to follow the better parent, that status is final and cannot be undone by the parent later losing 'goodness' (e.g., through apostasy).
3
Apply to apostasy
If the parent who was the better parent apostatizes, the child does not follow the parent into apostasy. The child's status remains the same.
4
Identify the exception
If the apostate parent joins the child in Dar al-Harb (territory not under Islamic rule), then a change occurs: for a minor married woman, her marriage is dissolved due to difference of dar.
This does not change the child's religious status but affects marital status.
5
Note the exception to the exception
If one of the parents died as a Muslim, the child's status remains attached to that deceased parent, and the above exception does not apply.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أنه بعد ما حكم بكونه تبعاً لخير الأبوين لا يزول بزوال الخيرية، فلو ارتد المسلم منهما لا يتبعه الولد في الردة إلا إن لحق به المرتد إلى دار الحرب فإن الصبية المنكوحة تبين من زوجها للتبياين إلا إذا كان أحد الأبوين مات على إسلامه وتمامه في المحيط.
Then know that after it is ruled that he is a follower of the better of the two parents, it is not removed by the removal of goodness. So if the Muslim among them apostatizes, the child does not follow him in apostasy except if the apostate joins him to Dar al-Harb. Then the minor married woman is separated from her husband due to difference of dar, except if one of the parents died as Muslim. The rest is in al-Muhit.
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage states an important principle: once the child is determined to be a follower of the better parent, that status is permanent and does not change if the parent later loses goodness (e.g., by apostasy). The child does not automatically become apostate if the parent apostatizes. However, if the apostate parent joins the child in Dar al-Harb, then the child's status may change, and this affects marriage rulings. The exception is if the parent died as Muslim.

Key Terms
ارتداد (irtidād): Apostasy from Islam by a parent after becoming Muslim.
دار الحرب (dār al-ḥarb): Territory not under Islamic rule, considered a zone of conflict.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The child becomes apostate as well.
B) The child remains Muslim because the status is permanent.
C) The child becomes a follower of the other parent.
D) The child becomes of no religion until puberty.

Key Takeaway
Once a child is ruled to follow the better parent, that status is permanent and unaffected by the parent's apostasy, with the limited exception of Dar al-Harb affecting marriage.

Ruling on Saying One Non-Muslim Group is Better than Another
Discussion of takfir for saying 'Nasrani is better than Yahudi' and implications for child's religion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on attributing goodness to a non-Muslim religion and explain the exception for Kitabi vs Majusi.

Core Idea
Attributing goodness to any false religion is kufr; one may only say one is worse.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq examines a sensitive issue: the legal status of comparing non-Muslim religions. It clarifies that praising any false religion—even comparatively—can constitute disbelief (kufr), with a nuanced exception recognized by some Hanafi scholars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act
The act is saying one non-Muslim group (e.g., Nasrani) is better than another (e.g., Yahudi).
This includes both explicit and implicit attributions of goodness.
2
State the ruling
According to al-Khulasa and Jami' al-Fusulayn, this incurs takfir (disbelief).
The proper expression is to say one is worse, not better.
3
Note the exception
Some earlier Hanafi scholars permitted saying 'Kitabi is better than Majusi' due to worldly legal differences.
The relied-upon view is that this is still problematic and should be avoided.
4
Apply the rule
One should avoid any statement that attributes goodness to a false religion.
Instead, use expressions like 'Yahudi is worse than Nasrani'.
5
Understand the basis
There is no real goodness in any false religion; praising them implies approval of falsehood.
This principle is derived from the consensus of Hanafi scholars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد ما حكم بكونه تبعاً لأقلهما شراً إذا تمجس المتبوع بطلت التبعية، ولم يقل المصنف والكتابي خير من المجوسي كما في المحيط، وبعض الكتب، لأنه لا خير في دين هؤلاء الطائفة ولكن في كل منهما خلاف الخير وفي المجوسية أكثر فيكون شراً منهما . وفي الخلاصة من كتاب ألفاظ التكفير : لو قال النصرانية خير من اليهودية يكفر، وينبغي أن يقول اليهودية شر من فهذا يقتضي أنه لو قال الكتابي خير من المجوسي يكفر مع أن هذه العبارة وقعت لبعض مشايخنا كما سمعت إلا أن يقال بالفرق وهو الظاهر لأنه لا خيرية لإحدى الملتين على الأخرى في أحكام الدنيا والآخرة بخلاف الكتابي بالنسبة إلى المجوسي للفرق بين أحكامهما في الدنيا والآخرة. وفي الخبازية ما يقتضي أن المنع إنما هو لتفضيل النصرانية على اليهودية والأمر بالعكس لأن اليهود نزاعهم في النبوات والنصارى في الإلهيات فالنصارى أشد كفراً ا هـ . وفيه نظر لأنه لو كان كذلك لم يصح قوله في الخلاصة «وينبغي أن يقول اليهودية شر من النصرانية فعلم أن التكفير إنما هو لأجل إثبات الخيرية للكافر ولذا قال في جامع الفصولين : لو قال النصرانية خير من المجوسية كفر وينبغي أن يقول المجوسية شر من النصرانية ا هـ . ويلزم على ما في البزازية من أن النصارى شر من اليهود أن الولد المتولد من يهودية ونصراني أو عكسه أن يكون تبعاً لليهودي دون النصراني.
And after it is ruled that he is a follower of the less evil of the two, if the followed one becomes Majusi, the following is void. And the author did not say 'Kitabi is better than Majusi' as in al-Muhit and some books, because there is no goodness in the religion of these groups, but each contains contrary to goodness, and in Majusiyya it is more, so he is worse than them. In al-Khulasa from the book Alfaz al-Takfir: If he says 'Nasrani is better than Yahudi', he becomes a disbeliever. It is proper to say 'Yahudi is worse than Nasrani'. This implies that if he says 'Kitabi is better than Majusi', he becomes a disbeliever, even though this expression is found from some of our teachers. However, it is said there is a distinction and it is apparent, because there is no superiority of one religion over the other in worldly and Hereafter rulings, unlike Kitabi relative to Majusi due to the difference in their rulings in this world and the Hereafter. In al-Khabbaziyya, what suggests the prohibition is only for preferring Nasrani over Yahudi, and the opposite, because the Jews dispute about prophecies and the Christians about divinity, so Christians are more severe in disbelief. But this is considered, because if so, the statement in al-Khulasa 'proper to say Yahudi is worse than Nasrani' would not be correct. Thus it is known that takfir is only for attributing goodness to a disbeliever. In Jami' al-Fusulayn: If he says 'Nasrani is better than Majusi', he becomes a disbeliever, and it is proper to say 'Majusi is worse than Nasrani'. And it follows from what is in al-Bazzaziyya that Christians are worse than Jews, that a child born from a Jewess and a Christian man or vice versa would follow the Jew.
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage addresses the delicate issue of comparing non-Muslim religions. The author first notes that the original text avoided saying 'Kitabi is better than Majusi' because there is no goodness in any of these false religions. Then he discusses the ruling in al-Khulasa that saying 'Nasrani is better than Yahudi' constitutes takfir (disbelief) because it attributes goodness to a false religion. The proper expression is to say one is worse than the other. This leads to a discussion about whether saying 'Kitabi is better than Majusi' also incurs takfir, given that some Hanafi scholars used such phrasing. The author presents a distinction: for religions like Christianity and Judaism, there is no superiority in any respect, so praising one is kufr; but for Kitabi vs. Majusi, there is a worldly legal difference (e.g., permissible marriage and slaughter), so it may be permissible to say Kitabi is better in that limited sense. The passage also notes that according to al-Bazzaziyya, since Nasrani is worse than Yahudi, a child of a Jewess and a Christian follows the Jew.

Key Terms
تكفير (takfīr): Declaring someone a disbeliever (kafir) for a statement or action.
خيرية (khayriyya): Goodness or superiority in a moral or religious sense.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Khulasa, what is the ruling for saying 'Nasrani is better than Yahudi'?
A) It is permissible because both are false.
B) It is makruh tanzih.
C) It makes one a disbeliever (kufr).
D) It is only disliked if said in public.

Key Takeaway
Saying one non-Muslim religion is better than another is kufr; one should say one is worse.

Child Follows Better Parent and the Dispute on Comparing Disbelievers
Intra-Hanafi dispute on comparing disbelievers and child's followership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on child following the better parent and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether saying a Kitabi is better than a Majusi is kufr.

Core Idea
A child follows the parent with the less evil religion; calling one disbeliever 'better' is dangerous if it implies goodness.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on the ruling that the child follows the better of his parents in religion. It also addresses a nuanced intra-Hanafi debate: Can we say one disbeliever is better than another? The text navigates between caution (to avoid attributing absolute goodness to a disbeliever) and practicality (since some disbelievers are indeed worse in rulings).

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule of child followership
The child follows the parent with the better (i.e., less evil) religion. This is a wajib ruling.
The 'better' parent is not necessarily the Muslim; if both are disbelievers, the one with the lesser evil is followed.
2
Understand the effect of apostasy or conversion
If the better parent apostatizes, the child does not follow him unless the apostate joins dar al-harb. If the followed becomes a Majusi, the followership is void.
Majusism is considered the worst, so if the followed becomes Majusi, the child cannot follow.
3
Analyze the dispute on saying one disbeliever is better
Some Hanafi scholars say it is kufr to say 'a Kitabi is better than a Majusi' because it establishes absolute goodness for a disbeliever.
The opposing view sees it as permissible because it only compares degrees of evil.
4
Examine the evidence for the preferred view
The text cites food rulings: Jews may be eaten from, Christians and Majusis not, showing Christians are worse than Jews in worldly rulings.
This supports the idea that Christians are 'less good' (i.e., more evil) than Jews.
5
Apply the practical consequences
The thamarah al-khilaf includes: (1) child of a Jewish mother and Christian father follows the Jew; (2) food from a Jew is permissible but from a Christian is disliked.
Knowing which disbeliever is worse affects daily interactions like accepting food invitations.

كتاب النكاح باب نكاح الكافر النصرانية ا هـ. القدير. ثم اعلم أنه بعد ما حكم بكونه تبعاً لخير الأبوين لا يزول بزوال الخيرية، فلو ارتد المسلم منهما لا يتبعه الولد في الردة إلا إن لحق به المرتد إلى دار الحرب فإن الصبية المنكوحة تبين من زوجها للتباين إلا إذا كان أحد الأبوين مات على إسلامه وتمامه في المحيط. وبعد ما حكم بكونه تبعاً لأقلهما شراً إذا تمجس المتبوع بطلت التبعية، ولم يقل المصنف والكتابي خير من المجوسي كما في المحيط، وبعض الكتب، لأنه لا خير في دين هؤلاء الطائفة ولكن في كل منهما خلاف الخير وفي المجوسية أكثر فيكون شراً منهما . وفي الخلاصة من كتاب ألفاظ التكفير : لو قال النصرانية خير من اليهودية يكفر، وينبغي أن يقول اليهودية شر من النصرانية فهذا يقتضي أنه لو قال الكتابي خير من المجوسي يكفر مع أن هذه العبارة وقعت لبعض مشايخنا كما سمعت إلا أن يقال بالفرق وهو الظاهر لأنه لا خيرية لإحدى الملتين على الأخرى في أحكام الدنيا والآخرة بخلاف الكتابي بالنسبة إلى المجوسي للفرق بين أحكامهما في الدنيا والآخرة. وفي الخبازية ما يقتضي أن المنع إنما هو لتفضيل النصرانية على اليهودية والأمر بالعكس لأن اليهود نزاعهم في النبوات والنصارى في الإلهيات فالنصارى أشد كفراً ا هـ . وفيه نظر لأنه لو كان كذلك لم يصح قوله في الخلاصة «وينبغي أن يقول اليهودية شر من النصرانية فعلم أن التكفير إنما هو لأجل إثبات الخيرية للكافر ولذا قال في جامع الفصولين : لو قال النصرانية خير من المجوسية كفر وينبغي أن يقول المجوسية شر من النصرانية ا هـ . ويلزم على ما في البزازية من أن النصارى شر من اليهود أن الولد المتولد من يهودية ونصراني أو عكسه أن يكون تبعاً لليهودي دون النصراني. فإن قلت: ما فائدته؟ قلت : خفة العقوبة في الآخرة، وأما في الدنيا فلما ذكره الولوالجي من كتاب الأضحية أن الكافر إذا دعا رجلاً إلى طعامه، فإن كان مجوسياً أو نصرانياً يكره وإن قال اشتريت اللحم من السوق لأن المجوسي يطبخ المنخنقة والموقوذة والمتردية والنصراني لا ذبيحة له وإنما يأكل ذبيحة المسلم أو يخنق، وإن كان الداعي إلى الطعام يهودياً فلا بأس بأكله لأن اليهودي لا يأكل إلا من ذبيحة اليهودي أو المسلم ا هـ. فعلم أن النصراني شر من اليهودي في أحكام الدنيا أيضاً .
Book of Nikah, Chapter on Marriage of the Disbeliever. The Christian? ... Then know that after it is ruled that he follows the better of the two parents, this does not cease with the cessation of being better. So if the Muslim among them apostatizes, the child does not follow him in apostasy unless the apostate joins the dar al-harb, for then the minor married woman is separated from her husband due to incompatibility, unless one of the parents died as a Muslim. Its completion is in al-Muhit. And after it is ruled that he follows the lesser in evil of the two, if the followed becomes a Majusi, the following is invalidated. The author did not say 'the Kitabi is better than the Majusi' as in al-Muhit and some books, because there is no good in the religion of these groups, but in each there is a lack of good, and in Majusism there is more, so it is worse than both. And in al-Khulasa from the book Alfaz al-Takfir: if one says 'the Christian is better than the Jew', he becomes a disbeliever, and he should say 'the Jew is worse than the Christian'. This implies that if one said 'the Kitabi is better than the Majusi', he becomes a disbeliever, even though this expression occurred from some of our scholars as I heard, unless it is said there is a difference, which is apparent, because there is no goodness of one religion over the other in the rulings of this world and the Hereafter, unlike the Kitabi relative to the Majusi due to the difference between their rulings in this world and the Hereafter. And in al-Khabbaziyya, it implies that the prohibition is for preferring the Christian over the Jew, and the opposite is the case because the Jews' dispute is about prophecies and the Christians' about divinity, so the Christians are more severe in disbelief. End quote. There is consideration in it, because if that were the case, his statement in al-Khulasa would not be valid: 'he should say the Jew is worse than the Christian'. So it is known that takfir is only for establishing goodness for the disbeliever. Hence, in Jami' al-Fusulain: if one says the Christian is better than the Majusi, he becomes a disbeliever, and he should say the Majusi is worse than the Christian. End quote. And it follows from what is in al-Bazzaziyya that the Christians are worse than the Jews that a child born from a Jewish woman and a Christian man, or vice versa, follows the Jew rather than the Christian. If you ask: what is the benefit? I say: lightness of punishment in the Hereafter. As for this world, it is due to what al-Walwaliji mentioned from the book of Sacrifice: that if a disbeliever invites a man to his food, if he is a Majusi or a Christian, it is disliked even if he says 'I bought meat from the market', because the Majusi cooks the strangled, beaten, and fallen animals, and the Christian has no slaughter, he only eats the slaughter of a Muslim or strangles. But if the inviter to food is a Jew, there is no problem in eating it, because a Jew only eats from the slaughter of a Jew or a Muslim. End quote. So it is known that the Christian is worse than the Jew in the rulings of this world as well.
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the issue of which religion a child follows when his parents are of different faiths. The author states that the child follows the better of the two parents in religion, and this followship does not dissolve if the better parent later apostatizes, except if that apostate joins the dar al-harb. However, if the inferior parent becomes a Majusi, the child's followership is void because Majusism is considered the worst. The author then addresses whether it is permissible to say a Kitabi (Jew or Christian) is better than a Majusi. Some scholars consider such a statement kufr because it attributes goodness to a disbeliever, while others allow it due to the differences in their worldly and otherworldly rulings. As an example, the food ruling from al-Walwaliji shows that a Christian's food is disliked while a Jew's is not, indicating Christians are worse. This demonstrates the practical implication of knowing which disbeliever is worse.

Key Terms
التبعية (al-taba'iyyah): The status of the child following the religion of the better parent
خير الأبوين (khayr al-abawayn): The parent with the better religion (lesser evil) among the two
الخيرية (al-khayriyyah): The quality of being better or having goodness in religion

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens to the child's followership if the better parent becomes a Majusi (Magian)?
A) The child continues to follow that parent.
B) The child follows the other parent.
C) The followership is invalidated.
D) The child becomes independent in choice.

Key Takeaway
Child follows the less evil parent; comparing disbelievers is tricky—avoid attributing absolute goodness.

Presentation of Islam to the Non-Muslim Spouse
Ruling with proof and scenario enumeration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on presenting Islam to the other spouse when one converts, identify the eight scenarios, and explain why two cases do not require presentation.

Core Idea
Islam is obedience, not a cause for separation; presentation establishes either purposes or separation.

When one spouse embraces Islam, the marriage's purposes are threatened by religious difference. The Hanafi school mandates presenting Islam to the other spouse to either preserve the union through shared faith or lawfully separate upon refusal. This lesson unpacks the reasoning, the eight possible scenarios, and the Hanafi critique of al-Shafi'i's automatic separation view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Ruling
Locate the matn statement that when a spouse converts, Islam is presented to the other. If accepted, the marriage continues; if refused, they are separated.
This is from Kanz al-Daqa'iq, commented on by al-Bazzaziyya.
2
Understand the Legal Rationale
The author explains that the purposes of marriage are lost, so a basis for separation is needed. Islam is an act of obedience and cannot serve as a cause for separation. Therefore, presentation is necessary.
This is a 'dalil aqli' – rational proof.
3
Analyze the Eight Scenarios
The passage enumerates four religious combinations of spouses (both Kitabi, both Majusi, one Kitabi one Majusi) and whether the husband or wife converts, giving eight cases. Two of these do not require presentation.
The two exceptions are: wife Kitabiyya and husband Muslim (valid marriage), and wife Majusiyya and husband Muslim (invalid marriage).
4
Contrast with al-Shafi'i
The author critiques al-Shafi'i's view that conversion itself causes separation, calling it an inversion. In Hanafi usul, an act of obedience cannot produce a harmful effect like separation.
The difference leads to different practical consequences regarding the waiting period.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وإذا أسلم أحد الزوجين عرض الإسلام على الآخر فإن أسلم وإلا فرق بينهما) لأن المقاصد قد فاتت فلا بد من سبب تبتنى عليه الفرقة والإسلام طاعة فلا يصلح سبباً فيعرض الإسلام لتحصل المقاصد بالإسلام أو تثبت الفرقة بالإباء وإضافة الشافعي الفرقة إلى الإسلام من باب فساد الوضع وهو أن يترتب على العلة نقيض ما تقتضيه، وسيأتي أن زوج الكتابية إذا أسلم فإنه يبقى النكاح لجواز التزوج بها ابتداء، فحينئذ صار المراد من عبارته هنا أنهما إما مجوسيان فأسلم الزوج أو المرأة، أو كتابيان فأسلمت المرأة، أو أحدهما كتابي والآخر فأسلم الكتابي أو المجوسي وهو المرأة، فالحاصل أنهما إما أن يكونا كتابيين أو مجوسيين أو أحدهما كتابي والآخر مجوسي وهو صادق بصورتين فهي أربعة، وكل من الأربعة إما أن يكون المسلم الزوج أو الزوجة فهي ثمانية ومنها مسألتان لا يعرض الإسلام فيهما على الآخر وهما إذا كانت المرأة كتابية والزوج كتابي أو مجوسي والمسلم. الزوج والباقية مجوسي هو الحال بمعنى الوصف. كذا قيل ولا يتم وقد قيل المنع من قولهم اليهودية خير من النصرانية باعتبار أن كفر النصارى أغلظ من كفر اليهود لأن نزاعهم في النبوات ونزاع النصارى في الإلهيات. وقوله تعالى وقالت اليهود عزير ابن الله ﴾ [التوبة : ٣٠] كلام طائفة قليلة كما صرح به في التفسير. وقوله تعالى لتجدن أشد الناس عداوة [المائدة: ۸۲] الآية لا يرد على هذا لأن البحث في قوة الكفر وشدته لا في قوة العداوة وضعفها . إذا تأملت النصوص بعلتها ومعلولها وحينئذ لا يتجه الاعتراض . ا هـ كلام البزازية
His saying: (And when one of the spouses becomes Muslim, Islam is presented to the other; if he accepts, then [the marriage remains]; otherwise, they are separated.) Because the purposes have been lost, so there must be a basis on which separation is founded. Islam is obedience, so it is not suitable as a cause (for separation). So Islam is presented so that the purposes are achieved through Islam, or separation is established through refusal. Al-Shafi'i attributed separation to Islam as a type of inversion, where the opposite of what the cause requires results. It will come that if a husband of a Kitabiyya becomes Muslim, the marriage remains because it is permissible to marry her initially. So here his expression means: either both are Majusis and the husband or wife becomes Muslim, or both are Kitabis and the wife becomes Muslim, or one is Kitabi and the other becomes Muslim, the Kitabi or the Majusi and it is the woman. So the sum is that either both are Kitabis, both are Majusis, or one is Kitabi and the other is Majusi, which is true in two forms. So they are four cases. And each of the four, either the Muslim is the husband or the wife, so they are eight. Among them are two cases where Islam is not presented to the other: when the woman is Kitabiyya and the husband is Kitabi or Majusi and the Muslim is the husband. And the remainder: Majusi is the state, meaning the description. Thus it was said, but it is not complete. It was also said that the prohibition of saying 'the Jew is better than the Christian' is because the disbelief of the Christians is more severe than the disbelief of the Jews, because their dispute is in prophecies and the Christians' dispute is in divinity. But the verse 'And the Jews said Uzair is the son of Allah' (9:30) is the speech of a small group, as explained in the tafsir. And the verse 'You will find the most hostile people' (5:82) does not refute this, because the discussion is about the strength and severity of disbelief, not about the strength of enmity. When you examine the texts with their 'illal and their effects, then no objection stands. End quote. This is the speech of al-Bazzaziyya.
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage comments on the matn ruling that when one spouse converts to Islam, the other is presented with Islam. The author explains the rationale: the purposes of marriage (procreation, companionship) are lost when spouses have different religions, so presenting Islam gives the other spouse the chance to accept and achieve those purposes, or to refuse and cause separation. He critiques al-Shafi'i's view that conversion itself causes separation, arguing that Islam is an act of obedience and cannot be a valid cause for separation. The author then enumerates eight scenarios based on the religions of the spouses (Kitabi or Majusi) and which one converts. Among these, two do not require presentation of Islam: when the wife is Kitabiyya and the husband is already Muslim (the marriage is valid) and when the wife is Majusiyya and the husband is Muslim (the marriage is invalid). The passage ends with a discussion about the severity of disbelief, quoting al-Bazzaziyya that Christians are more severe than Jews because their dispute concerns divinity, while Jews dispute only prophecies.

Key Terms
عرض الإسلام (ard al-islam): The act of offering Islam to the non-Muslim spouse when the other converts
الفرقة (al-furqah): Legal separation between spouses due to difference in religion
الكتابي (al-kitabi): A Jew or Christian who adheres to a revealed scripture

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what happens if a man converts to Islam but his wife remains a Majusi?
A) The marriage is immediately annulled
B) Islam is presented to the wife; if she refuses, they are separated
C) The husband must divorce her
D) The marriage is valid because he married her before Islam

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between conversion and refusal: presentation of Islam is required before separation, because Islam is obedience and cannot be a cause for divorce.

Guardian for the Insane Spouse
Ruling on guardian for insane spouse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the appointment of a guardian for an insane spouse when the father is absent, and explain why the judge cannot act directly.

Core Idea
The insane cannot divorce; the judge must appoint a guardian to act on their behalf.

This lesson examines the procedure when an insane person's spouse wishes to separate due to refusal of Islam. The passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies that if the insane person has no father, the judge appoints a guardian. The guardian then pronounces the separation. This ensures the right of the spouse while respecting the insane person's lack of legal capacity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the situation
The spouse of an insane person wants separation due to religious incompatibility (refusal to convert). The insane person has no father to act as guardian.
The father would normally be the guardian.
2
Locate the judge's role
The judge does not directly pronounce divorce. Instead, he appoints a guardian (wasi) for the insane person.
This is because the insane person lacks legal capacity for divorce.
3
State the guardian's action
The appointed guardian then pronounces the separation (tafriq) on behalf of the insane spouse.
The separation is effective through the guardian's action.
4
Understand the reasoning
The insane person is not capable of divorce, so the judge acts as deputy by appointing a guardian who can perform the act.
Quoted from al-Zahidi: 'because the insane person is not of the people of divorce'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (بل يعرض على أبويه) ذكر الباقاني في شرح الملتقى ما نصه: قال في روضة العلماء للزاهدي : فإن لم يكن له أب نصب القاضي عن المجنون وصياً فيقضي عليه بالفرقة. أقول: وإنما ينصب الولي لأن المجنون ليس من أهل التطليق لينوب القاضي بالتفريق ا هـ. وما نقله عن الزاهدي مذكور في التتارخانية قوله: (كالمرأة إذا وجدت الزوج عنيناً فإنه يؤجل ولو مجبوباً فإنه لا يؤجل) هكذا في نسخة والذي في عامة النسخ: كالمرأة إذا وجدت الزوج محبوباً فإنه لا يؤجل
His saying: (Rather, it is presented to his parents). Al-Baqqani mentioned in Sharh al-Multaqa: He said in Rawdat al-Ulama' by al-Zahidi: If he has no father, the judge appoints a guardian over the insane person and he pronounces separation. I say: He appoints a guardian because the insane person is not capable of divorce so that the judge may act as his deputy. End quote. What he transmitted from al-Zahidi is mentioned in al-Tatarkhaniyya. His saying: (Like the woman who finds her husband impotent: he is given a delay, but if he is castrated, he is not given a delay). Thus in one copy, and in the majority of copies: like the woman who finds her husband a castrato, he is not given a delay.
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage contains two separate rulings. First, regarding the presentation of Islam when a minor or insane person's parent converts: Islam is presented to the parents. If there is no father, the judge appoints a guardian for the insane person and then pronounces separation if the guardian refuses Islam. The reason is that the insane person cannot divorce himself, so the judge acts on his behalf. Second, concerning impotence: if a wife finds her husband impotent, she is entitled to a waiting period of one year. However, if the husband is castrated (majbub), there is no waiting period because there is no hope of cure. The text notes a variant: some copies say 'mahbub' (castrato) instead of 'majbub', meaning the same.

Key Terms
المجنون (al-majnoon): An insane person who lacks legal capacity for divorce
الولي (al-wali): Guardian appointed by the judge for the insane
التطليق (al-tatliq): Pronouncement of divorce or separation

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the judge appoint a guardian before pronouncing separation?
A) Because the insane person is not capable of divorce
B) Because it is customary
C) Because the father is absent
D) Because the judge needs permission

Key Takeaway
The judge must appoint a guardian for an insane spouse before any separation can be pronounced.

Impotence and Castration Rulings
Ruling on impotence delay

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the Hanafi rulings for an impotent husband ('anin) and a castrated husband (majbub) regarding the wife's right to a waiting period.

Core Idea
An impotent husband gets a one-year delay; a castrated husband gets none—no hope, no wait.

This lesson covers the wife's remedy when the husband cannot perform intercourse. The Hanafi school distinguishes between impotence ('inna) with possibility of cure and permanent castration. The passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies that only the former warrants a delay; the latter does not. A textual variant is also noted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
The text contrasts two types of husbands: the 'anin (impotent but potentially curable) and the majbub (castrated, permanently incapable).
Some manuscripts read 'mahbub' (beloved) but the intended meaning is 'majbub'.
2
State the ruling for the impotent
If the wife finds her husband impotent, he is given a delay (ajal). In other Hanafi texts, this delay is one year.
The delay is to allow time for treatment.
3
State the ruling for the castrated
If the husband is castrated, he is not given any delay. The wife may seek immediate annulment.
Because there is no hope of cure.
4
Note the textual variant
The passage notes that in one copy it says 'majbub', but the majority of copies say 'mahbub' (castrato). Both indicate permanent incapacity.
The difference is scribal; the ruling is the same.

Source Text

المجبوب (al-majbub): A man who has been castrated, permanently incapable of intercourse

B) He is not given any delay
C) He must divorce immediately
D) The wife must wait for three months

Key Takeaway
A wife with a castrated husband may seek immediate annulment; no waiting period is required.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
10 min
The Nature of Separation Upon Refusal: Talaq or Faskh?
Ruling on presentation of Islam to the other spouse when one converts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on the nature of separation when the non-Muslim spouse refuses Islam, and explain the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) regarding 'iddah.

Core Idea
When a spouse refuses Islam, the judge's separation is a talaq (Abu Hanifa) not a faskh (Abu Yusuf) — the difference affects the wife's 'iddah.

When one spouse converts to Islam, the other is presented with Islam. If they refuse, the judge separates them. But is this separation a divorce (talaq) or an annulment (faskh)? The Hanafi school has an internal difference: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say it is a talaq; Abu Yusuf says it is a faskh. This distinction has practical consequences, especially for the waiting period ('iddah). Let us examine the proofs and the relied-upon view.

Guided Path
1
State the issue
The core dispute is about the legal nature of the separation ordered by the judge when the non-Muslim spouse refuses to accept Islam after presentation.
2
Identify the majority Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa & Muhammad)
They hold that the separation is a talaq (divorce) pronounced by the judge who acts as an agent for the husband.
This is the mufta bihi view.
3
Examine the majority’s proof
By refusing, the husband refrains from keeping his wife with goodness while being able to convert. The judge therefore takes his place to release her with kindness, analogous to the case of an impotent husband.
4
Examine Abu Yusuf’s proof
Abu Yusuf argues that the separation is due to a cause in which both spouses participate (the refusal and the conversion), so it cannot be a talaq; it is a dissolution (faskh), like separation due to ownership.
5
Determine the thamarah and tarjih
The practical consequence (thamarah) is the type of 'iddah: if talaq, the wife observes the 'iddah of divorce; if faskh, the 'iddah may be different. The majority view is relied upon in fatwa.
The judge's role is limited: he acts for the husband, not the wife.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling on the procedure when one spouse becomes Muslim. The author explains that when a Muslim convert is one spouse, Islam must be presented to the other. If that spouse refuses, the judge separates them. This separation is considered a divorce (talaq) according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (the two), but Abu Yusuf holds it is not a divorce but a dissolution (faskh). The text then explores various scenarios: who is included (adults, discerning children, insane persons), the role of the judge in effecting the separation, and the difference between refusal and apostasy. It also addresses the case where the non-Muslim spouse changes religion (e.g., from Magian to Jew) after the other's conversion, noting that such changes do not affect the marriage because all disbelief is one religion. The passage also discusses the presentation to the parents of an insane spouse and the analogy with impotence. The commentator resolves an apparent contradiction in the author's work and provides the relied-upon view. This lesson is an intra-Hanafi dispute because of the difference between Abu Yusuf and the two regarding the nature of the separation.

Key Terms
إباء (ibā’): The act of refusing to embrace Islam when presented by the judge after one spouse's conversion.
فرقة (furqah): Separation between spouses; the text discusses whether it is a divorce (talaq) or a dissolution (faskh).
طلاق (talāq): Divorce, a unilateral repudiation by the husband or his agent.
فسخ (faskh): Annulment or dissolution of marriage not counting as divorce.
عرض الإسلام (ʿarḍ al-islām): The act of presenting Islam to the non-Muslim spouse by the judge or the Muslim spouse.
مجبوب (majbūb): A castrated male; in marriage law, no delay is given because there is no hope of cure.
عنن (ʿinnin): Impotence; a husband is given a delay to seek treatment before separation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the difference between Abu Yusuf's view and that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad regarding the separation when the non-Muslim spouse refuses Islam?
A) Abu Yusuf says it is a divorce, while the two say it is a dissolution.
B) Abu Yusuf says it is a dissolution, while the two say it is a divorce.
C) Abu Yusuf says the judge has no role, while the two say the judge pronounces it.
D) Abu Yusuf says the woman can also pronounce divorce, while the two say only the man.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi majority holds that the judge's separation upon refusal of Islam is a divorce (talaq), not a dissolution (faskh).

Nikah — Marriage
advanced
10 min
Ruling on Spouse's Refusal of Islam: Talaq or Faskh?
Commentary on intra-Hanafi dispute over legal nature of separation after spouse's refusal of Islam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) that the husband's refusal is a talaq and the wife's refusal is a faskh, and evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) of this dispute.

Core Idea
The judge acts as deputy for the husband in divorce, making his refusal a talaq; for the wife, it is a faskh.

When one spouse embraces Islam and the other refuses, the Hanafi school requires the judge to separate them. However, the legal nature of this separation—whether it is a divorce (talaq) initiated by the husband or a judicial dissolution (faskh)—is a matter of dispute between Abu Hanifa and Muhammad on one side, and Abu Yusuf on the other. This commentary clarifies the proof and the practical implications of each view.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi Position (Abu Hanifa & Muhammad)
Identify the ruling: the husband's refusal is a talaq (divorce) effected by the judge as his deputy; the wife's refusal is a faskh (dissolution) because she is not eligible for divorce.
This is the mufta bihi position.
2
Identify the Opponent and His View
Abu Yusuf holds that both cases are faskh, not talaq, because the cause of separation (difference in religion) is shared by both spouses.
Abu Yusuf compares it to separation due to ownership (e.g., slave marriage) which is always faskh.
3
Follow the Opponent's Proof
Abu Yusuf's argument: Separation due to a common cause resembles separation by ownership, which is a faskh; therefore, it should be faskh for both.
The key point is the shared nature of the cause.
4
Analyze the Hanafi Response
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad respond: The husband has the capacity to retain his wife by converting, but he refuses; thus he fails in his duty to keep her with kindness. The judge steps in as his deputy to issue a divorce, just as in cases of impotence or castration. The wife, lacking the right to divorce, cannot have the judge act as her deputy for talaq; hence her refusal results in faskh.
The distinction is based on the ability to initiate divorce (husband) vs. not (wife).
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf (Practical Consequence)
If classified as talaq, the husband can take his wife back during her iddah; the divorce counts toward the three-fold limit. If faskh, no return is possible except by a new marriage, and it does not count as a talaq.
This affects the number of divorces available and the ability to reconcile.

Source Text
