Marriage with a Pregnant Woman from Dar al-Harb: Prohibition Due to Lineage, Not Iddah
Clarifying that the prohibition is due to lineage, not iddah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on marrying a pregnant woman from Dar al-Harb, distinguish the majority view from the minority narration, and explain the underlying rationale based on lineage.

Core Idea
Marriage to a pregnant woman from Dar al-Harb is invalid because the established lineage occupies the womb—caution prohibits, not iddah.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq refines a common misunderstanding regarding the prohibition of marrying a pregnant woman from Dar al-Harb. The matn's qualification of 'ghayr ḥā'il' (non-pregnant) might suggest the prohibition is due to iddah. However, the commentator clarifies that the true reason is the established lineage (nasab) of the previous husband, which occupies the womb with another's right. This lesson unpacks the textual reasoning and the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is marrying a pregnant woman from Dar al-Harb. Two rulings exist: the majority holds the contract is invalid (batil) until delivery; a weak narration from Abu Hanifa holds the contract is valid but intercourse is haram until delivery.
The matn restricted the ruling to non-pregnant women, but the reason is not iddah.
2
Locate the dalil
The majority's proof: the lineage (nasab) of the previous husband is established via the marital bed (firash), so the womb is occupied with another's right, thus caution (ihtiyat) prohibits marriage. The minority's proof: the harbi's semen has no sanctity, akin to that of a fornicator.
The dalil is rational (aqlī) based on the principle of firash.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
For the majority: the legal link is that firash establishes lineage, which creates a proprietary-like right in the womb. Marrying her would cause confusion of lineage (ikhtilat al-ansab), which is prohibited by the Shari'a. This is contrasted with pregnancy from fornication, where no firash exists, so marriage is valid.
The contrast with fornication confirms the illa is lineage, not iddah.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Intra-Hanafi: the narration from al-Hasan from Abu Hanifa holds the contract valid but intercourse forbidden. This narration is considered weak (ghayr sahih) and not part of the zahir al-riwayah.
The minority view is based on the lack of hurmah of harbi semen.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The text states 'the majority are upon the first, and it is the more apparent (azhar)' because when firash is established for lineage, it also establishes prohibition of marriage out of caution. The mufta bihi is the majority view of invalidity.
The tarjih authority is the later Hanafi commentators (sharihun) who corrected the first view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيد بالحائل لأن الحامل لا يصح العقد عليها حتى تضع حملها، وظاهر مفهوم الكتاب أن ذلك لأجل العدة وليس كذلك كما في غاية البيان والتبيين. وروى الحسن عن أبي حنيفة أن العقد صحيح والوطء حرام حتى تضعه لأنه لا حرمة لماء الحربي كماء الزاني، وصحح الشارحون الأول لأن النسب ثابت فكان الرحم مشغولاً بحق الغير فكان الاحتياط في منع كالوطء بخلاف الحمل من الزنا وصحح الأقطع رواية الصحة والأكثر على الأول وهو الأظهر لأنه إذا ظهر الفراش في حق النسب يظهر في حق المنع من النكاح احتياطاً.
And it is qualified by 'non-pregnant' because the pregnant woman is not valid to contract marriage upon her until she delivers her pregnancy. And the apparent implication of the book (the matn) is that this is due to iddah, but it is not so, as has been clarified in Ghayat al-Bayan wa al-Tabyin. And al-Hasan narrated from Abu Hanifa that the contract is valid but intercourse is forbidden until she delivers, because the water (semen) of a harbi has no inviolability (hurmah) like the water of a fornicator. And the commentators (sharihun) corrected the first (that the contract is invalid) because the lineage is established, so the womb is occupied with the right of another, so caution dictates prohibition, like prohibition of intercourse, unlike pregnancy from fornication. And al-Aqta' corrected the narration of validity (the second view). But the majority are upon the first (invalidity), and it is the more apparent because when the marital bed (firash) is established in terms of lineage, it is also established in terms of prohibition of marriage as a precaution.
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This passage refines the understanding of the matn's qualification of 'حائل' (non-pregnant). The author of the matn restricted the ruling on marriage of a woman from Dar al-Harb to non-pregnant women. The apparent meaning is that this is due to the iddah of a pregnant woman, but in reality, it is due to the lineage (nasab) being established. The explanation is that if a woman is pregnant from the previous harbi husband, her womb is occupied with his child, and marrying her would create confusion of lineage. Thus, caution dictates prohibition. There is a narration from Abu Hanifa via al-Hasan that the marriage contract is valid but intercourse is forbidden until delivery, as the harbi's semen has no sanctity, akin to that of a fornicator. However, the majority of commentators (sharihun) corrected this: the contract is invalid because lineage is established. al-Aqta' corrected the riwaya of validity. The predominant view is that the marriage is invalid, and this is more apparent because when firash is established for lineage, it is also established for prohibition of marriage. The passage distinguishes this from pregnancy due to fornication, where such prohibition does not apply.

Key Terms
حائل (hai'l): A woman who is not pregnant (as opposed to حامل).
فراش (firash): The marital bed, used to establish paternity; the husband's ownership of the womb.
نسب (nasab): Lineage, paternity; the established blood relationship between a child and father.
ماء الحربي (ma' al-harbi): The semen of a non-Muslim enemy combatant; considered legally impure or lacking sanctity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why do the majority of Hanafi scholars hold that marriage to a pregnant woman from Dar al-Harb is invalid?
A) Because of the iddah, which must be observed after any separation.
B) Because the lineage (nasab) is established and the womb is occupied with the right of another.
C) Because the husband is a harbi and his marriage has no legal effect.
D) Because intercourse is forbidden until delivery.

Key Takeaway
The prohibition of marrying a pregnant woman from Dar al-Harb is due to established lineage, not iddah; the majority view invalidates the contract.

Apostasy Dissolves Marriage Immediately: Ruling and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on immediate dissolution of marriage upon apostasy and subsequent issues

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that apostasy immediately dissolves the marriage according to the Hanafi school, explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on this ruling, and apply the mufta bihi position in a given scenario.

Core Idea
Apostasy severs the marriage bond instantly, just like becoming mahram.

This lesson examines the ruling on apostasy of a spouse. The Hanafi school holds that the marriage dissolves immediately upon apostasy, without waiting for iddah or a judge's ruling. However, there is a significant intra-Hanafi dispute about how to handle the situation, especially to prevent women from using apostasy as a trick to escape marriage. The commentator reviews these positions and clarifies the relied-upon fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
Find the main claim: apostasy of either spouse dissolves the marriage immediately. Note that this is the position of the apparent riwaya (zahir al-riwayah).
The matn says 'فسخ في الحال' (immediate dissolution).
2
Understand the Legal Basis
The reason is that apostasy is a manafi (contradicting factor) that inherently severs the marriage bond, analogous to becoming mahram (e.g., through fosterage).
This is a rational proof (dalil aqli) based on the principle that a manafi nullifies a contract instantly.
3
Recognize the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Some scholars from Balkh and Samarqand held that apostasy does not dissolve the marriage immediately, to prevent women from using it as a trick to flee marriage. The majority from Bukhara held that it does dissolve, but the woman is forced to return to Islam and renew the marriage.
This dispute is about the policy of sadd al-dhara'i (blocking the means to evil) versus upholding the legal effect of manafi.
4
Analyze the Hanafi Response
The Bukhara position responds to the trickery concern by forcing the apostate to renew the marriage, thus achieving the closure without ignoring the contradicting factor. The continuation of marriage with a manafi is unheard of in the law.
The commentator rebuts a criticism from Jam' al-Fusulayn that forced renewal is also against the law, citing precedents for compelling marriage due to necessity.
5
Apply the Mufta Bihi Position
In practice, the relied-upon fatwa is immediate dissolution and forced renewal. The judge may also impose ta'zir (up to 75 lashes according to Abu Yusuf). If the husband does not request renewal, the marriage ends permanently.
This ruling also covers slavery issues: in Dar al-Islam, the apostate woman is not enslaved per zahir al-riwaya, but a mufti may use the nawadir for necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وارتداد أحدهما فسخ في الحال يعني فلا يتوقف على مضي ثلاثة قروء في المدخول بها، ولا على قضاء القاضي لأن وجود المنافي يوجبه كالمحرمية بخلاف الإسلام لأنه غير مناف للعصمة. أطلقه فشمل ارتداد المرأة وهو ظاهر الرواية، وبعض مشايخ بلخ ومشايخ سمرقند أفتوا بعدم الفرقة بردتها حسماً لباب المعصية والحيلة للخلاص منه، وعامة مشايخ بخاري أفتوا بالفرقة لكنها تجبر على الإسلام والنكاح مع زوجها الأول لأن الحسم يحصل بهذا الجبر فلا ضرورة إلى إسقاط اعتبار المنافي. وتعقبهم في جامع الفصولين بأن جبر الحرة لبالغة منافٍ للشرع أيضاً فلزمهم ما هربوا منه من إسقاط اعتبار المنافي ا هـ. وهو مردود لأن الجبر على النكاح عهد في الشرع في الجملة للضرورة كما في العبد والأمة والحر الصغير والحرة الصغيرة فجاز ارتكابه في غيرهم للضرورة ولم يعهد بقاء النكاح مع المنافي له لفظه وساق المسألة عنه بنحو ما ساقه المؤلف ثم قال : وهذا لا غبار عليه والظاهر أن ما وقع في نسخة صاحب الفتح تحريف والصواب ما أسمعتك قوله : (ما إذا خرجت مسلمة أو ذمية) وكذا إذا في دارنا أو صارت ذمية . أسلمت قوله : (وظاهر مفهوم الكتاب الخ قال الباقاني في شرح الملتقى : هذا الخلاف يتحقق في لأن الحق فافترقا . قالوا : ولكل قاض أن يجدد النكاح بمهر يسير ولو بدينار رضيت أو لا وتعزر خمسة وسبعين ا هـ . وهو اختيار لقول أبي يوسف في التعزير هنا فإن نهايته في تعزير الحرب عنده خمسة وسبعون، وعندهما تسعة وثلاثون مع أن القدسي في الحاوي قال بعد قول أبي يوسف المذكور وبه نأخذ. فعلى هذا المعتمد في نهاية التعزير قول أبي يوسف، سواء كان في تعزير المرتدة أو لا، وصحح في المحيط والخزانة ظاهر الرواية من وقوع الفرقة والجبر على تجديد النكاح من الأول وعدم تزوجها بغيره بعد إسلامها وقال الولوالجي: وعليه الفتوى. ولا يخفى أن محله ما إذا طلب الأول ذلك، أما إذا رضي بتزوجها من غيره فهو صحيح له، وكذلك لو لم يطلب تجديد النكاح واستمر ساكتاً لا يجدده القاضي حيث أخرجها من بيته. وفي القنية : المرتدة ما دامت في دار الإسلام فإنها لا تسترق في ظاهر الرواية. وفي النوادر عن أبي حنيفة أنها تسترق، ولو كان الزوج عالماً استولى عليها بعد الردة تكون فيئاً للمسلمين عند أبي حنيفة، ثم يشتريها من الإمام أو يصرفها إليه إن كان مصرفاً، فلو أفتى مفت بهذه الرواية حسماً لهذا الأمر لا بأس به :قلت وفي زماننا بعد فتنة التتر العامة صارت هذه الولايات التي غلبوا عليها وأجروا أحكامهم فيها كخوارزم وما وراء النهر وخراسان ونحوها صارت دار الحرب في الظاهر، فلو استولى عليها الزوج بعد الردة يملكها
His saying: 'And the apostasy of one of them is an immediate dissolution.' That is, it does not depend on the passing of three menstruations in the case of a consummated marriage, nor on the ruling of the judge, because the existence of a contradicting factor necessitates it, like becoming mahram. Unlike embracing Islam, because it does not contradict the bond. He made it absolute, so it includes the apostasy of the woman, and that is the apparent riwaya. And some scholars of Balkh and scholars of Samarqand gave fatwa that the separation does not occur by her apostasy, to close the door of sin and as a trick for escape from it. And the general scholars of Bukhara gave fatwa that the separation occurs, but she is forced to [embrace] Islam and to marriage with her first husband, because the closure is achieved by this compulsion, so there is no need to drop consideration of the contradicting factor. And they (the former) were criticized in Jam' al-Fusulayn that forcing a free adult woman is also against the law, so they fall into what they fled from, i.e., dropping consideration of the contradicting factor. But this is rebutted because compelling marriage is known in the law in general, due to necessity, as in the case of a slave, female slave, minor free boy, and minor free girl. So it is permissible to resort to it in other cases for necessity. And the continuation of marriage with a contradicting factor is not known. ... And they said: Every judge may renew the marriage with a small dowry, even if it is a dinar, whether she agrees or not, and he may impose a ta'zir of seventy-five [lashes]. This is a choice for the saying of Abu Yusuf regarding ta'zir here: its maximum in ta'zir of the harbi according to him is seventy-five, while according to them (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) it is thirty-nine. Although al-Qudusi in al-Hawi said after the mentioned saying of Abu Yusuf: 'And we take it.' On this, the relied-upon position regarding the maximum of ta'zir is the saying of Abu Yusuf, whether in ta'zir of the apostate or not. And it is corrected in al-Muhit and al-Khazanah that the apparent riwaya is that the separation occurs and she is forced to renew the marriage from the first [husband] and she does not marry another after her Islam. And al-Walwaliji said: 'And upon it is the fatwa.' It is not hidden that its scope is when the first husband requests that. But if he is satisfied with her marrying another, it is valid for him. Likewise, if he does not request renewal of the marriage and remains silent, the qadi does not renew it if he has removed her from his house. In al-Qunyah: The apostate woman, as long as she is in Dar al-Islam, is not enslaved according to the apparent riwaya. And in al-Nawadir from Abu Hanifa, she is enslaved. And if the husband is aware, he takes possession of her after apostasy; it becomes booty for the Muslims according to Abu Hanifa. Then he buys her from the imam or the imam assigns her to him if he is eligible. So if a mufti gives fatwa according to this riwaya to close this door, there is no harm. I say: In our time, after the general fitna of the Tatars, these territories they have overcome and enforced their rulings in, such as Khwarazm, Transoxiana, Khurasan and the like, have become Dar al-Harb in appearance. So if the husband takes possession of her after apostasy, he owns her.
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on apostasy (riddah) of a spouse and its effect on marriage. The commentator explains the matn's statement 'apostasy of one of them is an immediate dissolution' (فسخ في الحال). This means the marriage is dissolved instantly without waiting for three menstrual cycles (iddah) nor for a judge's ruling, because apostasy is a contradicting factor (manafi) that inherently severs the marriage bond, similar to becoming mahram (e.g., by fosterage). The opposite of apostasy is embracing Islam, which does not contradict the marital bond. The matn is absolute, including both husband and wife's apostasy. The commentator then reports an ikhtilaf among Hanafi scholars: some (Balkh and Samarqand) gave fatwa that apostasy does not dissolve the marriage immediately, to prevent women from using apostasy as a trick to escape marriage. However, the majority of Bukhara scholars held that the marriage is dissolved, but she is then forced to return to Islam and to renew the marriage with her first husband. This, they argue, achieves the aim of blocking the trick without ignoring the contradicting factor. A criticism from Jam' al-Fusulayn argues that forcing a free adult woman is also against Islamic law. The commentator rebuts this, stating that compelling marriage is permitted in the law for necessity, as seen with slaves and minors. Then the passage discusses the number of lashes for ta'zir: Abu Yusuf's view (75) is the relied-upon maximum, which is a choice by al-Qudusi. The commentator also mentions the issue of slavery: according to the apparent riwaya (zahir al-riwaya), an apostate woman in Dar al-Islam is not enslaved. However, in al-Nawadir, Abu Hanifa allowed it; if the husband is aware, he can take her as booty. The commentator also addresses the changing of lands to Dar al-Harb due to the Tartar invasions, which affects the ruling on the husband's right to own her.

Key Terms
ارتداد (riddah): Apostasy from Islam; to leave the religion while being a Muslim.
فسخ (faskh): Dissolution; annulment of a marriage contract without a divorce.
منافي (manafi): A contradicting factor; an element that fundamentally negates the marriage bond.
تعزير (ta'zir): Discretionary punishment for offenses that do not have fixed hadd penalties.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the zahir al-riwaya in the Hanafi school, when does a marriage dissolve upon apostasy of a spouse?
A) After three menstruations.
B) After a qadi's ruling.
C) Immediately upon apostasy.
D) Only after the spouse is forced to Islam.

Key Takeaway
Apostasy of a spouse instantaneously dissolves the marriage, and the apostate is compelled to return to Islam and renew the marriage if the other spouse desires it.

Judge's Authority to Renew Marriage after Apostasy
Commentary on judge's power to renew nikah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a Hanafi judge may compel renewal of a marriage after apostasy and identify the role of the husband's request.

Core Idea
The judge may renew the marriage only if the husband requests it; silence or consent to another marriage nullifies the judge's authority.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the judge's power in cases of apostasy. The author explains that the judge may renew the marriage contract even without the woman's consent, but only if the husband requests it. We will analyze the conditions and limits of this authority.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The judge's act of renewing the marriage is a discretionary authority (jā'iz). The text states: 'Every judge may renew the marriage with a small dower.'
2
Identify the condition of the husband's request
The text clarifies that the judge's authority is contingent upon the first husband requesting renewal. If he does not request, or if he is silent, the judge does not interfere.
Al-Walwalji: 'And upon it is the fatwa.'
3
Determine the consequence if the husband consents to another marriage
If the husband is pleased with her marrying another man, that is valid, and the judge cannot compel renewal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأن الحق فافترقا . قالوا : ولكل قاض أن يجدد النكاح بمهر يسير ولو بدينار رضيت أو لا وتعزر خمسة وسبعين ا هـ . وهو اختيار لقول أبي يوسف في التعزير هنا فإن نهايته في تعزير الحرب عنده خمسة وسبعون، وعندهما تسعة وثلاثون مع أن القدسي في الحاوي قال بعد قول أبي يوسف المذكور وبه نأخذ. فعلى هذا المعتمد في نهاية التعزير قول أبي يوسف، سواء كان في تعزير المرتدة أو لا، وصحح في المحيط والخزانة ظاهر الرواية من وقوع الفرقة والجبر على تجديد النكاح من الأول وعدم تزوجها بغيره بعد إسلامها وقال الولوالجي: وعليه الفتوى. ولا يخفى أن محله ما إذا طلب الأول ذلك، أما إذا رضي بتزوجها من غيره فهو صحيح له، وكذلك لو لم يطلب تجديد النكاح واستمر ساكتاً لا يجدده القاضي حيث أخرجها من بيته.
Because the truth... they differed. They said: Every judge may renew the marriage with a small dower, even if with a dinar, whether she consents or not, and she is given a ta'zir punishment of seventy-five. This is the choice of the saying of Abu Yusuf regarding ta'zir here, for its limit in ta'zir of the harbi according to him is seventy-five, while according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) it is thirty-nine, even though al-Qudsi in al-Hawi said after mentioning Abu Yusuf's saying: 'And we take with it.' Thus, the relied-upon in the limit of ta'zir is the saying of Abu Yusuf, whether in the ta'zir of the apostate or not. And it is authenticated in al-Muhit and al-Khazanah that the apparent of the narration (Zahir al-Riwayah) is that separation occurs and coercion to renew the marriage from the beginning, and that she cannot marry another after her Islam. Al-Walwalji said: 'And upon it is the fatwa.' It is not hidden that its place is when the first (husband) requests that. As for if he is pleased with her marrying another, it is valid for him. Similarly, if he does not request renewal of the marriage and remains silent, the judge does not renew it when the husband has expelled her from his house.
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This is a commentary passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It discusses the ruling regarding the judge's authority in the case of apostasy. The author explains that when a separation occurs due to apostasy, the judge may renew the marriage contract with a minimal mahr, even if the woman does not consent, and she receives a ta'zir punishment. The passage then details the difference of opinion between Abu Yusuf and the Sahibayn on the number of lashes for ta'zir: Abu Yusuf says 75, while the two say 39. The author notes that al-Qudsi in al-Hawi narrated Abu Yusuf's view as the one taken. The relied-upon position is Abu Yusuf's for all ta'zir, whether for apostasy or other. The passage also clarifies the conditions: the husband must request the renewal; if he accepts that she marries another, it is valid. And if he remains silent, the judge does not interfere.

Key Terms
تعزير (ta'zir): A discretionary punishment determined by the judge, not fixed by Shariah.
مهر (mahr): The dower given by the husband to the wife as part of the marriage contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view in this passage, what is the condition for the judge to renew the marriage?
A) The wife must consent.
B) The husband must request renewal.
C) The judge may act on his own discretion.
D) The wife must pay the mahr.

Key Takeaway
The judge's authority to renew a marriage after apostasis is conditional upon the husband's request; without it, the marriage ends permanently.

The Limit of Ta'zir Punishment – Abu Yusuf vs Sahibayn
Commentary on ta'zir limit for apostasy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two opinions on the ta'zir limit for an apostate woman and identify which view is relied upon in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
The relied-upon Hanafi view sets the maximum ta'zir at 75 lashes, as per Abu Yusuf, not 39.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses the ta'zir punishment for an apostate woman. The author presents an intra-Hanafi disagreement between Abu Yusuf and the Sahibayn on the maximum number of lashes. We will examine the arguments and the reason why Abu Yusuf's view is preferred.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Ta'zir is a discretionary punishment; for apostasy it is wajib. The text states she is given ta'zir of 75 lashes (according to the chosen view).
2
State the ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf says the maximum is 75 lashes, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say 39 lashes. This is based on analogy with the ta'zir of a harbi (enemy combatant).
3
Identify the tarjih and its basis
Al-Qudsi in al-Hawi narrated Abu Yusuf's view and said 'we take with it'. This is further supported by al-Muhit and al-Khazanah. The author concludes that the relied-upon view is Abu Yusuf's for all ta'zir, not limited to apostasy.
The tarjih authority is al-Qudsi and later Hanafi masters.
4
Note the scope
The chosen limit applies to all cases of ta'zir, not just apostasy, as the text says 'whether in ta'zir of the apostate or not'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأن الحق فافترقا . قالوا : ولكل قاض أن يجدد النكاح بمهر يسير ولو بدينار رضيت أو لا وتعزر خمسة وسبعين ا هـ . وهو اختيار لقول أبي يوسف في التعزير هنا فإن نهايته في تعزير الحرب عنده خمسة وسبعون، وعندهما تسعة وثلاثون مع أن القدسي في الحاوي قال بعد قول أبي يوسف المذكور وبه نأخذ. فعلى هذا المعتمد في نهاية التعزير قول أبي يوسف، سواء كان في تعزير المرتدة أو لا، وصحح في المحيط والخزانة ظاهر الرواية من وقوع الفرقة والجبر على تجديد النكاح من الأول وعدم تزوجها بغيره بعد إسلامها وقال الولوالجي: وعليه الفتوى. ولا يخفى أن محله ما إذا طلب الأول ذلك، أما إذا رضي بتزوجها من غيره فهو صحيح له، وكذلك لو لم يطلب تجديد النكاح واستمر ساكتاً لا يجدده القاضي حيث أخرجها من بيته.
Because the truth... they differed. They said: Every judge may renew the marriage with a small dower, even if with a dinar, whether she consents or not, and she is given a ta'zir punishment of seventy-five. This is the choice of the saying of Abu Yusuf regarding ta'zir here, for its limit in ta'zir of the harbi according to him is seventy-five, while according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) it is thirty-nine, even though al-Qudsi in al-Hawi said after mentioning Abu Yusuf's saying: 'And we take with it.' Thus, the relied-upon in the limit of ta'zir is the saying of Abu Yusuf, whether in the ta'zir of the apostate or not. And it is authenticated in al-Muhit and al-Khazanah that the apparent of the narration (Zahir al-Riwayah) is that separation occurs and coercion to renew the marriage from the beginning, and that she cannot marry another after her Islam. Al-Walwalji said: 'And upon it is the fatwa.' It is not hidden that its place is when the first (husband) requests that. As for if he is pleased with her marrying another, it is valid for him. Similarly, if he does not request renewal of the marriage and remains silent, the judge does not renew it when the husband has expelled her from his house.
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This is a commentary passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It discusses the ruling regarding the judge's authority in the case of apostasy. The author explains that when a separation occurs due to apostasy, the judge may renew the marriage contract with a minimal mahr, even if the woman does not consent, and she receives a ta'zir punishment. The passage then details the difference of opinion between Abu Yusuf and the Sahibayn on the number of lashes for ta'zir: Abu Yusuf says 75, while the two say 39. The author notes that al-Qudsi in al-Hawi narrated Abu Yusuf's view as the one taken. The relied-upon position is Abu Yusuf's for all ta'zir, whether for apostasy or other. The passage also clarifies the conditions: the husband must request the renewal; if he accepts that she marries another, it is valid. And if he remains silent, the judge does not interfere.

Key Terms
تعزير (ta'zir): A discretionary punishment determined by the judge, not fixed by Shariah.
حدث (hadd): Fixed punishment prescribed by Shariah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the relied-upon maximum ta'zir punishment according to this passage?
A) 39 lashes
B) 75 lashes
C) 100 lashes
D) It is not fixed.

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi for the maximum ta'zir is 75 lashes, based on Abu Yusuf's view, as confirmed by later Hanafi authorities.

Enslavement of the Apostate Woman: Zahir al-Riwayah vs. Nawadir
Commentary on intra-Hanafi dispute about enslaving an apostate woman and husband's right to possess her

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on enslaving an apostate woman and explain when the Nawadir narration may be relied upon in fatwa.

Core Idea
The apostate woman is not enslaved in Dar al-Islam per Zahir al-Riwayah, but a Nawadir narration allows it; fatwa may follow this to prevent mischief.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses an important intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the legal status of a woman who apostatizes from Islam. The disagreement centers on whether she can be enslaved, and if so, how her husband may acquire ownership. The text also tackles a practical scenario in lands that have effectively become Dar al-Harb, and the implications for the husband's rights, including the special case of a woman who had already borne her husband a child before apostasy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two narrated positions
State the Zahir al-Riwayah position (she is not enslaved in Dar al-Islam) and the Nawadir position from Abu Hanifa (she is enslaved).
2
Understand the husband’s acquisition
Explain the difference between Dar al-Islam and Dar al-Harb: in Dar al-Islam, the husband must buy her from the imam; in Dar al-Harb, he owns her directly.
3
Recognize the authority hierarchy
Note that Zahir al-Riwayah is the standard, but the Nawadir is given as a basis for fatwa to prevent harm, as cited from Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi.
4
Apply the umm walad exception
Determine that if the woman had borne a child to the husband before apostasy, she is treated as umm walad and cannot be sold, even if enslaved according to Nawadir.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي القنية : المرتدة ما دامت في دار الإسلام فإنها لا تسترق في ظاهر الرواية. وفي النوادر عن أبي حنيفة أنها تسترق، ولو كان الزوج عالماً استولى عليها بعد الردة تكون فيئاً للمسلمين عند أبي حنيفة، ثم يشتريها من الإمام أو يصرفها إليه إن كان مصرفاً، فلو أفتى مفت بهذه الرواية حسماً لهذا الأمر لا بأس به :قلت وفي زماننا بعد فتنة التتر العامة صارت هذه الولايات التي غلبوا عليها وأجروا أحكامهم فيها كخوارزم وما وراء النهر وخراسان ونحوها صارت دار الحرب في الظاهر، فلو استولى عليها الزوج بعد الردة يملكها ولا يحتاج إلى شرائها من الإمام فيفتى بحكم الرق حسماً لكيد الجهلة ومكر المكرة على ما أشار إليه في السير الكبير ا هـ . ما في القنية، وهكذا في خزانة الفتاوي ونقل قوله فلو أفتى مفت بهذه الرواية عن شمس الأئمة السرخسي . ثم اعلم أن على هذه الرواية للزوج أن يبيعها بعد الاستيلاء لأنه صار مالكاً لها، وينبغي أن يمتنع بيعها إذا كانت ولدت منه قبل الردة تنزيلاً لها منزلة أم ولده، وقد ذكر في الخانية أن أم الولد إذا ارتدت ولحقت بدار الحرب ثم سبيت ثم ملكها السيد يعود كونها أم ولده وأمية الولد تتكرر بتكرار الملك.
In al-Qunyah: The apostate woman as long as she is in Dar al-Islam, she is not enslaved according to Zahir al-Riwayah. And in Nawadir from Abu Hanifa that she is enslaved. And if the husband is knowledgeable, he takes possession of her after apostasy, she becomes fay' for the Muslims according to Abu Hanifa. Then he buys her from the imam or the imam transfers her to him if he is a recipient. So if a mufti gives fatwa by this narration to cut off this matter, there is no harm. I say: In our time after the general fitna of the Tatars, these territories that they overcame and implemented their laws in them, like Khwarazm, Ma wara' al-nahr, Khurasan and the like, became Dar al-Harb in appearance. So if the husband takes possession of her after apostasy, he owns her and does not need to buy her from the imam. So fatwa is given by the ruling of slavery to cut off the trickery of the ignorant and the deceit of the deceitful, as indicated in al-Siyar al-Kabir. This is in al-Qunyah, and similarly in Khazanat al-Fatawa, and the saying 'so if a mufti gives fatwa by this narration' is transmitted from Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi. Then know that according to this narration, the husband may sell her after taking possession because he has become her owner. But it is appropriate that he refrain from selling her if she gave birth to a child from him before apostasy, treating her in the position of umm walad. And it has been mentioned in al-Khaniyyah that the umm walad if she apostatizes and joins Dar al-Harb, then is captured, then the master takes possession of her, her status of being umm walad returns, and the status of umm walad is repeated by repetition of ownership.
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq deals with an important intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the status of an apostate woman. According to Zahir al-Riwayah (the six core books of Imam Muhammad), the apostate woman who remains in Dar al-Islam is not enslaved. However, there is a narration (Nawadir) from Imam Abu Hanifa that she is enslaved. The husband who knows this may take possession of her after her apostasy, but according to Abu Hanifa, she becomes fay' (booty) for the Muslims, so he must buy her from the imam. The passage then addresses a practical situation in the author's time: after the Mongol invasions, regions like Khwarazm, Transoxiana, and Khurasan had become Dar al-Harb effectively. In that case, the husband can take possession without buying from the imam. The author notes that fatwa may be given based on this narration to prevent evil. The passage also discusses the implications for the husband's right to sell her, except if she had already borne him a child before apostasy, in which case she is considered umm walad (mother of his child) and cannot be sold.

Key Terms
ظاهر الرواية (Zahir al-Riwayah): The well-known transmitted rulings from the six core books of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani.
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who has borne a child to her master, thus gaining certain rights and cannot be sold.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Nawadir narration, what must the husband do if he takes possession of his apostate wife in Dar al-Islam?
A) He automatically owns her without any further action.
B) He must buy her from the imam or the imam transfers her to him.
C) He must divorce her first.
D) He must pay a fine to the state.

Key Takeaway
The apostate woman's enslavement is disputed within the Hanafi school; the mufti may rely on the Nawadir to prevent mischief, but the umm walad exception always prevents sale.

intermediate

Apostasy as Faskh: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute and Its Legal Consequences
A ruling with intra-Hanafi dispute and talil on the nature of apostasy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to STATE the positions of Abu Hanifa, Muhammad, and Abu Yusuf on the nature of apostasy in marriage, and EXPLAIN the talil (causal reasoning) behind Abu Hanifa's view that apostasy is a faskh, not a talaq.

Core Idea
Apostasy annuls not divorces – the Hanafi majority sees it as faskh, preserving the count of divorces.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a critical ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school: whether apostasy dissolves marriage through faskh (annulment) or talaq (divorce). Abu Hanifa argues that apostasy contradicts the very bond of protection (ismah), making it a faskh, while his students differ. Understanding this distinction affects how many divorces remain available and whether a muhallil is needed for remarriage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Legal Ruling
State the main ruling: apostasy dissolves marriage through faskh according to Abu Hanifa, but is considered talaq by Muhammad and faskh by Abu Yusuf.
Focus on the core classification, not the details yet.
2
Analyze the Talil (Causal Reasoning)
Examine Abu Hanifa's reasoning: apostasy contradicts ismah (the bond of marriage), while talaq lifts (rafa') the bond. Since apostasy is a contradiction, it cannot be a lift, hence it is faskh.
Compare with the case of refusal (iba') where the husband refuses to embrace Islam, which is talaq because it fails to retain with kindness.
3
Map the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
List the three imams' positions: Abu Hanifa says apostasy = faskh; Muhammad says apostasy = talaq (like refusal); Abu Yusuf says apostasy = faskh (following his principle that refusal is faskh).
Recognize that Abu Yusuf is consistent with his own usul.
4
Determine the Practical Consequence
Faskh does not reduce the number of divorces. Thus, repeated apostasy and reconciliation is possible without needing a muhallil. If apostasy were talaq, each occurrence would consume one of the three divorces.
This is the key practical difference (thamarah).
5
Identify the Mufta Bihi
The relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa, as stated in al-Khaniyyah and supported by the majority of later Hanafi scholars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الخانية من باب الردة: رجل تزوج امرأة فغاب عنها قبل الدخول بها فأخبره مخبر أنها ارتدت والمخبر حر أو مملوك أو محدود في قذف ثقة عنده وسعه أن يصدقه ويتزوج أربعاً سواها، وكذا إذا كان غير ثقة وأكبر رأيه أنه صادق، وإن كان أكبر رأيه أنه كاذب لا أكثر يتزوج من وإن أخبرت المرأة أن زوجها قد ارتد لها أن تتزوج بآخر بعد انقضاء العدة في رواية الاستحسان، وفي رواية السير ليس لها أن تتزوج قال شمس الأئمة السرخسي : الأصح رواية الاستحسان ا هـ . وهو ثلاث، وإنما كانت ردته فسخاً وإباؤه طلاقاً عند أبي حنيفة لأن الردة منافية للنكاح لكونها منافية للعصمة والطلاق رافع فتعذر أن يجعل طلاقاً بخلاف الإباء فإنه يفوت الإمساك بالمعروف فيجب التسريح بالإحسان ولذا يتوقف على القضاء في الإباء دونها. وقال محمد: إن ردته طلاق كإبائه وأبو يوسف مر على أصله من أن إباءه فسخ فردته كذلك. وأفاد بقوله فسخ» أنه لا ينقص العدد ولذا قال في الخانية : رجل ارتد مراراً وجدد الإسلام في كل مرة وجدد النكاح على قول أبي حنيفة تحل امرأته من غير إصابة زوج ثان، ولم يذكر المؤلف العدة عليها ولا شك في وجوبها . قال في جامع الفصولين : وتعتد بثلاث حيض لو حرة ممن تحيض، وبثلاثة أشهر لو آيسة أو صغيرة، وبوضع الحمل و حاملاً لو دخل، سواء ارتد أو ارتدت، ولا نفقة لها في العدة ولو ارتد هو لا تجبر المرأة على التزوج اهـ. وفي الخلاصة : إذا ارتدت لا نفقة لها في العدة ولها السكنى وبه يفتى ذكره في ألفاظ التكفير . وفي الخانية : ولزوج المرتدة أن يتزوج بأختها وأربع سواها إذا لحقت بالدار كأنها ماتت، فإن خرجت إلى دار الإسلام مسلمة بعد ذلك لا يفسد نكاح أختها إذا ارتدت المعتدة ولحقت بدار وجوب أئمة بخاري لا مما في النواد تأمل قوله : (يملكها الخ) أي على ظاهر الرواية حيث كانت الدار دار حرب قوله : (وتعتد بثلاث حيض الخ أقول : ويلحقها الطلاق لو أوقعه في العدة إلا إذا لحق بدار الحرب لما سيأتي قبيل باب تفويض الطلاق عن البدائع ونصه : وإذا ارتد ولحق بدار الحرب وطلقها في العدة لم يقع لانقطاع العصمة فإن عاد إلى دار الإسلام وهي في العدة وقع، وإذا ارتدت ولحقت لم يقع عليها طلاقه، فإن عادت قبل الحيض لم يقع كذلك عند أبي حنيفة لبطلان العدة باللحاق ثم لا
In al-Khaniyyah from the chapter of apostasy: A man married a woman and he was absent before consummation, then an informer informed him that she had apostatized. If the informer is free, a slave, or one who has been flogged for qadhf (false accusation) but is trustworthy in his view, it is permissible for him to believe him and marry four others besides her. Similarly, if he is not trustworthy but his strong opinion is that he is truthful, [he may do so]. If his strong opinion is that he is lying, he does not marry more than [three?]. And if the woman informs that her husband has apostatized, she may marry another after the expiration of the 'iddah according to the riwaya of istihsan. In the riwaya of al-Siyar, she may not marry. Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi said: The correct is the riwaya of istihsan. This is three. And the apostasy of the husband is a faskh (annulment) and his refusal [to embrace Islam] is a talaq (divorce) according to Abu Hanifa, because apostasy contradicts marriage as it contradicts protection (ismah), and divorce is a lift (raafi'), so it is difficult to make it a talaq, unlike refusal, for it causes failure to retain with kindness, so it is obligatory to release with goodness. Therefore, it is dependent on the judge in refusal without apostasy. Muhammad said: His apostasy is talaq like his refusal, and Abu Yusuf followed his principle that his refusal is faskh, so his apostasy is also faskh. And he stated by his saying 'faskh' that it does not reduce the number [of divorces]. Therefore, it is said in al-Khaniyyah: A man apostatized repeatedly and renewed Islam each time and renewed the marriage; according to Abu Hanifa, his wife becomes permissible to him without the intervening of a second husband. The author did not mention the 'iddah upon her, but there is no doubt about its obligation. It is said in Jami' al-Fusulayn: She observes 'iddah of three menstrual cycles if she is free and menstruates, three months if she is past menopause or young, and until delivery if she is pregnant and he had consummated, whether he apostatized or she apostatized, and there is no nafaqah for her during 'iddah. Even if he apostatized, the woman is not compelled to marry. In al-Khulasa: If she apostatizes, there is no nafaqah for her during 'iddah, but she has the right to housing, and it is with this that fatwa is given, mentioned in Alfaz al-Takfir. In al-Khaniyyah: The husband of the apostate woman may marry her sister and four others besides her if she has joined the dar (enemy territory), as if she died. If she then comes out to Dar al-Islam as a Muslim, that does not invalidate the marriage of her sister. If the apostate woman in 'iddah apostatizes and joins the dar... [text continues with marginal comments on ownership and 'iddah]
Source pages p. 375

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq covers several related rulings on the effects of apostasy on marriage. It begins with a ruling from al-Khaniyyah: if a man is informed by a trustworthy person that his wife has apostatized while he is absent, he may believe that report and marry four other women. The same applies if the wife reports her husband's apostasy, but there is a difference of riwaya: according to istihsan she may remarry after 'iddah, while the riwaya of al-Siyar says she cannot. Imam al-Sarakhsi declares the istihsan riwaya as correct. The passage then discusses the legal nature of apostasy in marriage: Abu Hanifa holds that the husband's apostasy is a faskh (annulment), not a talaq, because apostasy destroys the marital bond, while divorce lifts it; they are different. His refusal to return to Islam, however, is considered talaq because it is a failure to keep his wife with kindness. Imam Muhammad considers both apostasy and refusal as talaq, while Abu Yusuf considers both as faskh. The practical consequence of Abu Hanifa's view is that apostasy does not reduce the number of divorces, so a man can apostatize multiple times and remarry his wife without need for a muhallil. The passage then details the 'iddah for an apostate woman: she observes three menstrual cycles if free and menstruating, three months if past menopause or young, and if pregnant, until delivery. There is no nafaqah during her 'iddah, but she has the right to housing. Additionally, the husband of an apostate woman may marry her sister and four others if she has joined enemy territory, as if she were dead. The text ends with marginal notes about ownership, 'iddah, and the effect of talaq pronounced during 'iddah when the apostate has joined dar al-harb.

Key Terms
فسخ (faskh): Annulment of the marriage contract without affecting the count of divorces.
عصمة (ismah): The protection and bond of marriage; apostasy contradicts it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is the legal nature of apostasy in marriage?
A) It is a divorce (talaq) that reduces the number of available divorces.
B) It is an annulment (faskh) that does not reduce the number of divorces.
C) It is equivalent to the husband's refusal to embrace Islam.
D) It automatically results in permanent separation without any 'iddah.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, apostasy dissolves marriage through faskh, preserving the count of divorces, based on the reasoning that apostasy contradicts the marital bond (ismah) rather than lifting it.

Acting on News of Apostasy: Remarriage Rights and 'Iddah Obligations
A ruling on conditions for remarriage based on news of apostasy and 'iddah rules

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to APPLY the conditions under which a spouse may remarry based on news of the other's apostasy, and STATE the 'iddah rules for an apostate woman according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Credible news of apostasy allows remarriage, but only after 'iddah with no nafaqah — only housing.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq also addresses a practical scenario: when a man receives news that his wife has apostatized, under what conditions may he marry another woman? Similarly, when a woman learns of her husband's apostasy, when may she remarry? The rulings involve assessment of the informant's trustworthiness and a riwaya ikhtilaf. Additionally, the passage details the 'iddah for an apostate woman, clarifying that she receives housing but not nafaqah, and the duration of 'iddah varies based on her condition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
A man is absent; news arrives that his wife apostatized. The ruling depends on the informant's credibility and the husband's strong opinion.
Also the converse: wife receives news of husband's apostasy.
2
Analyze the Conditions for Remarriage
If the informant is free, slave, or even a hadd-punished person but deemed trustworthy, the husband may believe and marry four others. If not trustworthy but his strong opinion leans truthful, same. If he thinks it's false, he cannot marry another.
The text says 'لا أكثر يتزوج من' possibly meaning he does not marry more than what? Possibly he cannot marry at all? The translation says he does not marry more than three? But the meaning is he cannot marry another because the first marriage is still intact. We'll interpret: he cannot marry others.
3
Examine the Riwaya Ikhtilaf for the Wife's Remarriage
If the wife receives news of her husband's apostasy, there are two riwayas: istihsan allows her to remarry after 'iddah; al-Siyar does not. Sarakhsi chooses istihsan.
This shows the role of istihsan in tarjih.
4
Detail the 'Iddah for an Apostate Woman
She must observe 'iddah of three menstrual cycles (if free and menstruating), three months (if menopausal or young), or until delivery (if pregnant and consummated). This applies whether he or she apostatized.
No nafaqah during 'iddah, but she has the right to housing (sukna).
5
Additional Rulings on the Husband's Rights
If the apostate wife has joined enemy territory (dar al-harb), the husband may marry her sister and up to four others, as if she died. If she returns Muslim, it does not invalidate the new marriage.
This is based on the principle that apostasy severs the marital bond completely.

The Legal Nature of Apostasy's Effect on Marriage: Faskh or Talaq
Explains intra-Hanafi dispute on apostasy's effect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on whether apostasy dissolves marriage as faskh or talaq and explain the practical consequence regarding the divorce count.

Core Idea
Apostasy dissolves marriage as a faskh, not talaq, preserving the husband's remaining divorces.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq delves into a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute: when a husband apostatizes, does his marriage end as a dissolution (faskh) or a divorce (talaq)? The answer has direct consequences on whether the number of divorces is reduced. Abu Hanifa's view treats apostasy as a fundamental incompatibility, while Muhammad al-Shaybani equates it with refusal to return to Islam. Let's unpack the reasoning and its practical application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core dispute
What is the legal nature of apostasy's effect on marriage? Is it a faskh (dissolution) or a talaq (divorce)?
The text presents three positions: Abu Hanifa, Muhammad, and Abu Yusuf.
2
Compare Abu Hanifa's and Muhammad's positions
Abu Hanifa distinguishes apostasy from refusal (iba'), making apostasy faskh and refusal talaq. Muhammad equates the two, treating both as talaq.
Abu Yusuf follows his own logic: refusal is faskh, so apostasy is also faskh.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's talil
Apostasy negates isma (protection), making marriage impossible to maintain; talaq requires an existing basis. Refusal fails to keep with kindness, so it becomes a divorce requiring judicial order.
Apostasy does not need a judge; refusal does.
4
Apply the practical consequence
If faskh, the divorce count is not reduced; if talaq, it counts toward the three allowed divorces.
This affects remarriage: after faskh, wife is permissible without a second husband; after talaq, after three, she is permanently prohibited.
5
Relate to the example from al-Khaniyah
A man apostatizes repeatedly, renews Islam, and renews marriage. According to Abu Hanifa, each apostasy is a faskh, so the wife remains permissible without a second husband.
Under Muhammad's view, three apostasies would be three talaqs, making her permanently forbidden.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما كانت ردته فسخاً وإباؤه طلاقاً عند أبي حنيفة لأن الردة منافية للنكاح لكونها منافية للعصمة والطلاق رافع فتعذر أن يجعل طلاقاً بخلاف الإباء فإنه يفوت الإمساك بالمعروف فيجب التسريح بالإحسان ولذا يتوقف على القضاء في الإباء دونها. وقال محمد: إن ردته طلاق كإبائه وأبو يوسف مر على أصله من أن إباءه فسخ فردته كذلك. وأفاد بقوله فسخ» أنه لا ينقص العدد ولذا قال في الخانية : رجل ارتد مراراً وجدد الإسلام في كل مرة وجدد النكاح على قول أبي حنيفة تحل امرأته من غير إصابة زوج ثان.
And indeed his apostasy is a dissolution and his refusal to return to Islam is a divorce according to Abu Hanifa, because apostasy is incompatible with marriage due to its incompatibility with protection, and divorce is a removal, so it is impossible to make it a divorce, unlike refusal, for it causes the loss of retention with kindness, so it is necessary to release with goodness, and therefore it depends on the judge in refusal without apostasy. And Muhammad said: His apostasy is a divorce like his refusal, and Abu Yusuf adhered to his principle that his refusal is a dissolution, so his apostasy is likewise. And by saying 'dissolution' he indicated that it does not reduce the number, and thus he said in al-Khaniyah: A man apostatized repeatedly, renewing Islam each time and renewing the marriage, according to Abu Hanifa's view, his wife becomes permissible to him without a second husband.
Source pages p. 375

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the legal effect of apostasy on a marriage. The issue is whether apostasy constitutes a dissolution (faskh) or a divorce (talaq). Abu Hanifa argues that apostasy is a dissolution rather than a divorce because apostasy negates the protection that upholds marriage, while divorce is an act that lifts a marriage that still has some basis. He contrasts this with the case of the husband's refusal to return to Islam after being ordered by a judge, which he considers a divorce because it establishes the failure of marital companionship. For apostasy, no judicial order is needed; it automatically ends the marriage. Muhammad al-Shaybani holds that apostasy is a divorce, just like refusal, because both involve the husband's rejection of Islam. Abu Yusuf follows his established principle that refusal is a dissolution, so apostasy is also a dissolution. The practical consequence is that if apostasy is a dissolution, it does not reduce the number of divorces the husband can give later. The text illustrates this with a case from al-Khaniyah: a man repeatedly apostatizes and returns to Islam, his marriage can be renewed without needing the wife to marry another man first, according to Abu Hanifa's view.

Key Terms
فسخ (faskh): In this context, legal dissolution of marriage without counting as a divorce; it annuls the marriage contract retroactively from the time of apostasy.
طلاق (talaq): Divorce pronounced by the husband; a revocable or irrevocable dissolution that counts towards the three divorces.
إباء (iba'): The husband's refusal to return to Islam when ordered by the judge, which constitutes a divorce according to Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is the legal effect of a husband's apostasy on his marriage?
A) It is a divorce (talaq)
B) It is a dissolution (faskh)
C) It has no effect
D) It requires a judge's ruling

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, apostasy dissolves marriage as a faskh, not talaq, preserving the husband's remaining divorces and allowing remarriage without a second husband.

The Obligatory 'Iddah for Apostasy and Its Duration
Expansion on 'iddah rulings for apostasy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the 'iddah obligations for a woman separated by apostasy and identify the duration based on her physical state.

Core Idea
Apostasy triggers wajib 'iddah, but the apostate wife receives no maintenance — only housing.

In this passage, the commentator expands on the matn by clarifying the waiting period for a woman whose husband apostatizes or who apostatizes herself. Although the matn did not explicitly mention it, the 'iddah is obligatory. The duration depends on whether she menstruates, is postmenopausal, a minor, or pregnant. Additionally, the apostate wife is not entitled to maintenance but is entitled to housing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the 'iddah for a woman separated by apostasy. The ruling is wajib (obligatory).
The matn did not mention it, but the commentator affirms it.
2
Determine the duration based on status
For a free woman who menstruates: three menstrual cycles. For a postmenopausal woman or minor: three months. For a pregnant woman: until delivery.
These durations are standard for non-menstruating or pregnant women.
3
Note the financial consequences
The apostate wife receives no maintenance during 'iddah, but she is entitled to housing.
The relied-upon fatwa from al-Khulasa grants housing.
4
Recognize applicability
These rules apply whether the husband apostatized or the wife apostatized (sawaa irtadda aw irtaddat).
The text explicitly covers both scenarios.
5
Understand the authority
The commentator quotes Jami' al-Fusulayn and al-Khulasa as sources for these rulings.
These are authoritative Hanafi texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولم يذكر المؤلف العدة عليها ولا شك في وجوبها . قال في جامع الفصولين : وتعتد بثلاث حيض لو حرة ممن تحيض، وبثلاثة أشهر لو آيسة أو صغيرة، وبوضع الحمل و حاملاً لو دخل، سواء ارتد أو ارتدت، ولا نفقة لها في العدة ولو ارتد هو لا تجبر المرأة على التزوج اهـ. وفي الخلاصة : إذا ارتدت لا نفقة لها في العدة ولها السكنى وبه يفتى ذكره في ألفاظ التكفير . وفي الخانية : ولزوج المرتدة أن يتزوج بأختها وأربع سواها إذا لحقت بالدار كأنها ماتت، فإن خرجت إلى دار الإسلام مسلمة بعد ذلك لا يفسد نكاح أختها إذا ارتدت المعتدة ولحقت بدار الحرب ثم قضى القاضي بلحاقها بطلت عدتها لتباين الدارين وانقطاع العصمة كأنها ماتت، فإن رجعت إلينا بعد ذلك مسلمة قبل انقضاء مدة العدة والحيض قال أبو يوسف : لا تعود معتدة. وقال محمد: تعود معتدة .اهـ.
The author did not mention the waiting period for her, but there is no doubt about its obligation. He said in Jami' al-Fusulayn: She observes 'iddah with three menstrual cycles if she is a free woman among those who menstruate, and with three months if she is postmenopausal or a minor, and by giving birth if she is pregnant, whether he apostatized or she apostatized, and there is no maintenance for her during the 'iddah, and if he apostatized, the woman is not forced to marry. In al-Khulasa: If she apostatized, there is no maintenance for her during the 'iddah and she has the right to housing, and according to this fatwa is given, mentioned in Alfaz al-Takfir. In al-Khaniyah: The husband of an apostate woman may marry her sister and four others if she has joined the enemy territory as if she had died. If she later comes out to the abode of Islam as a Muslim, that does not invalidate the marriage of her sister. If the woman observing 'iddah apostatized and joined the enemy territory, then the judge ruled her to have joined, her 'iddah becomes void due to the difference of the two abodes and the severance of protection as if she had died. If she returns to us after that as a Muslim before the expiry of the period of 'iddah and menstruation, Abu Yusuf said: She does not resume 'iddah. And Muhammad said: She resumes 'iddah.
Source pages p. 375

Commentary
This passage establishes the rulings related to the 'iddah of a woman whose husband apostatizes (or she apostatizes). The author notes that the waiting period is obligatory, even if not explicitly mentioned in the matn. The details from Jami' al-Fusulayn specify the duration based on the woman's status: three menstrual cycles for a free woman, three months for a minor or postmenopausal woman, and until delivery if pregnant. This applies regardless of which spouse apostatized. Importantly, the apostate woman receives no maintenance during her 'iddah, only housing according to al-Khulasa, and this is the relied-upon fatwa. The husband of an apostate wife is permitted to marry her sister and up to four wives simultaneously if she has gone to dar al-harb; her absence is equated to death. If she returns as a Muslim, the new marriage to her sister remains valid. The passage then addresses a scenario where the apostate woman joins dar al-harb while in 'iddah; the judge's ruling on her joining voids the 'iddah. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on whether she resumes her 'iddah if she returns as a Muslim before the 'iddah period would have ended: Abu Yusuf says no, Muhammad says yes.

Key Terms
عدة ('iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after separation from her husband, during which she cannot remarry.
دار الحرب (dar al-harb): Territory under non-Muslim rule with which there is a state of war; joining it severs marital ties.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) One menstrual cycle
B) Three menstrual cycles
C) Four months and ten days
D) No 'iddah required

Key Takeaway
The 'iddah for apostasy is wajib, with durations varying by the woman's physical state, and she is entitled to housing but not maintenance.

The Effect of Dar al-Harb on 'Iddah and the Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Resumption
Expansion on consequences of ikhtilaf al-dar and intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the legal consequences of ikhtilaf al-dar on 'iddah and marriage, and state the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on resumption of 'iddah.

Core Idea
Joining dar al-harb severs marital ties like death; the 'iddah is void, but if she returns before the period ends, the ruling differs between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.

This part of the passage addresses what happens when the apostate spouse joins the enemy territory (dar al-harb). The husband may marry her sister and up to four other wives, as if she had died. If the woman was in 'iddah and joined dar al-harb, the judge's ruling on her joining voids the 'iddah. However, if she returns as a Muslim before the 'iddah period would have ended, there is a difference of opinion between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on whether she must resume the 'iddah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The woman apostatizes and joins dar al-harb while in 'iddah.
This is a specific case of ikhtilaf al-dar.
2
Understand the legal effect
The difference of abodes (ikhtilaf al-dar) severs the marital bond; the 'iddah becomes void as if she died.
The text says 'batalat 'iddatuha' (her 'iddah becomes void).
3
Note the husband's permissions
The husband may marry her sister and up to four wives simultaneously, because she is like a deceased person.
This is from al-Khaniyah.
4
Consider the return scenario
If she returns as a Muslim before the 'iddah period would have ended, the ruling is disputed.
The return must be to dar al-Islam as a Muslim.
5
Compare Abu Yusuf and Muhammad
Abu Yusuf says she does not resume 'iddah; Muhammad says she does resume.
The text does not specify which is mufta bihi.

Mahr Entitlement When Marriage Ends by Apostasy
Ruling on mahr when marriage ends by apostasy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the mahr entitlement for a wife when marriage is dissolved due to apostasy, distinguishing between the husband's apostasy and the wife's apostasy.

Core Idea
Apostasy by the husband forfeits full or half mahr; apostasy by the wife forfeits everything.

This lesson addresses a specific scenario in marriage dissolution: apostasy. Islamic law differentiates between apostasy committed by the husband and that by the wife, because the mahr is an obligation of the husband. Understanding who caused the separation is key to determining the wife's mahr rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the marital status
Determine whether the marriage was consummated (including valid seclusion) or not. This affects the amount of mahr due.
Valid seclusion (khalwa) is legally equivalent to consummation in Hanafi fiqh.
2
Determine the apostate
Identify which spouse committed apostasy. The rule differs based on whether it is the husband or the wife.
3
Apply the mahr rule
If the husband apostatized: full mahr if consummated, half if not. If the wife apostatized: no mahr.
Half mahr applies if a specific mahr was mentioned; otherwise, a pleasure gift (mut'a) is given.
4
Recognize the scope
The rule applies regardless of the wife's status – free or slave, adult or minor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فللموطوءة المهر ولغيرها النصف إن ارتد وإن ارتدت لا والإباء نظيره وإن ارتدا معاً أو معه . أطلقه فشمل ارتداده وارتدادها والخلوة بها لأنها وطء حكماً قوله : (ولغيرها النصف إن ارتد لأن الفرقة من قبله قبل الدخول موجبة لنصف المهر عند التسمية وللمتعة عند عدمها قوله : (وإن ارتدت (لا) أي ليس لها شيء لأن الفرقة جاءت من قبلها قبله أطلقه فشمل الحرة والأمة الكبيرة والصغيرة.
For the woman who was consummated, the mahr, and for other than her, half, if he apostatized, and if she apostatized, nothing, and refusal is its equivalent, and if both apostatized together or with him. He made it absolute, so it includes his apostasy and her apostasy and seclusion with her, because it is considered intercourse legally. His saying: 'And for other than her, half, if he apostatized' because the separation from his side before consummation necessitates half the mahr if a dower was specified, and pleasure gifts if not specified. His saying: 'And if she apostatized, nothing' means she has nothing because the separation came from her side before him. He made it absolute, so it includes the free woman and the slave, the adult and the minor.
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage provides the rule for mahr when marriage is dissolved by apostasy, based on the matn. If the husband apostatizes and the marriage was consummated (including valid seclusion which is legally equivalent to consummation), the wife is entitled to the full specified mahr. If the husband apostatizes before consummation, the wife receives half the mahr (if a mahr was specified) or a pleasure gift (mut'a) if no mahr was specified. If the wife apostatizes, she forfeits the entire mahr, because the separation originated from her side. This rule applies regardless of whether the wife is free or slave, adult or minor.

Key Terms
مهر (mahr): The bridal gift required from the husband, which becomes fully due upon consummation.
موطوءة (mawtu'a): A woman with whom marital intercourse has taken place, including valid seclusion.
خلوة (khalwa): Valid seclusion between spouses after marriage, considered a constructive consummation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Full mahr
B) Half mahr
C) Nothing
D) A pleasure gift

Key Takeaway
The mahr entitlement in apostasy depends entirely on which spouse caused the separation: husband forfeits full or half, wife forfeits all.

Weighing Claims of Apostasy and Duress Between Spouses
Procedural rules for weighing conflicting claims of apostasy and duress between spouses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rules for weighing conflicting claims of apostasy and duress between spouses and apply them to given scenarios.

Core Idea
Claims of apostasy between spouses are resolved by defaulting to the Muslim status of the speaker, but duress and narration defenses shift the burden of proof.

When a spouse claims apostasy from the other, or when a spouse tries to excuse a blasphemous statement by duress, a judge must determine whose word is accepted. The Hanafi school provides detailed procedural rules that consider the context—such as whether the utterance was under coercion or mere narration. This lesson unpacks the principles from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, showing how evidence is weighed in these sensitive family cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the claim
Determine who is making the claim of apostasy and who is denying it. Is the claim against a spouse or by a spouse? The default presumption is that Muslims remain Muslim (istishab al-Islam).
2
Assess the nature of the utterance
If the husband admits uttering words of disbelief but claims duress, his claim is not accepted without evidence (bayyinah). If he claims mere narration (hikayah), his word is accepted with an oath, provided there is context.
The wife's testimony of hearing the word is not enough; the judge requires the husband's explanation.
3
Apply the procedural maxim on burden of proof
In disputes over duress, the one alleging duress must prove it (al-bayyinah ʿala man iddaʿa). The wife's claim that there was no duress is accepted if she is present, because she has direct knowledge.
The Ḥanafīs hold that nukul (refusal to swear) creates doubt and should not lead to takfīr.
4
Handle the case of oath refusal
If the husband refuses to take an oath, the ruling is problematic: some say marriage dissolves but takfīr is not established due to doubt (shubha). The commentator notes that there is no benefit in the oath if the marriage does not dissolve, so the better view is that dissolution occurs but takfīr is avoided.
This highlights the Ḥanafī caution: takfīr is not confirmed with any doubt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسلم أسر في دار الحرب وخرج إلى دار الإسلام ومعه امرأته فقالت المرأة ارتددت في دار الحرب، فإن أنكر الزوج ذلك كان القول قوله وإن قال تكلمت بالكفر مكرهاً وقالت المرأة لم تكن مكرهاً كان القول قول المرأة، فإن صدقته المرأة فيما قال فالقاضي لا يصدقه اهـ. وهكذا في الظهيرية إلا أنه لم يقيده بكونها معه وظاهر التقييد أنه لا يقبل قولها إذا لم تكن وله وجه ظاهر لأنه لا علم لها بذلك. وصرح في التتارخانية أنه لا يقبل قوله في دعوى الإكراه إلا ببينة ولو شهدوا على الإكراه إلا أنهم قالوا لا ندري أكفر أم لا وقال الأسير إنما أجريت كلمة الكفر عند الإكراه لا قبله ولا بعده فالقول قول الأسير، ولو قالت للقاضي سمعته يقول المسيح ابن الله تعالى فقال الزوج إنما حكيت قول النصارى فإن قرأنه لم يتكلم إلا بهذه الكلمة بانت امرأته وإن قال وصلت بكلامي فقلت النصارى يقولون وكذبته المرأة فالقول قوله مع اليمين ولا يحكم بكفره وإن تكل عن اليمين حكم به اهـ. وهو مشكل إن صحت النسخة لأن النكول شبهة والتكفير لا يثبت مع الشبهة ويمكن أن يقال : إنها تبين بالنكول ولا يثبت كفره، وإن قيل لا تبين أيضاً فمشكل، لأنه حينئذ لا فائدة في التحليف مع أنه لرجاء النكول
A Muslim was captured in dar al-harb and left for dar al-Islam with his wife. The woman said: 'I apostatized in dar al-harb.' If the husband denies that, the word is his. If he said: 'I spoke disbelief under duress' and the woman said: 'You were not under duress', the word is the woman's. If the woman verifies what he said, then the judge does not believe him. Similarly in al-Zahiriyyah, except that it did not restrict it to being with him, and apparently the restriction means that her statement is not accepted if she was not with him, and that has an obvious reason because she has no knowledge of that. And it is stated explicitly in al-Tatar Khaniyyah that his claim of duress is not accepted without evidence. If they witness that he was under duress but they do not know whether he disbelieved or not, and the captive says: 'I only uttered the word of disbelief at the time of duress, not before nor after', then the word is the captive's. If she said to the judge: 'I heard him say "al-Masih is the son of Allah"', and the husband said: 'I only narrated the statement of the Christians', then if she read it and he only uttered this word, his wife becomes separated. If he said: 'I connected my speech and said "the Christians say..."', and the woman belied him, then the word is his with oath, and he is not judged a disbeliever. If he refuses the oath, he is judged a disbeliever. This is problematic if the version is correct, because refusal is a doubt, and takfir is not established with doubt. It can be said: She becomes separate by his refusal, but he is not judged a disbeliever. If it is said she does not become separate either, it is problematic, because then there is no benefit in the oath even though it is for the sake of refusal.
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage deals with procedural issues when a spouse claims apostasy or duress. The first scenario involves a captive in dar al-harb who comes to dar al-Islam with his wife; the wife claims she apostatized. The husband's denial is given precedence because the default is that she remained Muslim. If the husband claims he spoke disbelief under duress and the wife denies the duress, her claim is accepted because she is witness to the situation. However, if she confirms his duress, the judge does not accept it (perhaps because it is a legal excuse that requires evidence). The Tatarkhaniyyah explicitly states that claims of duress require proof (bayyinah). A second scenario: a woman hears her husband say a blasphemous statement; the husband's explanation that he was merely narrating is accepted with an oath. If he refuses the oath, the commentator notes a problem: refusal creates doubt, and takfir is not made with doubt. The better view is that the marriage is dissolved by his refusal but he is not declared a disbeliever.

Key Terms
إكراه (ikrah): Duress; being compelled to act against one's will; it can affect the validity of statements of disbelief.
نكول (nukul): Refusal to take an oath; it creates a presumption but not certainty.
تكفير (takfir): Declaring someone a disbeliever (kafir); a serious legal step.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a husband admits uttering words of disbelief but claims he was under duress, and his wife denies the duress, whose statement is accepted?
A) The husband's, because he is the one who uttered the words.
B) The wife's, because she was present and has knowledge of the situation.
C) Neither; the judge must investigate further.
D) The husband's, if he takes an oath.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, accusations of apostasy between spouses require careful procedural handling: claims of duress must be proven, and takfir is never based on doubt.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
7 min
Ruling on Simultaneous vs. Sequential Acceptance of Islam by Spouses
Stating ruling on marriage dissolution upon conversion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the effect of simultaneous and sequential acceptance of Islam by non-Muslim spouses, and explain the analogous ruling of ibā' (refusal) on mahr and nafaqah.

Core Idea
Simultaneous Islam keeps marriage intact; sequential Islam dissolves it.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq presents a key Hanafi ruling regarding the marriage of non-Muslim spouses who embrace Islam. The ruling distinguishes between two scenarios: both converts together or one after the other. Additionally, it introduces the concept of ibā' — when one spouse accepts Islam and the other refuses — which is treated analogously to apostasy, affecting the entitlement to mahr and maintenance. Understanding this ruling is essential for applying the Hanafi framework on marital dissolution upon conversion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage contrasts simultaneous acceptance (aslamā ma‘an) with sequential acceptance (aslamā muta‘āqibān). Note the differing outcomes: for simultaneous, the marriage continues; for sequential, it dissolves.
2
Understand the source of the ruling
The commentator notes this ruling was already stated in the chapter on slave marriage, and he relies on that.
3
Differentiate the mahr and nafaqah rulings
The passage extends the analogy: if the husband is the renegade (or refuser), the wife is entitled to nafaqat al-‘iddah; if she is the apostate, she gets no nafaqah.
4
Apply the ibā' analogy
The refusal (ibā') after one spouse converts is treated like apostasy. If refusal occurs after consummation, she gets full mahr; if before, she gets half if he refuses; if she refuses, she gets nothing.
5
Note the cross-reference
The commentator points out that this ruling was explicitly stated earlier in the book, indicating the coherent system of Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب النكاح باب نكاح الكافر أسلماً معاً لم تبن وبانت لو أسلما متعاقبا . قدمنا التصريح بذلك في باب نكاح الرقيق في شرح قوله ويسقط المهر بقتل السيد أمته لا بقتل الحرة نفسها ولم أر من صرح به هنا للاكتفاء بما ذكروه هناك. وحكم نفقة العدة كحكم المهر قبل الدخول فإن كان هو المرتد فلها نفقة العدة وإن ارتدت فلا نفقة لها قوله : (والإباء نظيره) أي أن إباء أحد الزوجين عن الإسلام بعد إسلام الآخر نظير الارتداد، فإن كان بعد الدخول فلها كل المهر، وإن كان قبله فلها النصف إن كان هو الآبي عن الإسلام، الآبية فلا شيء لها كما لا نفقة لها في العدة.
Book of Nikah, Chapter on Marriage of the Disbeliever. If they embrace Islam together, they do not separate, and they separate if they embrace Islam successively. We have already stated this explicitly in the chapter on marriage of slaves in the commentary on his statement: 'The mahr is waived by the master killing his female slave, not by the free woman killing herself.' I have not seen anyone state it here, relying on what they mentioned there. The ruling on maintenance during the waiting period is like the ruling on mahr before consummation. If he is the apostate, she has maintenance during the waiting period; if she apostatizes, she has no maintenance. His statement: 'And refusal (iba') is analogous to it' means that the refusal of one of the spouses to embrace Islam after the other has embraced Islam is analogous to apostasy. If it is after consummation, she gets the entire mahr; if before, she gets half if he is the one refusing Islam; if she is the one refusing, she gets nothing, just as she has no maintenance during the waiting period.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, explained by the commentator. The ruling addresses the effect on a marriage when non-Muslim spouses embrace Islam. The default ruling is: if both embrace Islam simultaneously, their marriage remains valid (lam tabin). If they embrace Islam one after the other (mut'alāqib), the marriage dissolves (bānat). The commentator notes that this ruling has been explicitly stated earlier in the chapter on marriage of slaves, and he relies on that. He then clarifies that the same ruling applies to maintenance during the 'iddah period: if the husband apostatizes, she is entitled to maintenance; if she apostatizes, she is not. He then explains the analogy of 'iba' (refusal): if one spouse embraces Islam and the other refuses, it is treated like apostasy. The mahr is determined accordingly: if the refusal occurs after consummation, she gets full mahr; if before, she gets half if he is the refusing party; if she is the refusing party, she gets nothing. This is consistent with the rulings on apostasy.

Key Terms
إباء (ibā’): The refusal of one spouse to embrace Islam after the other has done so; treated analogous to apostasy in terms of effects on marriage and mahr.
مهر (mahr): The bridal gift (dower) that becomes obligatory upon the husband by marriage; its amount or half may be affected by apostasy or refusal before/after consummation.
نفقة العدة (nafaqat al-‘iddah): Maintenance during the waiting period; due to the wife from the husband in case of his apostasy, not hers.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a non-Muslim husband and wife both embrace Islam together, what is the status of their marriage?
A) It dissolves immediately.
B) It remains valid.
C) It dissolves only if they embrace Islam in the same sitting.
D) It requires a renewal of the marriage contract.

Key Takeaway
Simultaneous conversion preserves the marriage; sequential conversion dissolves it upon the first conversion, with mahr and nafaqah determined by analogy to apostasy.
